Al-'Attar's Critique and Justification
Al-'Attar criticizes textual corruption and explains his motivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of al-'Attar's critique of textual corruption and his justification for writing his hashya.

Core Idea
Al-'Attar's hashya is a corrective work born from compassion for students.

This passage from al-'Attar's hashya reveals the scholarly context behind his work. He criticizes earlier scholars for textual corruption and excessive objecting, and explains why he produced his own supercommentary. Understanding his motivation helps us appreciate the function of hashya works in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of criticism
Read the Arabic and translation to determine what specific scholarly practices al-'Attar criticizes. He mentions 'tashif' (textual corruption), quoting others without acknowledgement, and excessive objecting.
2
Analyze the evidence
Find the proverb al-'Attar uses: 'Rarely does a frequent speaker escape, or his stumble be overlooked.' Consider why he chooses this proverb to support his critique.
3
Understand the consequences
Al-'Attar describes that due to these practices, the book became difficult for students and in need of correction. Note his use of 'tu'irat' (became difficult) and 'ta'ashsharat' (became arduous).
4
Identify the solution
Al-'Attar states he wrote his hashya 'to aid the students and out of compassion for the learners, avoiding extremes.' Recognize that his goal is pedagogical, not personal attack.
He positions his work as a middle path between negligence and excess.
5
Evaluate the tone
Assess whether al-'Attar's critique is constructive or purely negative. Consider his compassion and his desire to 'collect the jewels of transmitted knowledge.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار التصحيف، هذا معَ نقله كلامَ الغيرِ بدونِ عَزْمٍ، وَوُقوعه بمقتضى الطَّبع البشري في الشهو، وتلاه العلامة ابن سعيد المغربي؛ فشُغِفَ بالاعتراض عليه، وولعَ بتعقُبِهِ في كلِّ ما عوّل عليه، وقد ألجأه ذلك إلى الاعتِسافِ وتجاوز الإنصاف، ووقع في أوهام وأغاليط تُعكِّرِ الأفهام، وقد قيل فيما سبقَ مِنَ الأمثالِ الَّتي تناقلها الرّجالُ : قَلَّ أَنْ سَلِمَ مِكثارٌ ، أو أُقِيلَ له عثار»، وكثيراً ما ينقلُ عبارةً غيرِهِ؛ مُوهِما أَنَّهَا مِمَّا سَنَح ؛ عندما أورى زنادَ فكرِهِ وقَدَح ، وربَّما أطال في بعض المواضع ذيل الكلامِ له
العطّار معَ عدمِ ملائمةِ الحال واقتضاء المقامِ ؛ فتوغَرَت بما ارتكباه للطَّالب المسالك، وتعشرت عليه المدارك، وصار الكتاب بسبب ذلك لغيرهما محتاجاً، ومُفتقراً لِمَن يسلك سبيل العدالة منهاجاً ، فوضعْتُ هذهِ الحاشية؛ إسعافاً للطالبين، وإشفاقاً على المشتغلين، مُتَجنّباً طَرَفَي التَّفريط والإفراط، ناظماً ما التقطته من جواهر من جواهرِ النُّقولِ في
Al-'Attar: The corruption [of the text], this along with his quoting the words of others without resolve, and his falling into error due to human nature. Ibn Sa'id al-Maghribi followed him; he was infatuated with objecting to him, and became addicted to tracking him in everything he relied upon. That drove him to excess and to exceed fairness, and he fell into delusions and errors that disturb understandings. It was said in the maxims transmitted by men: 'Rarely does a frequent speaker escape, or his stumble be overlooked.' And he often quotes the phrase of someone else, making it seem as if it is from his own inspiration; when he kindles the spark of his thought and strikes, and perhaps he prolongs the tail of speech in some places.
Al-'Attar: With the unsuitability of the situation and the requirement of the context, the paths became difficult for the student due to what they both committed, and the avenues became arduous, and the book thereby became in need of others, and dependent on one who follows the path of justice. So I wrote this hashya, to aid the students and out of compassion for the learners, avoiding the extremes of negligence and excess, arranging what I have gleaned from the jewels of transmitted knowledge in
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a critical note by the commentator al-'Attar, found as marginalia in the hashya. In the first part, he criticizes textual corruption and the author's habit of quoting others without acknowledgement, which leads to errors. He mentions Ibn Sa'id al-Maghribi, who was overly fond of objecting to everything, and this caused him to exceed fairness and fall into delusions. Al-'Attar cites a proverb: 'Rarely does a frequent speaker escape, or his stumble be overlooked.' He continues that the author often presents others' phrases as his own. In the second part, al-'Attar explains that because the book has become difficult for students due to these issues, he wrote his own hashya (supercommentary) to aid them, avoiding extremes, and compiling selections from precious transmitted knowledge.

Key Terms
الحاشية
التصحيف

A) To gain fame
B) To aid students and correct the book's difficulties
C) To attack Ibn Sa'id al-Maghribi
D) To summarize the original text

Key Takeaway
Al-'Attar wrote his hashya to correct textual errors and make the book accessible for students, thereby fulfilling a critical scholarly role.

Why 'Inna' is Used for Emphasis Even Without Denial
Author explains why 'inna' is used in 'inna ahaqqa' despite the audience not denying or doubting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the objection that 'inna' is only used for denying or doubting audiences, using the three reasons provided in the commentary.

Core Idea
Emphasis with 'inna' can highlight the greatness of a truth, even if universally accepted.

In Arabic, the particle 'inna' is typically used for emphasis. A potential objection arises: since emphasis is only needed when someone denies or doubts, and the audience here is not denying or doubting, why is 'inna' used in 'inna ahaqqa'? The commentator addresses this objection by providing alternative reasons for using 'inna', showing that its use is not restricted to denial or doubt. This lesson breaks down the dialectical method of raising and refuting objections, a crucial skill in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being raised
Find the objection in the Arabic: it claims that 'inna' is exclusively for emphasis, and emphasis is only needed when the audience denies or doubts. Since there is no denial or doubt here, 'inna' should not be used.
The objection is implicitly present and explicitly refuted.
2
List the three reasons given for using 'inna' despite no denial
The commentator gives three reasons: (1) treating the audience as if they were deniers for the sake of emphasis, (2) to beautify the wording, and (3) to indicate the greatness of the news—that praising Allah is the best speech.
The third reason is the most significant for the refutation.
3
Explain how the third reason refutes the exclusivity claim
The objection relies on the assumption that 'inna' is only for denial/doubt. The commentator denies this exclusivity (man' al-hasr) by showing that 'inna' can also be used to indicate the greatness of a well-known fact, thereby refuting the objection.
This is a common dialectical move: denying the claimed exclusivity of a condition.
4
Note the dialectical method of raising and refuting objections
This passage exemplifies the adab al-bahth (disputation) tradition: an objection is raised, then systematically refuted. The commentator also notes that many non-Arab scholars use this construction, showing its acceptance.
Understanding this method helps in analyzing similar arguments in other Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنَّ أَحقَّ ... إلخ) أكَّد، وإنْ كانَ المخاطب ليسَ مُنكِراً وَلَا شَاكًا، إِمَّا تنزيلاً له منزلة المنكِرِ ، وإمَّا لتزيين اللفظ ، وإمَّا لِلدَّلالة على عِظَم الخبر، وهوَ كونُ حمدِ اللهِ أحسن الكلام الذي ينطقُ بِهِ اللّسانُ. وَبِهَذا اندفعَ مَا يُقالُ : إِنَّ (إِنَّ) لا تكونُ إِلَّا لِلتَّأكيد، وهو لا يكونُ إِلَّا لِلمُنكِرِ أو لِلشَّاكٌ، وَلَا مُنكِرَ هُنَا وَلَا شَاكَ، وحاصلُ الدَّفع منع الحصرِ; إذْ قد يؤتى بها لِلدَّلالة على عِظَم الخبرِ وإِنْ كانَ مُشتهراً . (قَوْلُهُ : أَحَقِّ أَيْ : أَوْلَى وأشرف، فهو أفعلُ تفضيل بحسب الأصل، وقد العطار عنه إلى معنى الأوجبِ كَـ: زيدٌ أحقُّ بِمَالِهِ»، وَهِيَ هنا أفعلُ تفضيل . (قَوْلُهُ : إِنَّ أَحَقَّ ... إلخ) سلك هذا الطَّريقَ في تأدية الحمدِ كثير من الأعاجم ; كالشَّارحِ هنا، وكالقطب في شَرحِ الشَّمسيَّة، وغيرهما; ميلاً إلى جهة الاستغراب،
(His statement: 'Indeed the most worthy ... etc.') He emphasized, even though the addressee is not a denier nor a doubter, either by treating him as if he were a denier, or to beautify the wording, or to indicate the greatness of the news, which is that praising Allah is the best speech that the tongue utters. And with this is refuted what is said: 'Indeed (inna) is only for emphasis, and emphasis is only for the denier or the doubter, and there is no denier here nor a doubter.' The essence of the refutation is the denial of the exclusivity; because it may be brought to indicate the greatness of the news even if it is well-known. (His statement: 'ahaqq' meaning: more worthy and nobler, so it is a comparative in origin, and al-Attar shifted it to the meaning of more obligatory, like 'Zayd is more worthy of his wealth,' and here it is a comparative. (His statement: 'Indeed the most worthy ... etc.') Many non-Arabs followed this way in expressing praise, like the commentator here, and al-Qutb in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya, and others, inclining towards the mode of amazement.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'إن أحق' from the original text. It addresses a potential objection: why use the emphatic particle 'إن' (inna) when the audience does not deny or doubt the statement? The commentator provides three reasons: 1) treating the audience as if they were deniers for the sake of emphasis, 2) to beautify the wording, or 3) to indicate the greatness of the news—that praising Allah is the best speech. The refutation of the objection is that the claimed exclusivity of 'إن' for denial/doubt is false; it can also be used to highlight the importance of a well-known fact. The commentator then explains 'أحق' as a comparative meaning 'more worthy' or 'nobler,' with a note from al-Attar that it can also carry the sense of 'more obligatory'. Finally, the commentator notes that many non-Arab scholars, including himself (the author of this sharh) and al-Qutb in his Sharh al-Shamsiyya, use this construction to express praise in a surprising or amazing manner. This shows a dialectical method: raising an objection and providing a clear refutation.

أحق
تنزيلاً
تزيين اللفظ
عظم الخبر
منع الحصر

Checkpoint

A) To indicate that the speaker is a non-Arab.
B) To indicate the greatness of the news.
C) To make the sentence grammatically correct.
D) To show that the addressee is actually in denial.

Key Takeaway
The use of 'inna' in Arabic rhetoric is not limited to denial or doubt; it can also emphasize the greatness of a well-known truth, as demonstrated in the praise of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using the method of denying exclusivity (man' al-hasr), write a brief refutation by providing alternative valid reasons for the rule's application.

Distinguishing Hamd from Shukr
Commentary distinguishes hamd from shukr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hamd and shukr based on the specificity of their objects.

Core Idea
Praise (hamd) is general; gratitude (shukr) is for blessings only.

The commentary begins by noting that the author inserted a parenthetical remark not indicating a blessing, which alludes to a key difference between hamd and shukr. Understanding this distinction is essential for interpreting Islamic texts accurately, especially in contexts of praise and gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
Find 'الحمد' and 'الشكر' in the Arabic text. Notice that the commentator says the sentence does not indicate a blessing, which is relevant to shukr.
The terms are contrasted by the commentator.
2
Determine the criterion for distinction
The criterion is the object (متعلق): hamd has a general object, while shukr is specific to blessings.
This is stated explicitly by al-Dasuqi.
3
Apply the distinction to the original sentence
Since the sentence does not mention a blessing, it is appropriate for hamd but not for shukr. This confirms the presence of hamd in the opening.
The author's choice of hamd is deliberate and meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الَّذي شرَّفَ نوع الإنسان بحلْيَةِ الإدراك، وزينَةِ الإفهام . الدسوقي وَلَمَّا كانَ الحمدُ لَا يُشترط فيه أن يكون في مقابلة نعمة؛ أتى في جانبه بجملة معترضةٍ لا تدلُّ على النّعمةِ، فَفِى كلامِهِ إشارةٌ للفَرْقِ بين الحمدِ والشَّكْرِ مِن جهة المتعلّق، فَمتعلّق الحمدِ عام ، ومُتعلّق الشكر خاص بالنعمة. (قَوْلُهُ : الَّذِي) صفه الله (قَوْلُهُ : نَوْعَ الْإِنْسَانِ) الإضافةُ للبيان . (قَوْلُهُ : بِحِلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ) يجوزُ أنْ يُراد بحليةِ التّحلّي؛ أي: فالمعنى بالتحلّي بالإدراك، وحينئذٍ فَالإدراكُ شُبّه بالحلي، وحليه تخييل، ويجوز أن يُرادَ : المتحلَّى به، وحينئذٍ فالمعنى بالإدراكِ الشَّبيه بالحلية؛ أي: بما يتحلى به، فيكون تشبيهاً بليغاً، ولا تصح الاستعارة حينئذ ؛ للجمع بين الطرفين، والإدراك : العلوم والمعارِفُ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَزِيْنَةِ) يجوز أن يُراد بالزِّينةِ التَّزيُّنُ أو المتزيَّنُ به، ويُقالُ فيه ما قيل فيما قبله . — (قَوْلُهُ : الْإِفْهَامِ) - بفتح الهمزة - جمعُ فَهُم ، وَهوَ الإدراك، ويحتمل أَنْ يُقالَ: الإفهام بالكسرِ ؛ أي : للغير، فهو مغاير للإدراك، وهو أَوْلى. العطار وقد احتوى الكلامُ على عدَّةِ أنواع من البديعِ ؛ غير خفيَّةٍ عليكَ إِنْ كنتَ مِمَّنْ نظر في علم البديع . (قَوْلُهُ : بِحلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ) الباء داخلةٌ على المقصور، لا على المقصور عليهِ كَمَا وهِمَ، والحليةُ تُطلقُ بمعنى المصدر، وبمعنى المتحلَّى به، وكذلكَ الزِّينه، والإدراكُ : الفهم؛ يُستَعمل مصدراً، وبمعنى اسم المفعول، والأفهام يُقرأ بكسر الهمزة مصدراً، وبفتحها جمعاً لفهم وإرادتهما على حد سواء، وفي حلية الإدراك وزينة الأفهام تشبية بليغ، أو مكنيَّةٌ في الإدراك والأفهام، وتخييلية في حلية وزينة؛ هذا على أنَّ كُلا منهما مصدر، فإن كانا بمعنى اسم المفعول؛ فلا استعارة للزومِ الجمع بين الطرفين.
The one who honored the human species with the adornment of perception and the ornament of understandings. Al-Dasuqi: Since praise (hamd) does not require being in return for a blessing, he inserted in its side an intervening sentence that does not indicate blessing, so in his speech is an indication of the difference between praise and gratitude (shukr) from the aspect of the object: the object of praise is general, and the object of gratitude is specific to blessing. (His saying: 'who') – attribute of Allah. (His saying: 'the human species') – the possessive is for clarification. (His saying: 'with the adornment of perception') – it is permissible that 'adornment' be intended as adorning (al-tahalli), i.e., the meaning is with adorning with perception. Then perception is likened to jewelry, and its adornment is imaginary. And it is permissible that it be intended as that by which one adorns (al-mutahalla bihi). Then the meaning is with perception similar to jewelry, i.e., that by which one adorns, so it is an eloquent simile, and metaphor is not correct then due to combining both sides. And perception is knowledge and cognitions. (His saying: 'and the ornament') – it is permissible that 'ornament' be intended as adorning or that by which one adorns, and what was said before applies. (His saying: 'understandings' (al-ifham)) – with fathah on the hamza, plural of fahm, which is perception. And it is possible to say 'al-ifham' with kasrah, i.e., for others, so it differs from perception, and that is more appropriate. Al-'Attar: And the speech contains several types of badi' (rhetorical devices), not hidden from you if you have studied the science of badi'. (His saying: 'with the adornment of perception') – the ba' enters upon the attributed thing (al-maqsour), not upon the thing to which it is attributed (al-maqsour 'alayhi), as was mistakenly thought. And 'adornment' (hilyah) is used in the sense of the verbal noun (masdar) and in the sense of that by which one adorns. Similarly 'ornament' (zainah). Perception is understanding (fahm), used as a verbal noun and in the sense of object noun. And 'afham' is read with kasrah on the hamza as a verbal noun, and with fathah as a plural of fahm, and both are intended equally. In 'adornment of perception' and 'ornament of understandings' there is an eloquent simile, or a metonymical (makniyyah) [metaphor] in perception and understandings, and imagery (takhayyuliyyah) in 'adornment' and 'ornament' – this is on the basis that each is a verbal noun. If they are in the sense of object noun, then there is no metaphor because of combining both sides.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the opening line of the matn, which praises Allah for honoring humanity with perception and understanding. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by clarifying a subtle point: the statement contains a parenthetical remark that does not directly mention a blessing, and this alludes to the difference between hamd (praise) and shukr (gratitude). The author notes that hamd does not require being in response to a favor; it can be general. Shukr, however, is specifically for blessings. This distinction is important in Islamic thought (e.g., praising Allah for His essence vs. thanking Him for a specific favor).

Al-Dasuqi then proceeds to parse the phrase word by word, starting with 'الَّذي' (who), an attribute of Allah. 'نَوْعَ الْإِنْسَانِ' (the human species) has a possessive that is explanatory.

Next, he analyzes 'بِحِلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ' (with the adornment of perception). He presents two possible interpretations: (1) 'adornment' as the act of adorning (al-tahalli), i.e., the meaning is 'with the act of adorning with perception,' where perception is likened to jewelry and the adornment is a figurative image. (2) 'Adornment' as the thing used to adorn (al-mutahalla bihi), making 'perception similar to jewelry,' forming an eloquent simile (tashbih baligh). He notes that in the second case, a metaphor (isti'ara) is not possible because both terms are explicitly mentioned.

Similarly, 'زِيْنَةِ' (ornament) follows the same two possibilities.

Then he discusses 'الْإِفْهَامِ' (understandings). With fatha on the hamza, it is the plural of fahm (comprehension). With kasra, it is a verbal noun meaning 'making understand' (for others), which is distinct from 'idrak' (personal perception). He favors the latter as more appropriate.

Al-'Attar adds a note on rhetorical devices (badi'): the sentence contains multiple types. He corrects a misconception about the preposition 'ba' in 'بِحلْيَةِ': it attaches to the thing being adorned (al-maqsour), not to the thing by which it is adorned (al-maqsour 'alayhi). He reiterates the two meanings of hilyah and zina. For 'ifham,' he notes the two readings correspond to different meanings. He concludes by analyzing the rhetorical figure: if hilyah and zina are verbal nouns, then there is either an eloquent simile or a metonymical metaphor in idrak and afham with imagery in hilyah and zina. If they are object nouns, there is no metaphor.

Thus, the commentary focuses on precise lexical and rhetorical analysis, essential for understanding the author's intent.

Key Terms
الحمد
الشكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dasuqi, what is the difference between hamd and shukr as indicated in the opening sentence?
A) Hamd is for blessings, shukr is for non-blessings.
B) Hamd is general in object, shukr is specific to blessings.
C) Hamd is only for Allah, shukr is for people.
D) Hamd and shukr are the same.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that hamd is general praise, while shukr is specifically for blessings.

?

Rhetorical Analysis of Hilyah and Zina
Commentary explains the rhetorical use of حلية and زينة

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two possible meanings of hilyah and zina (masdar vs. ism maf'ul) and determine whether the phrase is a metaphor or a simile.

Core Idea
Hilyah and zina can mean either the act of adorning or the thing that adorns, changing the rhetorical figure.

The commentary dives into the phrase 'with the adornment of perception and the ornament of understandings.' The commentators explain that these words can be interpreted as verbal nouns or as object nouns, each leading to a different rhetorical figure: either a metaphor with imagery or an eloquent simile.

Guided Path
1
Locate the phrase 'بِحِلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ'
Find the phrase in the Arabic text. Note that 'hilyah' is the key word being analyzed.
The same analysis applies to 'زِيْنَةِ الْإِفْهَامِ'.
2
Understand the two meanings of hilyah
Hilyah can be a masdar (verbal noun) meaning 'the act of adorning' or an ism maf'ul (object noun) meaning 'the thing used to adorn'.
This is explicitly stated by al-Dasuqi.
3
Determine the resulting rhetorical figure for each meaning
If masdar, then idrak is likened to jewelry, and hilyah is a figurative image (takhayyuliyya), forming a metaphor. If ism maf'ul, then idrak itself is compared to jewelry, forming an eloquent simile (tashbih baligh).
Al-Dasuqi notes that metaphor is not possible in the second interpretation because both sides are mentioned.
4
Apply the same to 'زِيْنَةِ الْإِفْهَامِ' and note al-'Attar's correction
The same two possibilities apply to zina and afham. Al-'Attar clarifies that the preposition ba' enters upon the thing attributed (al-maqsour), and that ifham can be read with kasra (masdar) or fatha (plural).
Al-'Attar confirms the two readings and their implications.

Source Text

الَّذي شرَّفَ نوع الإنسان بحلْيَةِ الإدراك، وزينَةِ الإفهام . الدسوقي وَلَمَّا كانَ الحمدُ لَا يُشترط فيه أن يكون في مقابلة نعمة؛ أتى في جانبه بجملة معترضةٍ لا تدلُّ على النّعمةِ، فَفِى كلامِهِ إشارةٌ للفَرْقِ بين الحمدِ والشَّكْرِ مِن جهة المتعلّق، فَمتعلّق الحمدِ عام ، ومُتعلّق الشكر خاص بالنعمة. (قَوْلُهُ : الَّذِي) صفه الله (قَوْلُهُ : نَوْعَ الْإِنْسَانِ) الإضافةُ للبيان . (قَوْلُهُ : بِحِلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ) يجوزُ أنْ يُراد بحليةِ التّحلّي؛ أي: فالمعنى بالتحلّي بالإدراك، وحينئذٍ فَالإدراكُ شُبّه بالحلي، وحليه تخييل، ويجوز أن يُرادَ : المتحلَّى به، وحينئذٍ فالمعنى بالإدراكِ الشَّبيه بالحلية؛ أي: بما يتحلى به، فيكون تشبيهاً بليغاً، ولا تصح الاستعارة حينئذ ؛ للجمع بين الطرفين، والإدراك : العلوم والمعارِفُ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَزِيْنَةِ) يجوز أن يُراد بالزِّينةِ التَّزيُّنُ أو المتزيَّنُ به، ويُقالُ فيه ما قيل فيما قبله . — (قَوْلُهُ : الْإِفْهَامِ) - بفتح الهمزة - جمعُ فَهُم ، وَهوَ الإدراك، ويحتمل أَنْ يُقالَ: الإفهام بالكسرِ ؛ أي : للغير، فهو مغاير للإدراك، وهو أَوْلى. العطار وقد احتوى الكلامُ على عدَّةِ أنواع من البديعِ ؛ غير خفيَّةٍ عليكَ إِنْ كنتَ مِمَّنْ نظر في علم البديع . (قَوْلُهُ : بِحلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ) الباء داخلةٌ على المقصور، لا على المقصور عليهِ كَمَا وهِمَ، والحليةُ تُطلقُ بمعنى المصدر، وبمعنى المتحلَّى به، وكذلكَ الزِّينه، والإدراكُ : الفهم؛ يُستَعمل مصدراً، وبمعنى اسم المفعول، والأفهام يُقرأ بكسر الهمزة مصدراً، وبفتحها جمعاً لفهم وإرادتهما على حد سواء، وفي حلية الإدراك وزينة الأفهام تشبية بليغ، أو مكنيَّةٌ في الإدراك والأفهام، وتخييلية في حلية وزينة؛ هذا على أنَّ كُلا منهما مصدر، فإن كانا بمعنى اسم المفعول؛ فلا استعارة للزومِ الجمع بين الطرفين.
The one who honored the human species with the adornment of perception and the ornament of understandings. Al-Dasuqi: Since praise (hamd) does not require being in return for a blessing, he inserted in its side an intervening sentence that does not indicate blessing, so in his speech is an indication of the difference between praise and gratitude (shukr) from the aspect of the object: the object of praise is general, and the object of gratitude is specific to blessing. (His saying: 'who') – attribute of Allah. (His saying: 'the human species') – the possessive is for clarification. (His saying: 'with the adornment of perception') – it is permissible that 'adornment' be intended as adorning (al-tahalli), i.e., the meaning is with adorning with perception. Then perception is likened to jewelry, and its adornment is imaginary. And it is permissible that it be intended as that by which one adorns (al-mutahalla bihi). Then the meaning is with perception similar to jewelry, i.e., that by which one adorns, so it is an eloquent simile, and metaphor is not correct then due to combining both sides. And perception is knowledge and cognitions. (His saying: 'and the ornament') – it is permissible that 'ornament' be intended as adorning or that by which one adorns, and what was said before applies. (His saying: 'understandings' (al-ifham)) – with fathah on the hamza, plural of fahm, which is perception. And it is possible to say 'al-ifham' with kasrah, i.e., for others, so it differs from perception, and that is more appropriate. Al-'Attar: And the speech contains several types of badi' (rhetorical devices), not hidden from you if you have studied the science of badi'. (His saying: 'with the adornment of perception') – the ba' enters upon the attributed thing (al-maqsour), not upon the thing to which it is attributed (al-maqsour 'alayhi), as was mistakenly thought. And 'adornment' (hilyah) is used in the sense of the verbal noun (masdar) and in the sense of that by which one adorns. Similarly 'ornament' (zainah). Perception is understanding (fahm), used as a verbal noun and in the sense of object noun. And 'afham' is read with kasrah on the hamza as a verbal noun, and with fathah as a plural of fahm, and both are intended equally. In 'adornment of perception' and 'ornament of understandings' there is an eloquent simile, or a metonymical (makniyyah) [metaphor] in perception and understandings, and imagery (takhayyuliyyah) in 'adornment' and 'ornament' – this is on the basis that each is a verbal noun. If they are in the sense of object noun, then there is no metaphor because of combining both sides.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the opening line of the matn, which praises Allah for honoring humanity with perception and understanding. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, begins by clarifying a subtle point: the statement contains a parenthetical remark that does not directly mention a blessing, and this alludes to the difference between hamd (praise) and shukr (gratitude). The author notes that hamd does not require being in response to a favor; it can be general. Shukr, however, is specifically for blessings. This distinction is important in Islamic thought (e.g., praising Allah for His essence vs. thanking Him for a specific favor).

Al-Dasuqi then proceeds to parse the phrase word by word, starting with 'الَّذي' (who), an attribute of Allah. 'نَوْعَ الْإِنْسَانِ' (the human species) has a possessive that is explanatory.

Next, he analyzes 'بِحِلْيَةِ الْإِدْرَاكِ' (with the adornment of perception). He presents two possible interpretations: (1) 'adornment' as the act of adorning (al-tahalli), i.e., the meaning is 'with the act of adorning with perception,' where perception is likened to jewelry and the adornment is a figurative image. (2) 'Adornment' as the thing used to adorn (al-mutahalla bihi), making 'perception similar to jewelry,' forming an eloquent simile (tashbih baligh). He notes that in the second case, a metaphor (isti'ara) is not possible because both terms are explicitly mentioned.

Similarly, 'زِيْنَةِ' (ornament) follows the same two possibilities.

Then he discusses 'الْإِفْهَامِ' (understandings). With fatha on the hamza, it is the plural of fahm (comprehension). With kasra, it is a verbal noun meaning 'making understand' (for others), which is distinct from 'idrak' (personal perception). He favors the latter as more appropriate.

Al-'Attar adds a note on rhetorical devices (badi'): the sentence contains multiple types. He corrects a misconception about the preposition 'ba' in 'بِحلْيَةِ': it attaches to the thing being adorned (al-maqsour), not to the thing by which it is adorned (al-maqsour 'alayhi). He reiterates the two meanings of hilyah and zina. For 'ifham,' he notes the two readings correspond to different meanings. He concludes by analyzing the rhetorical figure: if hilyah and zina are verbal nouns, then there is either an eloquent simile or a metonymical metaphor in idrak and afham with imagery in hilyah and zina. If they are object nouns, there is no metaphor.

Thus, the commentary focuses on precise lexical and rhetorical analysis, essential for understanding the author's intent.

الإدراك
الزينة
الإفهام

Human Specification with Precious Speech
Commentary on Allah's specification of humans with speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of 'idraj' in the context of Allah's specification of humans and identify the condition (shart al-intizam) for this ability.

Core Idea
Humans alone are gifted to embed precise meanings in refined words – a divine favor.

This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary unpacks a dense phrase from the matn: 'وخَصَّصَه بإدراج دُرَرِ المعاني في جواهر الألفاظ'. It clarifies that this ability is unique to humans, not angels or jinn, and requires a harmony between word and meaning. Understanding this helps us appreciate the Islamic view of human linguistic superiority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the words 'إدراج', 'دُرَر', 'جواهر', and 'انتظام' in the Arabic. Note their literal meanings and how the commentator redefines them for this context.
2
Understand the specification
The commentator says that 'وخَصَّصَه' means Allah made the capacity to gather precise meanings in few words exclusive to humans. This does not mean every human can do it; it is a species-specific gift.
Be careful: specification ≠ universal individual capability.
3
Analyse the condition of order
The phrase 'على شرط الانتظام' is a hal describing the insertion. 'انتظام' means suitability and straightness – words must match meanings in nobility. The commentator prefers 'انتظام' over 'نظام' because it emphasizes correspondence.
4
Examine the idafa constructions
The idafas 'دُرَرِ المعاني' and 'جواهر الألفاظ' are idafat tashbih (genitive of comparison). The first compares precise meanings to pearls; the second compares words to jewels in beauty.
Al-'Attar suggests 'أصداف الألفاظ' would be more consistent with the 'molds' metaphor, but 'جواهر' emphasizes preciousness.
5
Note the transition to prayer
The commentator explains that 'ثمَّ الصَّلاةُ' is conjoined to an implied 'الحمد لله', and 'ثمَّ' indicates that prayer (salat upon the Prophet) is secondary in rank to praise.
This shows the textual structure: praise first, then prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الشَّارِحِ وخصَّصَه بإدراج دُرَرِ المعاني في جواهر الألفاظ على شرط الانتظام، ثمَّ الصَّلاةُ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَخَصَّصَهُ) أي : نوع الإنسان بإدراج ؛ أي : طي، والمرادُ بِهِ هنا : الجمع ؛ أي: جمعُ المعاني في ألفاظ قليلةٍ، وهو عطفٌ على شرَّفٌ، والباء داخلةٌ على المقصور ؛ أي: وجعل إدراج المعاني الدَّقيقةِ في الألفاظ النفيسة؛ أي: جمعها فيها مقصوراً على الإنسان لا يتعدَّاهُ لِغيرِهِ مِنَ الملائكة والجنّ، وجعل الإدراج المذكور قاصراً على الإنسانِ لا يقتضي قدرة كلِّ فردٍ من أفرادِهِ عليه، ونازع بعضُهم في الجنّ فقال : إنَّهم كَالإنس في ذلك وانظره، وما ذكره هو الحقُّ من أنَّ الألفاظ قوالب للمعاني ؛ أي : إنَّ الألفاظ تلاحظ أوّلاً لأجل أن يستحضر بها المعاني. (قَوْلُهُ : دُرَرٍ) جمع درّة، وَهِيَ اللُّؤلؤة الكبيرةُ، مُستعارُ لِلدَّقيقِ مِنَ (المعاني) وقوله : (في جواهر (في جواهر الألفاظ مُتعلّق بإدراج، وإضافة (جواهر) للألفاظ مِن إضافة المشبِّهِ بِهِ لِلمشبَّه ، أي : في الألفاظ الشَّبيهة بالجواهر في الحُسْنِ، والجواهرُ جمعُ جوهرة: وهي الأحجارُ النَّفيسة، و(قَوْلُهُ : على شرط الانتظام) حال مِنَ الإدراج، وَلَوْ قال : على شرط النظامِ؛ لَكَانَ أظهر، كذا قِيْلَ وفيهِ : أَنَّ مَا قالَهُ الشَّارحُ أظهرُ ، وذلكَ أنَّ الانتظام معناه: المناسبة والاستقامة، وهي مُرادةٌ هنا، وذلك أن تكون الألفاظ موافقة للمعاني في الشَّرفِ؛ احترازاً عن أن تكونَ الألفاظ خسيسةً، كما إذا كانَتْ مُجنسة، والمعنى مبتذل، أو بالعكس، وإضافة شرط لِمَا بعدَهُ بيانية . (قَوْلُهُ : ثمَّ الصَّلَاةُ . . . إِلَخ عطفٌ على مُتوهّم ؛ أي: الحمدُ لله، ثم ... إلخ، العطار (قَوْلُهُ : وخَصَّصَهُ بِإِدْرَاج الباء داخلةٌ على المقصور أيضاً، والإدراج: الإدخال، وإضافةُ دُرَرٍ للمعاني وجواهر للألفاظ من قبيل لُجين الماء، والمناسب لقولهم : الألفاظ قوالب المعاني ؛ أن يقول في صدف الألفاظ، وكأنَّهُ اختارَ التَّعبير بجواهر؛ للإشارة إلى نفاسةِ تلكَ الألفاظ أيضاً . The commentator's introduction: And He specified him by inserting pearls of meanings into jewels of words on the condition of order, then the prayer. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'and He specified him') – i.e., the human species by insertion (idraj); i.e., folding, and what is meant here is gathering; i.e., gathering meanings in few words. And it is a conjunction to 'honored', and the ba' enters upon the attributed thing (al-maqsour). i.e., He made the insertion of precise meanings into precious words – i.e., collecting them therein – confined to humans, not extending to other than them from angels and jinn. And making the mentioned insertion restricted to humans does not necessitate the ability of every individual among them to do so. And some disputed concerning the jinn, saying: They are like humans in that. And what he (the author) mentioned is the truth, that words are molds for meanings; i.e., words are observed first in order to bring to mind the meanings. (His saying: 'pearls' (durarin)) – plural of durrah, which is a large pearl, used metaphorically for the precise (of meanings). And his saying: 'in jewels (of words)' is connected to 'insertion', and the idafa of 'jewels' to 'words' is the idafa of the thing compared to the thing compared to, i.e., words similar to jewels in beauty. And 'jewels' (jawahir) is plural of jawharah, which are precious stones. And (his saying: 'on the condition of order' (shart al-intizam)) is a state (hal) from 'insertion'. And if he had said 'on the condition of system (nidham)', it would have been more apparent, as was said. But there is in that: what the commentator said is more apparent, because 'intizam' means suitability and straightness, which is intended here – that words should correspond to meanings in nobility, as a precaution against words being base, as when they are vulgar (mujannisah) and the meaning is trivial, or vice versa. And the addition of 'shart' to what follows is explanatory (bayaniyyah). (His saying: 'then the prayer...etc.) is a conjunction to an implied [word]; i.e., 'Praise be to Allah, then...' Al-'Attar: (His saying: 'and He specified him by insertion') – the ba' also enters upon the attributed thing. And 'idraj' means insertion (idkhal). The idafa of 'pearls' to meanings and 'jewels' to words is of the type of 'silver of water' (lujayn al-ma'). And it would be more appropriate for their saying 'words are molds for meanings' that he say 'in the shells of words', but it seems he chose the expression 'jewels' to indicate the preciousness of those words as well. (His saying: 'then the prayer') – the conjunction with 'thumma' indicates the delay of the rank of prayer from
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the next part of the matn, which speaks of Allah specifying humanity with the ability to insert pearls of meanings into jewels of words, on condition of order, followed by the prayer (salat) upon the Prophet. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, first explains that 'وخَصَّصَه' (and He specified him) refers to the human species being singled out for the act of 'إدراج' (insertion/embedding). 'إدراج' here means 'جمع' (gathering) – gathering many meanings into few words. This is conjoined to the earlier verb 'شرَّف' (honored). The preposition 'ba' in 'بإدراج' attaches to the thing being specified (al-maqsour).

Al-Dasuqi clarifies that this specification means that the ability to embed precise meanings in precious words is exclusive to humans, not shared by angels or jinn. However, this does not imply that every individual human possesses this ability; it is a species-specific gift. He notes a debate about jinn – some argue they share this capability, but the author's position (that it is exclusive to humans) is correct. He then explains the phrase 'الألفاظ قوالب للمعاني' (words are molds for meanings), meaning that words are first considered as containers to bring meanings to mind.

Next, he analyses 'دُرَرِ المعاني' (pearls of meanings): 'durrah' is a large pearl, used metaphorically for precise meanings. 'في جواهر الألفاظ' (in jewels of the words) – 'jawahir' are precious stones; the genitive construction is a 'idafat tashbih' (genitive of comparison), meaning words are like jewels in beauty. The phrase 'على شرط الانتظام' (on the condition of order) is a hal describing the state of the insertion. He notes that 'intizam' (order) means suitability and straightness, i.e., words must match meanings in nobility to avoid baseness. He prefers 'intizam' over 'nidham' because it emphasizes the correspondence.

Then he explains that 'ثمَّ الصَّلاةُ' (then the prayer) is conjoined to an understood clause 'الحمد لله' (Praise be to Allah), indicating that after praising Allah, the salat upon the Prophet follows.

Al-'Attar adds that the idafa of 'durrar' to 'ma'ani' and 'jawahir' to 'alfaz' is like the figurative expression 'lujayn al-ma' (silver of water). He suggests that if the metaphor 'words are molds' were strictly applied, it would have been more appropriate to say 'in shells of words', but the author chose 'jewels' to emphasize the preciousness of the words. He also notes that the conjunction 'thumma' in 'ثم الصلاة' indicates the lower rank of prayer compared to praise.

Thus, the commentary delves into lexical, rhetorical, and theological nuances of the matn's claim about human linguistic superiority.

الدرر
الجواهر

B) Humans are uniquely capable of this, but not every individual.

Key Takeaway

Grammatical Precision: The Case of 'Kāffa' and Linguistic Authority
Critique of al-Zamakhshari's usage of 'kāffa' and the source of Arabic purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct grammatical usage of 'kāffa' and explain why al-Zamakhshari's alternative is considered erroneous by the commentators, and assess the criteria for linguistic authority in Arabic.

Core Idea
Linguistic precision in Qur'anic grammar is non-negotiable; errors in Arabic affect the validity of legal rulings and theological arguments.

This passage from a hashīya on logic demonstrates how even a small grammatical detail like the case of the word 'kāffa' can be the subject of rigorous critique. The commentators defend the strict standard that 'kāffa' must be an accusative circumstantial (ḥāl), and they challenge al-Zamakhshari's authority as a non-Arab. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate linguistic arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical issue with 'kāffa'
Notice that the passage begins by stating that 'kāffa' should only be used in the accusative case as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl), and that using it in the genitive (as in the phrase 'ilā kāffati l-anām') is incorrect. This is the key grammatical rule being defended.
The accusative case is marked by the fatḥa, while the genitive would be kasra.
2
Extract al-Zamakhshari's two errors from the Qur'anic examples
From verse 2:208, al-Zamakhshari allowed 'kāffa' to be a circumstantial accusative from either the subject or object. The author says this is wrong because 'kāffa' is specific to rational beings. From verse 34:28, al-Zamakhshari interpreted 'kāffa' as an adjective for an implied noun ('risāla'), which removes it from its established circumstantial usage. This is considered worse.
The author emphasizes that these errors arise from tampering with the fixed grammatical function of the word.
3
Understand the commentator's rejection of al-Zamakhshari's authority
The commentator (al-Muḥashshī) argues that al-Zamakhshari, being non-Arab, cannot be an authority because Arabic was divinely preserved only among Arabs who did not mix with city-dwellers. This is a strong statement about the source of linguistic reliability.
This principle is not about ethnicity but about purity of language through isolation from foreign influence.
4
Grasp the nuance of 'urban Arab' (ḥaḍarī)
A further clarification is given: 'ḥaḍarī' does not mean any city-dweller, but specifically an Arab who mixed with non-Arabs and grew up among them, thus losing the pure Arabic. This is why people of Mecca and Medina remain authorities, as they did not mix extensively after early Islam.
The story of Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali illustrates how mixing led to solecism, necessitating grammar.
5
Reflect on the story of Abu al-Aswad as evidence
The passage ends by referencing the well-known story that prompted Ali to commission the writing of grammar rules after hearing a mistake. This serves as historical proof that linguistic change resulted from mixing with non-Arabs, reinforcing the need for strict standards.
This is a foundational narrative in the history of Arabic grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نوح (قَوْلُهُ: إِلَى كَافَّةِ الْأَنَامِ) فيه استعمال كافة مجرورة، ولا تُستعمل إلا منصوبةٌ على الحال كما في المغني؛ قالَ : وتَجويزُ الزَّمخشري للوجهين؛ أي: الحال مِنَ الفاعل والمفعول في قولِهِ تعالى: ﴿وَادْخُلُوا فِي السّامِ كَافَةً﴾ [البقرة: ٢٠٨]؛ وَهم ؛ لأنَّ كافَّةٌ تختصُّ بِمَن يعقلُ، وَوهمُهُ في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَاكَ إِلَّا كَافَةً لِلنَّاسِ [سبا: ۲۸] إذ قدَّرَ كافة نعتاً لمصدر محذوف ; أي: رسالةٌ كَافَّةٌ أَشدَّ; لأَنَّهُ أضاف إلى استعماله فيما لا يعقل إخراجُهُ عمَّا التزم فيه من الحالية، ووهمه في خطبة المفصَّل أشدُّ وأشدُّ; لإخراجهِ إِيَّاهُ عن النَّصبِ ألبنَّة ا.هـ. قال المحشّي : ودَعوى أنَّ الزَّمخشري مِمَّنْ يُحتَجُ بتراكيبِهِ لا تُسمَعُ ; لأنَّ تلكَ مرتبةٌ لا ينالها العربي الحضري، فكيفَ ينالُها العجمي؟ وذلك لأنَّ اللهَ تعالى خَصَّ العربَ الَّذِينَ لم يخالطوا الحَضَرَ بعصمة ألسنتهم عن الخطأ ا.هـ. ومَا قِيلَ عليهِ : إِنَّهُ إفراط بدليل صحة الأخذ عن أهل مكَّةَ ،والمدينة، وبِلُغَتِهِمْ جَاءَ التَّنزيلُ؛ فَذُهولٌ عَن معنى قولهم : اللغة لا تُؤخَذَ عن حضري ... إلخ، إذ ليس معناه: مَن سَكَنَ الحاضرة، بل المعنى حَضَريٌّ خالط العجم ونشأ بين أظهرهم، كما يُشيرُ لذلك قول : المحشّي: «الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُخالطوا الحَضَرَ»، ولَمْ يَقُلْ: «أهل الحاضرة»، فالمضافُ مُقَدَّرُ ; أي: أهلُ الحَضَرِ ، فإِنَّهُ لَمَّا فُتِحَت مدائنُ العَجم والرُّوم، وانتشر العرب فيها وتناسلوا ; دخلَ اللَّحنُ على نَسلِهم بسبب المخالطة، وقصَّةُ أبي الأسودِ الدُّوليّ الَّتي دَعَت عليا رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ لوضعِ النَّحو؛ شاهدٌ على مَا قُلْنَا، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
[Nuh?] His statement: 'to all of creation' includes the usage of 'kāffa' in the genitive case, but it is only used in the accusative as a circumstantial accusative, as in al-Mughnī. He said: The permission of al-Zamakhshari for two possibilities (i.e., circumstantial accusative from the subject or object) in His statement: 'Enter into peace, all of you' [al-Baqara 2:208] is an error, because 'kāffa' is specific to those who possess intellect. And his error in His statement: 'We have not sent you except as a bringer of good news and a warner to all mankind' [Saba 34:28] when he considered 'kāffa' as an adjective for a deleted noun, i.e., 'a universal message'. Worse, because he added to his usage for non-intelligent beings, taking it out from the state he committed to of being a circumstantial accusative. And his error in the introduction to al-Mufassal is worse and worse, for taking it out from the accusative based on foundation. End. The commentator said: The claim that al-Zamakhshari is among those whose constructions are to be cited is not heard, because that is a rank not attained by the urban Arab, so how can the non-Arab attain it? That is because God specially protected the Arabs who did not mix with city-dwellers from error in their tongues. End. And what is said against him [the commentator]: That is excessive, as proven by the validity of taking [language] from the people of Mecca and Medina, and the revelation came in their language. This is a misunderstanding of the meaning of their saying: 'Language is not taken from an urban Arab'... etc. The meaning is not one who lives in the city, but rather an urban Arab who mixed with non-Arabs and grew up among them, as indicated by the commentator's words: 'those who did not mix with city-dwellers' and he did not say 'people of the city'. So the genitive noun is implied, i.e., 'people of the city'. For when the cities of the non-Arabs and Romans were conquered, and the Arabs spread among them and interbred, solecism entered among their descendants due to mixing. And the story of Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali that prompted 'Ali, may God be pleased with him, to establish grammar is a witness to what we said. So ponder.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashīya) on the correct usage of the word 'kāffa' and the authority of Arabic. The author begins by noting that the word 'kāffa' is only correctly used in the accusative as a circumstantial accusative, not in the genitive. He then criticizes al-Zamakhshari for allowing it to be used as an adjective or in a different case, citing two Qur'anic verses. The first is 'Enter into peace, all of you' where the word is indeed in the accusative, but al-Zamakhshari allowed that it could be a circumstantial accusative of the subject or object; the author says this is erroneous because 'kāffa' is only used for rational beings. The second verse is about the Prophet being sent 'kāffa' to people, where al-Zamakhshari considered 'kāffa' an adjective for an implied noun 'message'. The author says this is worse because it removes it from the circumstantial accusative usage. He then mentions an even worse error in the introduction to al-Mufassal. Next, a commentator (al-Muḥashshī) argues that al-Zamakhshari's linguistic authority is not acceptable because he is non-Arab and Arabs who did not mix with city-dwellers are the authorities. This leads to a discussion about the source of Arabic: some say language cannot be taken from urban Arabs, but the commentator clarifies that this means those who mixed with non-Arabs, not those who lived in cities like Mecca and Medina. The story of Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali is given as evidence that the mixing of Arabs with non-Arabs after conquests led to linguistic errors, prompting the need for grammar.

Key Terms
كافة (kāffa): All, entire; here discussed as a word that must be in the accusative case as a circumstantial accusative, specific to rational beings [Note: In later grammatical works, the usage of 'kāffa' is sometimes allowed as an adjective; this passage insists on the strictness of the standard view.]
حاليّة (ḥāliyya): The state of being a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl), which is the only correct grammatical function for 'kāffa'
حضري (ḥaḍarī): An urban Arab who mixed with non-Arabs; distinguished from a desert Arab (badawī) who preserved pure Arabic [Note: This is a technical term in Arabic linguistics for an authority on language; it is not used in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is al-Zamakhshari's usage of 'kāffa' in the verse 'Wa mā arsalnāka illā kāffatan li-n-nās' considered worse?
A) Because he used it as a circumstantial accusative
B) Because he considered it an adjective for an implied noun, taking it out of the circumstantial accusative
C) Because he used it for non-intelligent beings
D) Because he made it a noun in the genitive

Key Takeaway
The case of 'kāffa' shows that linguistic authority in Arabic is reserved for those who preserved the language unsullied by foreign mixing, a principle that underscores the importance of reliable transmission in Islamic scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another Qur'anic word that has a strict grammatical condition (e.g., 'inna' and its sisters) and explain how violating that condition would change the meaning of an ayah.

Distinguishing Fadila and Fadila; Reading Unsiyya
Author defines fadila and fawadil, and explains two interpretations of unsiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fadila and fadila (fawadil) and analyse the two readings of 'unsiyya' in defining prophetic virtues.

Core Idea
Virtue's transcendence depends on whether its benefit stays or extends; the philology of 'unsiyya' reveals its theological weight.

Precise definitions are crucial in Islamic texts, especially when discussing virtues (fada'il) attributed to the Prophet. The commentator breaks down the term 'al-fada'il al-unsiyya' by first contrasting fadila and fadila, then examining two possible readings of 'unsiyya'—each carrying distinct implications about the nature of these virtues. This lesson will guide you through the lexical and conceptual distinctions so you can apply similar precision in your own study of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms and their definitions
Locate the definitions of 'fadila' (and its plural 'fada'il') and 'fadila' (and its plural 'fawadil') in the Arabic text. Fadila is defined as 'al-maziyya al-qasira' (non-transcendent merit), while fadila is 'al-maziyya al-muta'addiya' (transcendent merit). Note which benefits only the possessor and which benefits others.
2
Contrast the definitions
Reflect on the difference: a non-transcendent merit is self-contained, whereas a transcendent merit extends benefit to others. The commentator suggests that the context would have been better served by using 'fawadil' (transcendent merits) rather than 'fada'il'. Consider why: the virtues being described (knowledge, generosity, bravery, etc.) are typically transcendent.
3
Examine the two readings of 'unsiyya'
The word 'unsiyya' can be read with a kasr on the hamza (unsiyya, from 'ins' meaning human) or with a damm (unsiyya, from 'uns' meaning intimacy/companionship). The first reading yields 'virtues attributed to humans' (like knowledge, generosity, bravery, patience, forbearance). The second reading yields 'virtues that bring comfort and intimacy', contrasting with wildness (wahsha).
4
Evaluate why the commentator prefers the damm reading
The commentator states that the damm reading is 'better' (awla). Al-Attar elaborates: the damm reading reminds of the Prophet's lack of pride and tyranny, as intimacy implies approachability. He refutes the criticism that this reading is 'cold forgotten', arguing it is 'accepted direction' because acquiring virtues is specific to humans, and the reading highlights the origin of virtues while excluding prophetic perfections (which are beyond human acquisition).
5
Synthesize the implications
Consider how the choice of reading affects the meaning of 'al-fada'il al-unsiyya' in the original text. The damm reading aligns with praising the Prophet's character, emphasizing that his virtues are comforting and accessible, while the kasr reading simply labels the virtues as human. The commentator's preference shows an awareness of both linguistic and theological nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : وَالْفَضَائِل جمع فضيلة؛ وهي المزيّة القاصرة، ويقابلها الفواضل؛ جمع فاضلة؛ وهي المزيّة المتعدية، ولو عبر بها كان أولى، والإنسية - بالكسر - نسبة للإِنسِ خلاف الجنّ؛ أي: الفضائل المنسوبة للإنس كالعلم، والكرم، والشَّجاعة، والصبر، والحِلْم ، أو الأنسية - بالصّم - نسبة للأنس ضد الوحشة؛ أي : الفضائل التي يُستأنس بها، وهو أولى.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: الأُنْسِيَّةِ) بضم الهمزة؛ نسبةٌ للأُنسِ ضِدَّ الوحشة، ففيه تنبية على عدم كبره وجبروته، قيل : ومن البارِدِ المغسول قراءتُه بكسر الهمزة؛ نسبةً إلى الإِنْسِ مقابل الجن .ا.هـ. وأقول : ليس هو من البارِدِ المغسول ؛ بل مِنَ التَّوجيه المقبولِ ؛ لأنَّ اقتناء الفضائل واكتسابها مُختصّ بالنَّوعِ الإنساني، فَفِيه تنبيه على أصل الفضائل، وأنَّهُ جمع منها ما يمكن تحصيله للنَّوعِ الإنساني مِمَّا يصحُ أن يَتَّصِفَ بِهِ، فَخَرَجَتِ الكَمَالاتُ النَّبوية.
His statement: 'al-fada'il' is the plural of fadila, which is a non-transcendent merit, and its opposite is al-fawadil, plural of fadila, which is a transcendent merit. It would have been better to use it. 'al-unsiyya' with kasra is a relation to ins (human) as opposed to jinn, i.e., the virtues attributed to humans like knowledge, generosity, bravery, patience, forbearance. Or 'al-unsiyya' with damm is a relation to uns (intimacy) as opposed to wildness, i.e., virtues that are comforting, and this is better.
Al-Attar: His statement: 'al-unsiyya' with damm of the hamza is a relation to uns as opposed to wildness, so it contains a reminder of his lack of pride and tyranny. It was said: Among the cold forgotten is reading it with kasra of the hamza as a relation to ins as opposed to jinn. End quote. I say: It is not from the cold forgotten, but rather from the accepted direction, because acquiring and obtaining virtues is specific to the human species, so it contains a reminder of the origin of virtues, and that he gathered from them what can be obtained for the human species from that which is truly attributed to him, thus the prophetic perfections are excluded.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the term 'al-fada'il al-unsiyya' from the matn. The commentator first explains the word 'al-fada'il' as the plural of 'fadila', which is a non-transcendent merit (a quality that benefits only the possessor), and contrasts it with 'al-fawadil' (plural of 'fadila'), which is a transcendent merit (a quality that benefits others). He remarks that using 'fawadil' would have been better in this context. Then he analyzes 'al-unsiyya' with two possible readings: with a kasra on the hamza, it relates to 'ins' (human being), giving virtues such as knowledge, generosity, bravery, patience, and forbearance; with a damm, it relates to 'uns' (intimacy), meaning virtues that bring comfort. The commentator prefers the damm reading as more appropriate. Then al-Attar adds a further refinement: the damm reading emphasizes the absence of pride and tyranny. He refutes a criticism that the damm reading is 'cold forgotten', arguing instead that it is 'accepted direction' because acquiring virtues is specific to humans, and this reading highlights the origin of virtues and excludes prophetic perfections. The discussion shows careful lexical and theological analysis of a single phrase.

Key Takeaway
Precision in defining terms like fadila/fadila and understanding reading variations (like unsiyya) is essential for accurate theological and lexical analysis in Islamic texts.

Lexical and Rhetorical Layers in Praise Phrases
Lexical and rhetorical commentary on a praise phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to break down the lexical roots and metaphorical extensions in the phrase 'ara'ik al-saltana bihadratihi al-shamma' using the commentator's method.

Core Idea
Praise phrases are built from layered words: each has a concrete origin and a metaphorical lift.

When the commentator explains 'ara'ik al-saltana bihadratihi al-shamma', he does not just translate—he uncovers the etymological roots (arika from arak tree), the semantic shift (hadra from courtyard to presence), and the rhetorical figures (shamma' from high nose to elevated). Understanding this method trains you to handle any complex expression in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase components
Locate the three key words in the Arabic: 'ara'ik', 'hadra', 'shamma'. Note that 'ara'ik' is plural, 'hadra' is a verbal noun, 'shamma' is a feminine adjective.
2
Trace the etymology of each
For 'arika', the commentator gives two possible origins: from the arak tree or from the verb 'araka' meaning to reside. For 'hadra', it originally means 'the vicinity or courtyard of a man'. For 'shamma', it is derived from 'shammam' meaning height of the nose.
The etymology shows the concrete image behind the abstract.
3
Identify the metaphorical extension
Arika moves from a tree-made couch to any throne. Hadra moves from courtyard to the person's presence (essence). Shamma moves from high nose to absolute loftiness.
This is the process of 'itlaq min al-taqyid' (absoluteness from restriction) mentioned.
4
Analyze the rhetorical figures in al-Attar's addition
Al-Attar identifies two possibilities: a mursal metaphor (part for whole) or a tacit metaphor (likening hadra to a woman with high nose, with 'shamma' as a fictional object). Decide which fits.
This shows how the same phrase can be analyzed rhetorically in multiple ways.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : وَشَرَفَ أَرَائِكَ جمع أريكة وهي الشرير، وتسميته بذلك؛ إمَّا لِكُونِهِ في الأصل كانَ يُتَّخذُ من أراك ، أو لكونه مكان الإقامة من قولهم: أرك بالمكان أروكاً : إذا أقامَ .به (قَوْلُهُ : السَّلْطَنَةِ) أي أهلها أي الشلاطين. (قَوْلُهُ : بِحَضْرَتِهِ) مُتعلّق بشرف ; أي : بذاته، والحضرة في الأصل: قربُ الرَّجل وفناؤه، و(الشَّمَّاء) : المرتفعة، والشَّممُ في الأصل: ارتفاع الأنف، أطلق عن قيدهِ وأُريد به مُطلق ارتفاع.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَرَائِكَ السَّلْطَنَةِ) الأرائِكُ جمعُ أريكة؛ بمعنى الشرير، سُمِّيت بذلك لِكونها مكان الإقامة، يُقَالُ : أرك بالمكان أروكاً ؛ أقام على رعي الأَرَاكِ، ثمَّ استُعمل في مُطلَقِ الإقامة. (قَوْلُهُ : بِحَضْرَتِهِ السَّمَاءِ) حضرةُ الرَّجلِ : مَوضِعُ مُحُضُورِه، والشَّمَاءُ : ذاتُ الشَّمَمِ؛ أي: ارتفاعُ الأَنفِ، وفي الكلام مجاز مُرسَل؛ علاقتُهُ الإطلاق عن التَّقييد، أو استعارة مكنيَّةٌ بتشبيه الحضرة بامرأةٍ شَمَّاءَ ، والشَّمَّاءُ تَخييل .
His statement: 'and the honor of the ara'ik' – ara'ik is the plural of arieka, which is the couch; it is called that either because originally it was made from the arak tree, or because it is a place of residence from their saying: 'araka bi al-makani arukan' meaning he resided there. His statement: 'al-saltana' means its people, i.e., the sultans. His statement: 'bihadratihi' is attached to 'sharaf', i.e., by his essence; hadra originally means the vicinity of a man and his courtyard; 'al-shamma' means the elevated; shammam originally means the height of the nose, released from its restriction and intended as absolute elevation.
Al-Attar: His statement: 'ara'ik al-saltana' – ara'ik is plural of arieka meaning couch; it is named that because it is a place of residence; they say: 'araka bi al-makani arukan' he stayed and grazed on arak, then used for absolute residence. His statement: 'bihadratihi al-shamma' – a man's hadra is the place of his presence; al-shamma is the one with shammam, i.e., high nose; in the phrase there is a mursal metaphor, its relationship is absoluteness from restriction, or a tacit metaphor by likening the presence to a woman with a high nose, and 'al-shamma' is a fictional object.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides a lexical commentary on a phrase praising the Prophet or ruler. The commentator first explains 'ara'ik' (plural of 'arika') as couches or thrones, deriving it either from the arak tree (used to make furniture) or from the root 'ark' meaning residence. Then 'al-saltana' is glossed as the sultans (rulers). 'Bihadratihi' is attached to 'sharaf', meaning 'by his presence' or 'by his essence'. 'Hadra' originally means the vicinity or courtyard of a man, here used for the person himself. 'Al-shamma' means elevated, originally referring to a high nose but used generically for height. Al-Attar adds that 'ara'ik al-saltana' are the couches of sovereignty, and 'bihadratihi al-shamma' involves either a mursal metaphor (the part for the whole) or a tacit metaphor likening the presence to a woman with a high nose, with 'al-shamma' being a fictional object. This shows the rhetorical and lexical depth of the praise formula.

أريكة
حضرة
شماء

A) A high nose
B) The vicinity or courtyard of a man
C) A couch
D) Absolute elevation

Key Takeaway
Understanding the etymology and metaphorical shifts in key terms is essential for accurate interpretation of Islamic texts.

Does the commentator see it as a mursal metaphor or a tacit metaphor?

Understanding Iqitibas in the Commentator's Praise
Author explains the phrase about giving kingship and wisdom, noting Quranic allusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an iqitibas in a classical Islamic text and explain how the commentator uses it to enrich the meaning of his praise.

Core Idea
An iqitibas borrows Quranic phrasing without attribution, infusing authority into one's speech.

The commentator begins his introduction with a powerful phrase: 'Wa atahu al-mulk wa al-hikmah' – a clear echo of the Quranic verse about Prophet David (2:251). Al-Dasuqi's commentary reveals that this is not a direct quote but an iqitibas, a rhetorical device that allows the commentator to invoke divine authority while adapting the phrase to his own context. In this lesson, we'll dissect how iqitibas works and why it is effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic allusion
The commentator says: 'wa atahu al-mulk wa al-hikmah'. Compare this with the Quranic verse in 2:251: 'Wa atahu Allahu al-mulka wa al-hikmata'. Notice the absence of the word 'Allah' and the slight change in case ending (al-mulk vs al-mulka).
2
Define iqitibas as given by al-Dasuqi
Al-Dasuqi defines iqitibas: 'that you include in the speech something from the Quran or hadith, not claiming that it is from it, and slight change does not harm it.' This is the core of the device.
The change here is omitting 'Allah' and adjusting the case.
3
Analyze the commentator's purpose
By using iqitibas, the commentator links his praise to the divine gift of kingship and wisdom given to Prophet David, thus elevating the person being praised. The slight change makes it his own expression while retaining the Quranic resonance.
This is a common technique in Islamic scholarly introductions.
4
Connect to the broader commentary style
Al-Dasuqi then proceeds to explain each word in detail, showing how the commentator's phrase is enriched by metaphor (tashyid, raf' ma'alim) and lexical precision. The iqitibas is just one layer of meaning.
The commentary demonstrates the depth of rhetorical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الشارح: وآتاه الملك والحكمة وعلَّمه ممَّا يشاءُ، ووفَّقَه لتشييد قواعدِ الدِّيْنِ، ورَفْعِ معالم المعاني (١) لأهل اليقين، وخصَّصَهُ بِاللُّطفِ العميم،
الدسوقي

(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُلْكَ ) أي : التَّصرُّفَ بالأمر والنهي، والمراد بالحكمة : العِلْمُ النَّافع، وفي قوله وآتاه . . . إلخ اقتباس، وهو: أن يضمَّنَ الكلام شيئاً من القرآن أو الحديث لا على أنَّه منه، ولا يضرُّ فيه التَّغييرُ اليسيرُ كَما هنا، فإنَّ لفظ الآية : وَءَاتَهُ اللهُ الْمُلْكَ وَالْحِكْمَةَ... إلخ [البقرة: ٢٥١] وهنا لم يذكر لفظ الجلالة (قَوْلُهُ : وَوَفَّقَهُ) أي : خلَقَ فيه قدرة على التشييد ورغبه فيه . (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَهْل الْيَقِينِ) أي: أهلِ العِلْمِ، وهو مُتعلّق بمحذوف صفة للمعالي؛ أي : المعالي الكائنة لأهل اليقين؛ أي: أَنَّهُ رفعَ وأظهر العلاماتِ المراتب الكائنة للعلماء، وهي عِلْمُهُم بعد أنْ كانَ مَخفيًّا لا يشتغل به أحد، أو متعلق برفع ; أي: أنَّه رفع لأهلِ العِلْمِ العلاماتِ الدَّالَّة على رفعهم، وهيَ العِلْمُ . (قَوْلُهُ : بِاللَّطْفِ الباء داخلةٌ على المقصور، والمراد به الإحسان . (قَوْلُهُ : الْعَمِيم) أي : الكثيرِ العموم.
Commentator's introduction: And He gave him kingship and wisdom and taught him from what He wills, and gave him success to strengthen the foundations of the religion, and to raise the landmarks of meanings (or high ranks) for the people of certainty, and singled him out with comprehensive kindness.
Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'wa atahu' i.e., He gave him. His statement: 'al-mulk' i.e., authority to command and forbid, and by wisdom is meant beneficial knowledge. In his statement 'wa atahu... etc.' there is an iqitibas (Quranic allusion), which is to include in the speech something from the Quran or hadith without claiming it is from it, and slight change does not harm it, as here, for the wording of the verse is: 'Wa atahu Allahu al-mulka wa al-hikmata...' [Quran 2:251], and here the divine name is not mentioned. His statement: 'wa waffaqahu' i.e., He created in him ability for strengthening and desire for it. His statement: 'li-tashyidi' i.e., to raise, manifest, and publicize; tashyid originally means raising a deficient building, so it is used metaphorically as an explicit metaphor, or used as a mursal metaphor due to the relationship of absoluteness and restriction; the addition of 'qawa'id' to 'al-din' is explanatory. His statement: 'wa raf'i ma'alim' – ma'alim is plural of ma'lam, which is a sign by which one is guided; his statement: 'al-ma'ali' is plural of ma'la, which is a high rank; i.e., raising the signs that indicate high ranks; those signs are like knowledge, generosity, and authorship; the intended meaning of raising the mentioned signs is manifesting them; so resemblance of manifestation to raising, and its name is used metaphorically. His statement: 'li-ahli al-yaqin' i.e., the people of knowledge, and it is connected to a deleted adjective for 'al-ma'ali'; i.e., the high ranks belonging to the people of certainty; i.e., that he raised and manifested the signs of the ranks belonging to the scholars, which is their knowledge after it was hidden and no one engaged in it, or it is connected to 'raf''; i.e., that he raised for the people of knowledge the signs indicating their elevation, which is knowledge. His statement: 'bi al-lutfi' – the bi is attached to the thing limited, and by it is meant benevolence. His statement: 'al-'amim' i.e., the one of abundant comprehensiveness.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the commentator's introduction (muqaddimat al-sharh). It consists of a line of praise: 'And He gave him kingship and wisdom...' followed by detailed commentary by al-Dasuqi. The commentator explains each word: 'wa atahu' means He gave; 'al-mulk' means authority to command and forbid; 'al-hikmah' is beneficial knowledge. He notes that the phrase is an iqitibas (Quranic allusion) to Qatada's story of Prophet David (or Solomon) in Quran 2:251, where the wording is slightly changed (omitting 'Allah'). Then 'wa waffaqahu' means created ability and desire. 'Li-tashyidi' means to raise and manifest, originally referring to raising a deficient building, used here metaphorically. 'Wa raf'i ma'alim' – 'ma'alim' is plural of 'ma'lam' (sign); there is a textual variant: 'ma'ali' (high ranks). The commentator interprets 'raf'i ma'alim' as raising the signs that indicate high ranks, such as knowledge, generosity, and authorship. The phrase 'li-ahli al-yaqin' means for the people of knowledge; it clarifies that the high ranks are for the scholars. Finally, 'bi al-lutfi al-'amim' means with comprehensive kindness. This commentary shows the rhetorical and lexical enrichment of a simple praise sentence, tying it to Quranic precedent and explaining each metaphorical layer.

تشييد
معلم

Infinite Regress Objection to Defining Judgment
Objection that the definition leads to infinite regress and the answer using idh'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the infinite regress objection to a definition of judgment and explain how the concept of idh'an resolves it.

Core Idea
Analytical definitions of judgment lead to regress; idh'an is the holistic act that stops the chain.

This passage tackles a powerful objection to a common definition of judgment (hukm). If judgment is 'perceiving that a relation is actual,' then each such perception itself seems to require another judgment, leading to an infinite regress. The solution lies in recognizing that judgment is not an analytical process but a holistic act of acquiescence called idh'an. By understanding this, you will see how logicians defend the simplicity of judgment against seemingly devastating logical attacks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Locate the definition: judgment (hukm) is defined as the perception that a relation (between subject and predicate) is actual (wāqi'a). Find this in the Arabic: 'والحكم الذي هو في بيانه'.
2
Trace the regress
Follow the objection: If judgment is perceiving a relation's actuality, then that perception itself is a new relation (between the original relation and actuality). This new relation must itself be perceived as actual, requiring another judgment, and so on ad infinitum.
The text says: 'فيظهر فيه تصديق وحكم آخر... وهكذا' – 'so another assent and judgment appears, and so on'.
3
Understand why the regress is impossible
The text declares this infinite chain as 'باطل قطعاً' (absolutely false). If one judgment depended on infinitely many prior judgments, no judgment could ever occur. Recognize this as a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the solution: idh'an
The answer (مخلص الجواب) states that the perception after grasping the two extremes is a holistic matter (أمر إجمالي) called idh'an. This is not an analytical judgment; it is a simple act of acquiescence. Only when expressed in detail does it appear to involve another judgment.
The term idh'an (الإذعان) is key: a non-analytic, immediate mental acceptance.
5
Test with conscience (wijdan)
The text appeals to intuitive experience (الوجدان). When you judge 'Zayd is standing,' you do not perform an infinite series of meta-judgments. The judgment feels immediate and holistic, supporting the idh'an account.
This step shows how logical analysis must align with psychological reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكم الذي هو في بيانه، فههنا تصديق وحكم آخر، وهو أن تدرك النفس أن النسبة بين تلك النسبة وبين واقعة واقعة، فيلزم هناك تصديق وحكم ثالث، وهكذا؛ فيتوقف حصول حكم واحد على أحكام غير متناهية، وهو باطل قطعاً. ومحصل الجواب أن المدرك بعد إدراك الطرفين أمر إجمالي يقال له الإذعان إذا عبر عنه بالتفصيل يظهر فيه تصديق آخر، والحكم هو ذلك المجمل كما يشهد به الوجدان.
And the judgment that is in its explanation: here there is another assent and judgment, which is that the soul perceives that the relation between that relation and actuality is actual, so a third assent and judgment follows, and so on; thus the occurrence of a single judgment depends on infinite judgments, which is absolutely false. The summary of the answer is that the perception after perceiving the two extremes is a holistic matter called idh'an; if it is expressed in detail, another assent appears, and the judgment is that holistic matter as conscience testifies.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents a classical logical objection and its refutation. The objection is against the definition of judgment (hukm) as 'perception that the relation is actual.' The critic (al-'Attar) argues that if judgment is perceiving that a relation is actual, then that very perception is a new relation (between the original relation and actuality), which must itself be perceived as actual, leading to a third judgment, and so forth ad infinitum. This would make any single judgment impossible, since it would require an infinite chain of prior judgments—an absurd consequence. The answer (from al-Dasuqi) is that the perception after grasping the two terms (subject and predicate) is a holistic, non-analytic act called idh'an (acquiescence). When this holistic act is analyzed into detailed components, it appears to involve another judgment, but in reality, the judgment is just that holistic perception. This is supported by intuitive experience (wijdan): one immediately feels that judging 'Zayd is standing' does not require an infinite series of meta-judgments.

Key Terms
الإذعان (al-idh'an)
الحكم (al-hukm)
التصديق (al-tasdiq)
الوجدان (al-wijdān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That every definition requires another definition
B) That perceiving a relation's actuality would itself require a judgment about that perception, leading to an infinite chain
C) That taslim cannot be defined
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Judgment is not an analytical process but a holistic act of acquiescence (idh'an), which prevents the infinite regress that would arise if judgment required explicit meta-perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the definition of 'knowledge' (al-'ilm) as 'certain belief that corresponds to reality.' Could a similar infinite regress objection be raised? If so, how would the concept of idh'an resolve it? Provide a brief analysis.
?
Task 2: In a debate, an opponent challenges the definition of 'valid contract' (al-'aqd al-sahīh) in fiqh as 'a contract that fulfills its conditions and produces its effects.' Construct a regress objection similar to the one in this lesson, and then defend the definition using the idea of a holistic perception (idh'an).
