Critiquing Thought as Motion in Quality
Author discusses and defends the claim that thought is a movement in the category of quality against al-Dawānī's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Dawānī's objection to thought being motion in quality and evaluate the author's defense using the concept of attention and intensity of mental forms.

Core Idea
Thought's finite states cannot produce the infinite individuals required for continuous motion.

The claim that thought (fikr) is a motion in psychic quality seems plausible, but al-Jalāl al-Dawānī challenges it with a rigorous logical objection: motion demands an infinite series of distinct individuals per instant, yet thought contains only a bounded number of knowledge states. The author responds by invoking attention (iltifāt) and the varying intensity of mental forms to salvage the idea. This debate sharpens our understanding of what counts as motion and how mental processes relate to Aristotelian categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Find the statement that the scholars (al-qawm) made thought a motion in psychic qualities. This is the target of criticism.
The claim is in the first sentence: 'وجَعْلُ الحركة المذكورة من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيَّاتِ النَّفْسانيَّةِ'.
2
Extract al-Dawānī's objection
Al-Dawānī argues that motion requires an infinite series of distinct individuals (afrad) from the category at each instant. Since thought only has a finite number of knowledge states, it cannot meet this condition.
Key phrases: 'لا بد في الحركة من كون الشيء بحيث يفرض في كل آن فرد... والآنات غير واقفة عند حد'.
3
Analyze the proposed counterargument
Someone might say that attention (iltifāt) gradually transfers from one concept to another, producing a continuum. The author rejects this by noting that attention is an act of the soul, and motion is restricted to four categories only (quantity, quality, position, posture).
The counterargument is introduced with 'لا يقال: النفس إذا لاحظت الجنس...' and refuted with 'لأنا نقول: قد صرحوا بأن الالتفات فعل من أفعال النفس'.
4
Assess the concession
The author concedes that if varying degrees of attention imply varying intensity of mental forms, then the soul could have a motion among forms differing in intensity, which is not far-fetched. Evaluate whether this salvages the original claim.
The concession begins with 'وذلك لو قيل بأن اختلاف مراتب الالتفات يستلزم اختلاف الصور في الشدة والضعف'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجَعْلُ الحركة المذكورة من قبيل الحركة في الكيفيَّاتِ النَّفْسانيَّةِ هو ما نَصَّ عليهِ القَومُ، وبحث فيه الجَلالُ الدَّوَانيُّ بأنَّهُ لا بُدَّ في الحركةِ مِن كَونِ الشَّيْء بحيثُ يفرضُ في كُلِّ آن فرد مِنَ المقولةِ الَّتي فيها الحركة لا يكون ذلك الفرد في الآنِ السَّابق ولا في الآنِ اللَّاحِقِ، والآنَاتُ الَّتي يُمكِنُ فرضُها في الزَّمانِ غيرُ واقفةٍ عندَ حَدَّ عندَهُم، وَالَّا الْأَفْرَادُ الْمَفْرُوضَةُ غَيْرُ وَاقِفَةٍ، وَمَوْلَّا أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي صُورَةِ الْفِكْرِ إِلَّا عُلُومٌ محصورة؛ لَا سِيَّمَا فِي الرُّجُوعِ مِنَ الْمَبَادِي إِلَى الْمَطَالِبِ، فَلَا يُصَوَّرُ كَوْنُ النَّفْسِ فِي كُلِّ آنٍ مُتَّصِفَةٌ بِفَرْدٍ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ لَا يَكُونُ قَبْلَهُ وَلَا بَعْدَهُ، لَا يُقَالُ: النَّفْسُ إِذَا لاحظت الجنس مثلاً والتفتت إليه؛ فإنَّها تنتقل منه إلى الفصل بالتدريج، ويضعف التفاتها إلى الجنس تدريجاً، ويقوى التفاتها إلى الفصل بالتدريج؛ لأنا نقول: قد صرَّحُوا بِأَنَّ الالتفات فِعْلٌ مِنْ أَفْعَالِ النَّفْسِ، وَقَدْ صَرَّحُوا بِأَنَّ لَا حَرَكَةَ إِلَّا فِي مَقُولَةِ الْكُمِّ وَالْكَيْفِ وَالْأَيْنَ وَالْوَضْعِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ فِي الِالْتِفَاتِ حَرَكَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ لَوْ قِيْلَ بِأَنَّ اخْتِلَافَ مَرَاتِبِ الِالْتِفَاتِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ اخْتِلَافَ الصُّوَرِ فِي الشِّدَّةِ وَالضَّعْفِ؛ فَلِلنَّفْسِ فِي كُلِّ مَرْتَبَةٍ مِنْ مَرَاتِبِ الِالْتِفَاتِ صُورَةٌ فِي مَرْتَبَةٍ مِنَ الشِّدَّةِ وَالضَّعْفِ؛ مُخْتَلِفَةٍ فِي الشِّدَّةِ وَالضَّعْفِ لِلصُّوَرِ السَّابِقَةِ وَاللَّاحِقَةِ، فَيَكُونُ لَهَا حَرَكَةٌ فِي الصُّوَرِ لَمْ يَبْعُدْ ا . هـ .
And the making of the aforementioned movement of the type of movement in psychic qualities is what the scholars stated. And al-Jalāl al-Dawānī investigated it: that for motion it is necessary that the thing be such that in every instant a particular from the category in which the motion occurs is posited, such that that particular does not exist in the previous instant nor in the subsequent instant. And the instants that can be posited in time are not limited according to them, and likewise the individuals posited are not limited. And it is known that in the picture of thought there is only a bounded number of pieces of knowledge, especially in the return from the principles to the sought objects; so it is not conceivable that the soul is at every instant characterized by a particular of knowledge that does not exist before or after. One cannot say: the soul when it observes the genus, for example, and attends to it, then it transfers from it to the species gradually, and its attention to the genus weakens gradually, and its attention to the species strengthens gradually; because we say: they have stated that attention (iltifāt) is an act of the soul, and they have stated that there is no motion except in the categories of quantity, quality, position, and posture. So there is no motion in attention. And even if it is said that the variation in degrees of attention necessitates variation in the cognitive forms (suwar) in intensity and weakness; so for the soul, at every degree of attention there is a form at a degree of intensity and weakness, different from the preceding and subsequent forms, then it would have a motion among forms, which is not far-fetched. End.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) on the earlier claim that thought (fikr) involves motion in the category of quality (kayf). The author (al-Dasūqī) acknowledges that early scholars (al-qawm) asserted this. However, al-Jalāl al-Dawānī raises an objection: for any motion, at each instant there must be a new individual from the category, distinct from previous and later ones, and these instants are infinite. But in thought, we have only a finite number of mental states (e.g., knowledge of genus, species, minor premise, major premise), especially in the second movement returning from principles to the desired conclusion. Thus it is impossible for the soul to have a continuous series of distinct individuals of knowledge at every instant. A possible counterargument is that the soul gradually transfers attention from one concept to another, with weakening and strengthening of attention. The author responds by pointing out that attention (iltifāt) is an act of the soul, and earlier authorities stated that motion only occurs in four categories: quantity, quality, position, and posture. Since attention is not one of these, it cannot involve motion. However, the author then entertains a concession: if one argues that varying degrees of attention imply varying intensities of the mental forms (suwar), then the soul would have a series of forms differing in intensity, and that could be considered a motion among forms. The author ends by saying that this is not far-fetched (lam yab'ud), implying the defense is plausible.

Key Terms
التفات
الحركة في الكيف
صور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is al-Dawānī's main objection to considering thought as motion in quality?
A) Thought has too many steps to be a single motion.
B) Finite mental states cannot produce infinite individuals required for continuous motion.
C) Attention (iltifāt) is not a category of motion.
D) The soul does not experience time continuously.

Key Takeaway
The debate over thought as motion in quality hinges on whether finite mental states can satisfy the infinite individual requirement of motion, a problem resolved by considering intensity variations in mental forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct al-Dawānī's objection in your own words and state whether the author's final concession successfully answers it. Support your answer with details from the passage.
?
Consider a modern example: a student gradually understanding a difficult concept. Does this involve motion in quality according to the criteria in this passage? Explain why or why not using the concepts of finite states and attention.

Al-Dasuqi's Four Interpretations of Acquired Knowledge
Al-Dasuqi's analysis of the phrase 'acquisition of the image'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four interpretations of 'acquisition of the image' and identify which category of knowledge each implies according to al-Dasuqi.

Core Idea
The same definition yields four different views of knowledge depending on how 'acquisition' is interpreted.

Al-Dasuqi unpacks the phrase 'acquisition of the image in the mind' and shows that its grammatical construction leads to four distinct ontological positions on what knowledge is. This analysis reveals how a single definition can mask deep philosophical disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of idafa
Al-Dasuqi first splits the construct 'hudul al-surah' into two types: descriptive attribution (idafat al-sifa li'l-mawsuf) and real attribution (idafa haqiqiyya). The first treats the image as a quality of the mind; the second treats the acquisition as a real process.
2
Break down the real attribution into three sub-interpretations
For the real attribution, 'hudul' can mean impression (intiqash), acquisition (tahsil), or relation (nisba). Each corresponds to a different metaphysical account of knowledge.
3
Link each interpretation to a category of knowledge
Impression implies knowledge is a passion (infi‘al); acquisition implies it is an action (fi‘l); relation implies it is a relation (idafa). The descriptive attribution aligns with knowledge as a quality (kayf).
Al-Dasuqi notes that the quality view is the preponderant one.
4
Recognize al-Dasuqi's own position
He states that the descriptive attribution (kayf) is the preponderant view (al-rajih). This shows his preference among the four.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ (وَقَدْ يَقَعُ فِيْهِ)؛ أي: في ذلك الاكتساب،
الدسوقي
واعلَمْ أَنَّهُ إِنْ جُعِلَتْ إضافة حصول من إضافةِ الصِّفة للموصوف؛ أي: صورة الشَّيء الحاصلة في العقل؛ كانَتْ تلكَ الصُّورة مجزوماً بها أو مظنونةٌ؛ كانَتْ مطابقة للواقع أوْ لَا ، كانت تصوريَّةٌ أو تصديقيَّة ؛ كان مارا على القول بأنَّ العِلْمَ مِن قبيل الكيف، وهو الراجح، وإن جعلت الإضافة حقيقية، وهو المتبادر من كلامه، فإِنْ فُسِّرَ حصولُ الصُّورة بانتقاشِهَا في العقل؛ كانَ مارا على القول بأنَّ العِلْمَ انفعال، وإنْ فُسِّرَ بتحصيل الصُّورة في العقل؛ كانَ مارا على القول بأنَّهُ من قبيل الفعل(۱)، وإنْ فُسِّرَ بالنّسبة الحاصلة بين الحاصل والمحصول؛ كان مارا على القول بأنَّ العِلْمَ مِن قبيل الإضافة .
(قَوْلُهُ : الاكْتِسَابِ) أي: الاكتساب بالنَّظرِ ؛ أي : العِلْم المكتسب به .
العطّار
نفس المدركِ وغيره؛ فالمراد بالعقل : الذَّاتُ المجرَّدَةُ المعبَّرُ عنهَا بِالنَّفْسِ النَّاطِقَةِ عندَهُم، وبالصُّورةِ ما يعم الخارجيَّة والذهنية، وبالحصول: الحصول ؛ سواء كان بنفسِهِ أو بمثالِهِ، وبالمغايرة المستفادةِ مِنَ الظرفيَّةِ أعمُّ مِنَ الذَّاتِيَّةِ والاعتباريَّةِ، وبلفظة «في الجارَّةِ معنى «عند»، وهذهِ كُلُّهَا تكلُّفات، وأَمَّا إِنْ جَعَلَ التَّعرِيفَ لِلعِلْمِ الحصوليّ لأنَّ الكلامَ هُنَا في تعريفِ العِلْمِ الَّذي يقعُ بِهِ الكَشبُ والعِلْمُ المكتَسَبُ ؛ لم يحتج إلى هذه التَّأويلات، والمراد بالعقل: قوَّةُ تدركُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ بِنَفْسِهَا ، والمحسوسات بالواسطة وبصورةِ الشَّيء ما يكونُ الةٌ لامتيازه؛ سواء كان نفس ماهية الشَّيء أو شَبَحاً له، بناءً على ما تقدَّمَ مِنَ الخِلَافِ في أنَّ الحاصِلَ في الذهن الأشياءُ أنفسها أو أشباحُها وأمثالها ، والظرفية باقية على معناها الحقيقي، ولَيْسَتْ بمعنى عندَ كَمَا هو على التَّأويل الأوَّلِ ، وقد سبق لكَ كَلامٌ يتعلَّقُ بِمَا هنا فَضَمَّهُ إِلَيهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ الاكْتِسَابُ بِالنَّظَرِ هذا مبني على ما أسلفه، وقد تقدَّمَ لكَ أَنَّ الصَّواب عودُ الضَّميرِ على النَّظَرِ ، فَلْيَكُنْ هُنَا كذلِكَ، 
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : من قبيل (الفعل) وقد مرَّ أنَّه مشهور النَّقل عن الإمام الرازي ومن تبعه من المتأخرين ولم يقل به أحد من المتقدمين. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Introduction of the author: (And it may occur in it); that is, in that acquisition. al-Dasuqi: Know that if the construct of 'hudul' is made as a descriptive attribution to the described, i.e., the image of the thing obtained in the mind, whether that image is certain or conjectured, whether it corresponds to reality or not, whether it is conceptual or assenting, it proceeds according to the view that knowledge is of the category of quality (kayf) – which is the preponderant view. If the construct is made real (haqiqiyya) – which is the apparent meaning of his statement – then if the acquisition of the image is interpreted as its impression (intiqash) in the mind, it proceeds according to the view that knowledge is a passion (infi‘al); if interpreted as the acquisition of the image in the mind, it proceeds according to the view that it is of the category of action (fi‘l); if interpreted as the relation (nisba) obtaining between the acquirer and the acquired, it proceeds according to the view that knowledge is of the category of relation (idafa). (His statement: al-iktisab) means acquisition through reasoning (nazar); i.e., knowledge acquired through it. al-Attar: (The intellect is) the very perceiver and other; so by 'aql is meant the abstract substance expressed as the rational soul (nafs natiqa) in their terminology; by 'surah' is meant what includes both external and mental images; by 'hudul' is meant acquisition, whether by itself or by its likeness; the difference derived from the preposition 'fi' is more general than essential (dhatiyya) and conventional (i‘tibariyya); and the particle 'fi' carries the meaning of 'at'. All these are distortions. But if the definition is made for acquired knowledge because the discussion here is about defining the knowledge by which acquisition occurs and the acquired knowledge, then these interpretations are not needed. The meaning of 'aql is a faculty that perceives universals by itself and sensibles through a medium; and by 'surah of the thing' is meant what is a tool for its discernment, whether it is the very essence of the thing or a likeness (shabah) of it, based on the previous disagreement as to whether what occurs in the mind is the things themselves or their likenesses and images. The preposition 'fi' remains in its real meaning and is not in the sense of 'at' as in the first interpretation. There is previous discussion related to this, so join it to this. (His statement: i.e., acquisition through reasoning) is based on what he previously stated; you have already learned that the correct view is that the pronoun refers to 'nazar', so let it be the same here. (Footnote) (His statement: of the category of action) – it has been mentioned that it is a well-known attribution to Imam al-Razi and those among the later scholars who followed him, and none of the earlier scholars said it. End quote. al-Sharqawi.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of acquired knowledge ('ilm al-husuli) as 'the acquisition of the image of the thing in the mind'. The commentator al-Dasuqi first explains that the phrase 'idafat hudul' can be interpreted in two ways: as a descriptive attribution (idafat al-sifa li'l-mawsuf) meaning the image itself is the quality, which aligns with the view that knowledge is a quality (kayf) – the preponderant view. Alternatively, as a real attribution (idafa haqiqiyya), which then splits into three views depending on how 'hudul al-sura' is understood: as impression (intiqash) -> knowledge is a passion; as acquisition (tahsil) -> knowledge is an action; as relation (nisba) -> knowledge is a relation. Al-Attar then critiques these interpretations, arguing that they are forced (takallufat) and unnecessary. He suggests that if the definition is meant for acquired knowledge specifically, it is simpler: the 'aql is the faculty that perceives universals directly and sensibles indirectly, and the image is a tool for discernment – either the essence itself or a likeness. The issue of whether images are the things themselves or their likenesses remains a previous disagreement. This commentary shows the depth of philosophical debate on the nature of knowledge. For example, knowledge that prayer is obligatory is a conceptual knowledge that corresponds to a real state; the error, if any, lies in the judgment, not in the concept itself.

Key Terms
إضافة حصول
الكيف
الانفعال
الفعل
الإضافة

B) Acquisition (tahsil)

Key Takeaway
Al-Dasuqi shows that the same definition of knowledge can be read in four ways, each implying a different ontological category for knowledge.

Al-Attar's Critique of Forced Interpretations
Al-Attar's critique and simplification of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Attar's critique of forced interpretations and reconstruct his simpler definition of acquired knowledge.

Core Idea
Forced interpretations arise when the definition is applied generally; restricting it to acquired knowledge yields clarity.

Al-Attar enters the discussion to challenge the elaborate interpretations al-Dasuqi offered. He argues that they are unnecessary distortions (takallufat) and that a straightforward reading suffices if we understand the definition as specific to acquired knowledge, not a general definition of all knowledge.

Guided Path
1
List the forced interpretations al-Attar identifies
Al-Attar enumerates several forced moves: interpreting 'aql as the abstract substance, 'surah' as including external and mental, 'hudul' as covering self and likeness, 'fi' as meaning 'at', and the difference as general. He calls these 'takallufat' (distortions).
2
Understand the reason for the forced interpretations
These distortions arise because commentators tried to make the definition work for all knowledge, including presential knowledge. Al-Attar suggests that the definition is only for acquired knowledge, so the wording 'in the mind' is literal and unproblematic.
3
Grasp al-Attar's simpler definitions of key terms
He defines 'aql as the faculty that perceives universals directly and sensibles indirectly; 'surah' as a tool for discernment, either the essence itself or a likeness; and 'fi' retains its real spatial sense, not metaphorical.
4
Recognize the unresolved disagreement
Al-Attar notes that the issue of whether the mind contains things themselves or their likenesses remains an earlier dispute. This affects whether 'surah' refers to the essence or a likeness.

Critiquing the Dependency of Starting a Science
Author critiques the necessity of knowing definition and purpose before study.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the logical strength of the claim that studying a science depends on knowing its definition and purpose, using the author's counterexample.

Core Idea
Not all learning requires explicit prior definitions; many succeed without them.

In the Islamic tradition, introductory works often state that one must know a science's definition and purpose before studying it. Here, the author questions this assumption, arguing that the word 'dependent' implies impossibility of starting without them, which is refuted by everyday experience. Let's examine his argument and its implications for how we approach learning Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find in the passage the standard position: that commencing a science depends on prior knowledge of its definition and purpose. Note the precise Arabic phrase: 'الشُّرُوعُ فِي مَسَائِل الْعِلْمِ مَوْقُوفًا عَلَى...'
The claim is found in the matn being commented on.
2
Understand the meaning of 'dependency' (tawaqquf)
The author defines tawaqquf as 'impossibility without' – i.e., A cannot happen without B. This is a strong condition. The question is: does knowing the definition and purpose meet this condition?
Contrast with a weaker condition like 'recommended' or 'helpful'.
3
Find the author's objection
The author states that nothing mentioned indicates dependence in this strong sense. He then gives a counterexample: many students learn literary sciences like grammar (النَّحْو) while being heedless of their definition and purpose.
The word 'النُّهولِ' (heedlessness/ignorance) is key.
4
Evaluate the force of the counterexample
The counterexample shows that learning is possible without explicit prior definition. This refutes the necessity claim. However, it does not show that definition and purpose are useless – only that they are not necessary conditions.
The critique is precise: it targets the modal strength, not the usefulness.
5
Apply to other Islamic sciences
Consider studying 'Usul al-Fiqh' or 'Tafsir'. Does a student truly need to know the formal definition before understanding its first issue? The author suggests they can begin without it.
This aligns with how many actually learn: gradually through examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الشُّرُوعُ فِي مَسَائِل الْعِلْمِ مَوْقُوفًا عَلَى:
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الشُّرُوعُ ...إلخ) هذا إيفاءٌ بِمَا وَعَدَ بِهِ سابقاً بقوله : وستعرفُ وجة توقف الشروع ... إلخ، وهذا ما ذَكَرَهُ القَومُ، وقَدِ اعترض عليهم المصنِّفُ في شَرْحِ الأصلِ قائِلاً : إِنَّ المفهوم مِن تَوقُفِ الشُروعِ على الشَّيء أَنَّهُ لا يُمكِنُ الشُّروع بِدونِهِ، وظاهرُ أنَّ شيئاً مِمَّا ذُكِرَ لا يدلُّ على التَّوقُفِ بِهَذا المعنى، أَلا ترى أنَّ كثيراً مِنَ الطَّلبة يُحَصِّلُ كثيراً من العلوم الأدبيَّةِ كالنَّحْوِ وَغيرِهِ مَعَ النُّهولِ(۱) عَن رسمِهَا
And the commencement of the study of the issues of a science is dependent on: [Dassuqi] (His saying: 'And the commencement... etc.') This is a fulfillment of what he promised earlier in his saying: 'And you will know the aspect of the dependence of the commencement... etc.', and this is what the people (the scholars) mentioned. And the author (al-musannif) objected to them in the commentary on the original text, saying: 'The understanding from the dependence of the commencement on something is that commencement is not possible without it, and it is apparent that nothing of what was mentioned indicates dependence in this sense. Do you not see that many students acquire many literary sciences like grammar and others while being heedless of their definition and purpose?'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the standard view that studying a science requires prior knowledge of its definition (hadd) and purpose (ghaya). The author, al-musannif, is objecting to this claim. Dassuqi notes that the promise made earlier is being fulfilled. The standard position is that one must first know the definition and purpose of a science before starting its study. The author objects that the word 'dependent on' (mawquf 'ala) implies impossibility without it, but many students successfully learn sciences like grammar without explicitly knowing the formal definition and purpose. So the claim of dependence is too strong. This critiques a standard introductory pedagogical principle and shows a nuanced understanding of how learning actually occurs. The objection is epistemological: it challenges the necessity of certain conditions for acquiring knowledge.

الشُّرُوع
التَّوَقُّف
النُّهول

Mental Metaphor in Atttributing Infallibility to Logic
Commentary on the metaphorical attribution of infallibility to logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a text uses mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli) and distinguish the real agent from the apparent agent in attributions of causality in Islamic logic and theology.

Core Idea
Infallibility is attributed to logic metaphorically; the real cause is observance of its rules.

The commentator explains a phrase from the matn that calls logic an infallible guide. This seems like praise, but it requires careful theological and logical unpacking. The passage teaches us about mental metaphor and the principle that apparent causation is attributed to the observable cause, while ultimate causation belongs to Allah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for reading Islamic texts that use rhetorical emphasis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphorical attribution
Look at the phrase 'logic is infallible.' The commentator says this is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli). What is being attributed to what?
2
Determine the real agent
The commentator explains that the real agent of infallibility is the observance (mura'a) of logic's rules. Why is the attribution shifted?
3
Connect to the theological principle
The commentator cites al-Sakkaki: an event whose agent is apparent is attributed to that agent, otherwise to Allah. How does this principle apply here?
4
Evaluate the rhetorical purpose
The emphasis and exaggeration encourage learning and teaching logic. What is the pedagogical benefit of this metaphorical language?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه مِنَ التَّأكيد والمبالَغَةِ ما لا يخفى . وإِنَّما كانَ الشُّروعُ في مسائل العِلْم موقوفاً على: الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَفِيهِ ) أي : في هذا الإطلاق المجازي. (قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ التَأْكِيْدِ) أي: لأنَّ إسناد العصمةِ لِلمنطق فيه إشارة إلى الحثّ على تعلمه وتعليمِهِ ومُلاحظته . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْمُبَالَغَةِ) أي : من حيثُ إِنَّهُ أسند العصمة إليه، مع أنَّ حقها أن تسند لمراعاته. العطار فَهوَ مجاز عقلي، والإسنادُ الحقيقي إنَّما هو للمراعاة ، وقَوْلُ المحنِّي : التَّحقيقُ أَنَّ العَاصِمَ هو الله ؛ إنْ أرادَ أَنَّ العَاصِمَ هو الله بالنِّسبة للحقيقة؛ أي: الواقع؛ فَجَميعُ الأفعالِ كُلُّها مَخلوقةٌ لهُ سبحانَهُ ، فهوَ فاعِلٌ في الحقيقة؛ أي: بالنسبة للواقع، وهذا غير منظور إليه، بل المنظورُ إليه في الإسنادِ ؛ الفاعِلُ الظَّاهِرُ ، كما قالَ الشَّكَّاكِيُّ : إنَّ الحدثَ الَّذي يظهرُ فاعلُهُ يُنسَبُ إليه، والذي لا يظهرُ ؛ يُنسَبُ إلى ذاتِهِ تعالى .ا.هـ. ولا خفاءَ أَنَّ الفَاعِلَ الظَّاهري للعصمةِ هو المراعاة، ولو التفثنا للواقع؛ لانسدَّ بَابُ الحقيقة العقلية.
And in it there is emphasis and exaggeration that is not hidden. And indeed, starting into the issues of a science is dependent upon: al-Dasuqi (his statement: 'And in it') means: in this metaphorical usage. (His statement: 'of emphasis') means: because attributing infallibility to logic contains an indication to encourage learning it, teaching it, and observing it. (His statement: 'and exaggeration') means: from the perspective that he attributed infallibility to it, while its right is to be attributed to its observance. al-‘Attar: So it is a mental metaphor, and the real attribution is only to observance. And the statement of al-Muhanni: 'The reality is that the Infallible is Allah; if he intended that the Infallible is Allah with respect to reality, i.e., actuality, then all acts are created by Him—He is the true agent; but this is not considered, rather what is considered in attribution is the apparent agent, as al-Shakkaki said: An event whose agent is apparent is attributed to it, and that which is not apparent is attributed to His Essence, may He be exalted.' End quote. And it is not hidden that the apparent agent of infallibility is observance, and if we turned to reality, the door of mental truth would be closed.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's phrase 'And in it there is emphasis and exaggeration that is not hidden.' The commentator explains that the attribution of infallibility to logic is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli). The real attributant should be the observance (mura'a) of logic's rules, not logic itself. The commentator then cites al-Muhanni and al-Sakkaki to distinguish between the true agent (Allah) and the apparent agent (the observable cause). The point is that calling logic 'infallible' is a rhetorical emphasis to encourage its study, but in reality, infallibility comes from Allah through the observance of logical principles.

The Condition of Middle Term Repetition for Syllogistic Productivity
Author critiques sharh's wording about the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of middle term repetition in a categorical syllogism and evaluate alternative phrasings based on that condition.

Core Idea
The middle term must be repeated; even one word can break a syllogism.

In this passage, Dassuqi critiques the commentator's wording for defining the subject of logic. He argues that saying 'in the science' instead of 'in logic' fails to repeat the middle term, making the syllogism unproductive. Understanding this condition is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Find the criticized phrase
Identify the phrase that Dassuqi objects to: the sharh uses 'fi al-'ilm' (in the science). Dassuqi says it should be 'fi al-mantiq' (in logic).
2
Understand the reason
Dassuqi explains that 'in logic' ensures the middle term is repeated, which is necessary for a syllogism to be productive. Without repetition, no conclusion follows.
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises and is eliminated in the conclusion.
3
Consider the exception
Dassuqi allows 'al-'ilm' if the definite article refers to a previously mentioned science (al-'ahd al-dhikri), in which case the middle term is implicitly repeated.
This is a technical nuance about reference.
4
Apply the principle
If the middle term is not explicitly repeated, the syllogism fails. For example, 'Every changing thing is temporal; every temporal thing has a cause' works if 'temporal' is repeated.
This rule applies to all categorical syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكانَ الأَولى لِلشَّارحِ أنْ يَقُولَ: في المَنطِقِ، بَدَلَ قَوْلِهِ: في العِلْمِ؛ لأجْلِ أن يَكُونَ الحَدُّ الوَسَطُ مُكَرَّراً، فَيَنْتُجَ القِيَاسُ؛ إذْ مَا ذَكَرَهُ غَيْرُ مُنتِجٍ لِعَدَمِ تَكَرُّرِ الحَدِّ الأَوْسَطِ، إلَّا أنْ تَجعَلَ «أل» في «العِلْمِ» لِلعَهْدِ الذِّكْرِيِّ، فَتَأمَّل. الطبيعي، أو يُثَبتُ لَهُ أيْ: لِلنَّوْعِ مَا يَعْرِضُهُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ؛ بِشَرْطِ أن لَا يَتَجاوَزَ في العُمُومِ عَنْ مَوْضُوعِ العِلْمِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ نَاقِدُ المُحَصَّلِ، كَقَوْلِ الفُقَهَاءِ: كُلُّ مُسْكِرٍ حَرامٌ؛ فإنَّ مَوْضُوعَ عِلْمِ الفِقْهِ إنَّمَا هو أفْعَالُ المُكَلَّفِينَ، وشُرْبُ المُسْكِرِ نَوْعٌ مِنْهَا، أُثبِتَ لَهُ الحُرْمَةُ اللَّاحِقَةُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ مِنْهُ هُوَ كَوْنُهُ مَنْهِيَّاً عَنْهُ، وإنَّمَا اشْتُرِطَ هذا الشَّرطُ لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ المَحْمُولُ بالنِّسْبَةِ إلى مَوْضُوعِ العِلْمِ مِنَ الأَعْرَاضِ الغَرِيبَةِ، أو تَجعَلَ عَرَضُهُ الذَّاتِيُّ أو نَوْعُهُ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ وتُثبِتَ لَهُ العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ لَهُ أو لِمَا لَحِقَهُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ بِالشَّرطِ المَذكُورِ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ مُتَحَرِّكٍ بِحَرَكَتَيْنِ مُسْتَقِيمَتَيْنِ لَا وأنْ يَسْكُنَ بَيْنَهُمَا، فَقَوْلُهُم: مَا يَبْحَثُ عَنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ؛ مُجمَلٌ، مُفَصَّلُهُ مَا ذَكَرْنَاهُ. ا.ه. أيْ: وَلَيْسَ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى مَا يُفهَمُ مِنْهُ الإجْمَالُ بِأنْ يَكُونَ المَعْنَى مَا يَبْحَثُ عَنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ في الجُمْلَةِ، ثُمَّ إنَّ قَوْلَهُ: أو يُجعَلُ عَرَضُهُ الذَّاتِيُّ أو نَوْعُهُ ... إلخ؛ كَلامٌ مُوجَزٌ يَحْتَوِي عَلَى أرْبَعِ صُوَرٍ؛ الأُولَى: أنْ يُجعَلَ عَرَضُهُ الذَّاتِيُّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ حَرَكَةٍ تَنْطَبِقُ عَلَى الزَّمَانِ، والثَّانِيَةُ: أنْ يُجعَلَ العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ مَا يَلْحَقُهُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ حَرَكَةٍ تَنْقَسِمُ إلى غَيْرِ النِّهَايَةِ، والثَّالِثَةُ: أنْ يُجعَلَ نَوْعُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتِيِّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ عَرَضُ ذَاتِيٌّ لَهُ، ومِثَالُهُ: مَا ذَكَرَهُ مِنَ المِثَالِ؛ فإنَّ المُتَحَرِّكَ بِالحَرَكَتَيْنِ المُسْتَقِيمَتَيْنِ نَوْعُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتِيِّ، والسُّكُونَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَرَضُ ذَاتِيٌّ لَهُ، والرَّابِعَةُ: أنْ يُجعَلَ نَوْعُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتِيِّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ مَا يَلْحَقُهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ الأَمْرِ الأَعَمِّ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ حَرَكَةٍ بَطِيئَةٍ لَا يَتَخَلَّلُ السُّكُونُ بَيْنَهَا، قَالَ الفَاضِلُ
It would have been better for the commentator to say: 'in logic', instead of his saying: 'in the science'; so that the middle term be repeated, and thus the syllogism produce a result; for what he mentioned does not produce a result due to the lack of repetition of the middle term, unless you take 'al' in 'al-'ilm' as referring to a previously mentioned [science] (al-'ahd al-dhikri), so ponder. The natural [science], or it is established for it, i.e., for the species, what occurs to it due to a more general matter, on condition that it does not exceed in generality the subject of the science, as the critic of al-Muhassal stated, like the saying of the jurists: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'; for the subject of fiqh is only the acts of the legally responsible, and drinking an intoxicant is a type of them, and the prohibition that attaches due to a more general matter is established for it, which is its being prohibited. And this condition is stipulated only so that the predicate relative to the subject of the science not be among the accidental (gharib) attributes. Or you make its essential attribute or its type the subject of the question and establish for it the essential attribute belonging to it or to what attaches to it due to a more general matter under the mentioned condition, like their saying: 'Every moving thing with two rectilinear motions such that it does not rest between them.' So their saying: 'What investigates its essential attributes' is a summary; its detailed explanation is what we have mentioned. End quote. That is, its meaning is not according to what is understood from the summary, that the meaning is 'what investigates its essential attributes in general'. Then his saying: 'Or you make its essential attribute or its type... etc.' is concise speech containing four forms: The first: that you make its essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it the essential attribute, like their saying: 'Every motion corresponds to time.' The second: that you make the essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it what attaches to it due to a more general matter, like their saying: 'Every motion is divisible ad infinitum.' The third: that you make the type of the essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it an essential attribute belonging to it, and its example is what he mentioned of the example; for the thing moving with two rectilinear motions is a type of the essential attribute, and rest between them is an essential attribute for it. The fourth: that you make the type of the essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it what attaches to it by means of a more general matter, like their saying: 'Every slow motion, rest does not intervene between it.' The virtuous [scholar] said:
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by Dassuqi on the sharh of the Tadhheeb. It begins with an objection to the commentator's use of 'fi al-'ilm' (in the science) instead of 'fi al-mantiq' (in logic). The reason given is that the middle term must be repeated in a productive syllogism; by specifying 'logic' the middle term becomes explicit. However, an exception is made if 'al-'ilm' refers by prior mention to a specific science. The discussion then shifts to defining the condition for essential attributes: they must not exceed the generality of the subject of the science. An example from fiqh is given: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' — the subject of fiqh is actions of the mukallaf, and drinking intoxicant is a type of action; the predicate 'forbidden' attaches due to a more general attribute (being prohibited). If this condition were not met, the predicate would be an accidental attribute (gharib). The author then elaborates on the statement 'what investigates its essential attributes' by offering a detailed breakdown into four forms. The first form: making the essential attribute itself the subject and establishing its essential attribute (e.g., motion corresponds to time). The second: making the essential attribute the subject and establishing what attaches to it via a more general attribute (e.g., motion is divisible). The third: making the type of the essential attribute the subject and establishing an essential attribute for that type (e.g., motion with two rectilinear motions has rest between them as an essential attribute). The fourth: making the type of the essential attribute the subject and establishing what attaches to it via a more general attribute (e.g., slow motion lacks intervening rest). These distinctions ensure that the investigation covers only essential attributes, avoiding accidental ones that would invalidate the scientific nature of the inquiry.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
العهد الذكري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dassuqi, what is the main problem with using 'fi al-'ilm' instead of 'fi al-mantiq'?
A) It changes the subject from logic to science.
B) It prevents the middle term from being repeated, making the syllogism invalid.
C) It makes the definition too broad.
D) It introduces an ambiguity about which science is meant.

Key Takeaway
The middle term must be repeated in a categorical syllogism for it to be productive; even the choice of a definite article can affect validity.

The Four Forms of Essential Attributes in Scientific Questions
Author explains four forms of essential attributes with conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four forms of essential attributes as they appear in the subject of a scientific question and apply the condition that the attribute must not exceed the subject's generality.

Core Idea
Essential attributes (al-'arad al-dhati) are restricted to the subject's scope; they come in four forms: subject as essential attribute or its type, and predicate as essential or through a wider attribute.

This passage unpacks the phrase 'what investigates its essential attributes' into four precise forms. It also emphasizes the condition that the essential attribute must not exceed the subject of the science in generality, ensuring it is not an accidental (gharib) attribute. Examples from fiqh and natural science illustrate each form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
The essential attribute must not be more general than the subject of the science. If it exceeds, it becomes a 'arad gharib (accidental).
This ensures the investigation stays within the science's domain.
2
Identify the four forms
The forms are combinations of whether the subject of the question is the essential attribute itself or its type, and whether the predicate is the essential attribute or something that attaches via a more general matter.
The author enumerates four distinct logical structures.
3
Analyze the first form
Subject = essential attribute; predicate = essential attribute. Example: 'Every motion corresponds to time.' Here 'corresponds to time' is an essential attribute of motion, without mediation.
4
Analyze the second form
Subject = essential attribute; predicate = what attaches via a more general matter. Example: 'Every motion is divisible ad infinitum.' The more general matter is magnitude, making divisibility an essential accident through a wider concept.
5
Analyze the third and fourth forms
Third: Subject = type of essential attribute; predicate = essential attribute. Example: 'Every moving thing with two rectilinear motions does not rest between them.' Fourth: Subject = type of essential attribute; predicate = via more general matter. Example: 'Every slow motion lacks intervening rest.'
These forms cover all possibilities for essential attributes in scientific questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكانَ الأَولى لِلشَّارحِ أنْ يَقُولَ: في المَنطِقِ، بَدَلَ قَوْلِهِ: في العِلْمِ؛ لأجْلِ أن يَكُونَ الحَدُّ الوَسَطُ مُكَرَّراً، فَيَنْتُجَ القِيَاسُ؛ إذْ مَا ذَكَرَهُ غَيْرُ مُنتِجٍ لِعَدَمِ تَكَرُّرِ الحَدِّ الأَوْسَطِ، إلَّا أنْ تَجعَلَ «أل» في «العِلْمِ» لِلعَهْدِ الذِّكْرِيِّ، فَتَأمَّل. الطبيعي، أو يُثَبتُ لَهُ أيْ: لِلنَّوْعِ مَا يَعْرِضُهُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ؛ بِشَرْطِ أن لَا يَتَجاوَزَ في العُمُومِ عَنْ مَوْضُوعِ العِلْمِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ نَاقِدُ المُحَصَّلِ، كَقَوْلِ الفُقَهَاءِ: كُلُّ مُسْكِرٍ حَرامٌ؛ فإنَّ مَوْضُوعَ عِلْمِ الفِقْهِ إنَّمَا هو أفْعَالُ المُكَلَّفِينَ، وشُرْبُ المُسْكِرِ نَوْعٌ مِنْهَا، أُثبِتَ لَهُ الحُرْمَةُ اللَّاحِقَةُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ مِنْهُ هُوَ كَوْنُهُ مَنْهِيَّاً عَنْهُ، وإنَّمَا اشْتُرِطَ هذا الشَّرطُ لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ المَحْمُولُ بالنِّسْبَةِ إلى مَوْضُوعِ العِلْمِ مِنَ الأَعْرَاضِ الغَرِيبَةِ، أو تَجعَلَ عَرَضُهُ الذَّاتِيُّ أو نَوْعُهُ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ وتُثبِتَ لَهُ العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ لَهُ أو لِمَا لَحِقَهُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ بِالشَّرطِ المَذكُورِ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ مُتَحَرِّكٍ بِحَرَكَتَيْنِ مُسْتَقِيمَتَيْنِ لَا وأنْ يَسْكُنَ بَيْنَهُمَا، فَقَوْلُهُم: مَا يَبْحَثُ عَنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ؛ مُجمَلٌ، مُفَصَّلُهُ مَا ذَكَرْنَاهُ. ا.ه. أيْ: وَلَيْسَ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى مَا يُفهَمُ مِنْهُ الإجْمَالُ بِأنْ يَكُونَ المَعْنَى مَا يَبْحَثُ عَنْ أَعْرَاضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ في الجُمْلَةِ، ثُمَّ إنَّ قَوْلَهُ: أو يُجعَلُ عَرَضُهُ الذَّاتِيُّ أو نَوْعُهُ ... إلخ؛ كَلامٌ مُوجَزٌ يَحْتَوِي عَلَى أرْبَعِ صُوَرٍ؛ الأُولَى: أنْ يُجعَلَ عَرَضُهُ الذَّاتِيُّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ حَرَكَةٍ تَنْطَبِقُ عَلَى الزَّمَانِ، والثَّانِيَةُ: أنْ يُجعَلَ العَرَضُ الذَّاتِيُّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ مَا يَلْحَقُهُ لِأمْرٍ أَعَمَّ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ حَرَكَةٍ تَنْقَسِمُ إلى غَيْرِ النِّهَايَةِ، والثَّالِثَةُ: أنْ يُجعَلَ نَوْعُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتِيِّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ عَرَضُ ذَاتِيٌّ لَهُ، ومِثَالُهُ: مَا ذَكَرَهُ مِنَ المِثَالِ؛ فإنَّ المُتَحَرِّكَ بِالحَرَكَتَيْنِ المُسْتَقِيمَتَيْنِ نَوْعُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتِيِّ، والسُّكُونَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَرَضُ ذَاتِيٌّ لَهُ، والرَّابِعَةُ: أنْ يُجعَلَ نَوْعُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتِيِّ مَوْضُوعَ المَسْألَةِ ويُثبَتَ لَهُ مَا يَلْحَقُهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ الأَمْرِ الأَعَمِّ كَقَوْلِهِم: كُلُّ حَرَكَةٍ بَطِيئَةٍ لَا يَتَخَلَّلُ السُّكُونُ بَيْنَهَا، قَالَ الفَاضِلُ
It would have been better for the commentator to say: 'in logic', instead of his saying: 'in the science'; so that the middle term be repeated, and thus the syllogism produce a result; for what he mentioned does not produce a result due to the lack of repetition of the middle term, unless you take 'al' in 'al-'ilm' as referring to a previously mentioned [science] (al-'ahd al-dhikri), so ponder. The natural [science], or it is established for it, i.e., for the species, what occurs to it due to a more general matter, on condition that it does not exceed in generality the subject of the science, as the critic of al-Muhassal stated, like the saying of the jurists: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'; for the subject of fiqh is only the acts of the legally responsible, and drinking an intoxicant is a type of them, and the prohibition that attaches due to a more general matter is established for it, which is its being prohibited. And this condition is stipulated only so that the predicate relative to the subject of the science not be among the accidental (gharib) attributes. Or you make its essential attribute or its type the subject of the question and establish for it the essential attribute belonging to it or to what attaches to it due to a more general matter under the mentioned condition, like their saying: 'Every moving thing with two rectilinear motions such that it does not rest between them.' So their saying: 'What investigates its essential attributes' is a summary; its detailed explanation is what we have mentioned. End quote. That is, its meaning is not according to what is understood from the summary, that the meaning is 'what investigates its essential attributes in general'. Then his saying: 'Or you make its essential attribute or its type... etc.' is concise speech containing four forms: The first: that you make its essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it the essential attribute, like their saying: 'Every motion corresponds to time.' The second: that you make the essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it what attaches to it due to a more general matter, like their saying: 'Every motion is divisible ad infinitum.' The third: that you make the type of the essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it an essential attribute belonging to it, and its example is what he mentioned of the example; for the thing moving with two rectilinear motions is a type of the essential attribute, and rest between them is an essential attribute for it. The fourth: that you make the type of the essential attribute the subject of the question and establish for it what attaches to it by means of a more general matter, like their saying: 'Every slow motion, rest does not intervene between it.' The virtuous [scholar] said:
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by Dassuqi on the sharh of the Tadhheeb. It begins with an objection to the commentator's use of 'fi al-'ilm' (in the science) instead of 'fi al-mantiq' (in logic). The reason given is that the middle term must be repeated in a productive syllogism; by specifying 'logic' the middle term becomes explicit. However, an exception is made if 'al-'ilm' refers by prior mention to a specific science. The discussion then shifts to defining the condition for essential attributes: they must not exceed the generality of the subject of the science. An example from fiqh is given: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' — the subject of fiqh is actions of the mukallaf, and drinking intoxicant is a type of action; the predicate 'forbidden' attaches due to a more general attribute (being prohibited). If this condition were not met, the predicate would be an accidental attribute (gharib). The author then elaborates on the statement 'what investigates its essential attributes' by offering a detailed breakdown into four forms. The first form: making the essential attribute itself the subject and establishing its essential attribute (e.g., motion corresponds to time). The second: making the essential attribute the subject and establishing what attaches to it via a more general attribute (e.g., motion is divisible). The third: making the type of the essential attribute the subject and establishing an essential attribute for that type (e.g., motion with two rectilinear motions has rest between them as an essential attribute). The fourth: making the type of the essential attribute the subject and establishing what attaches to it via a more general attribute (e.g., slow motion lacks intervening rest). These distinctions ensure that the investigation covers only essential attributes, avoiding accidental ones that would invalidate the scientific nature of the inquiry.

Key Terms
العرض الذاتي
العارض الغريب
العهد الذكري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' from fiqh, which form of essential attributes does Dassuqi say it belongs to?
A) First form: essential attribute subject, essential predicate
B) Second form: essential attribute subject, predicate via more general matter
C) Third form: type of essential attribute subject, essential predicate
D) Fourth form: type of essential attribute subject, predicate via more general matter

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes in a science are strictly limited to the subject's scope and appear in four distinct forms depending on whether the subject is the essential attribute or its type, and whether the predicate is direct or mediated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'Every moving thing is changing.' Identify which of the four forms this example fits, and justify by stating the subject of the question, the predicate, and whether the predicate is a direct essential attribute or via a more general matter.

The Independence of Inclusion and Implication in Signification
Dusuqi and Attar discuss the logical independence of inclusion and implication in signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between taḍammun and iltizām and identify scenarios where each can exist without the other.

Core Idea
Inclusion and implication are logically independent: one can exist without the other.

In this lesson, we explore the commentary of Dusuqi and Attar on the relationship between two types of signification: taḍammun (inclusion) and iltizām (implication). They argue for their logical independence, challenging the assumption that inclusion always implies implication and vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two theses
The opening statement presents two claims: (1) inclusion can occur without implication, and (2) implication can occur without inclusion. Note that these are presented as possibilities.
2
Understand the justification for the first thesis
For inclusion without implication: compound meanings may have no mental implication. Their parts are included, but no necessary further meaning follows.
Example: a compound term like 'human' includes 'animal' and 'rational', but that inclusion does not necessarily imply something external like 'mortality' in every context.
3
Understand the justification for the second thesis
For implication without inclusion: simple meanings can have a concomitant that is not part of their essence. The text gives the example of a point implying indivisibility.
The point is simple; its indivisibility is a necessary concomitant (lāzim), not a part of its meaning.
4
Examine Dusuqi's critique of Fakhr al-Razi
Fakhr al-Razi claimed conformance (muṭābaqa) always entails implication because every word implies otherness. Dusuqi responds that this otherness is a 'clear implication in the more general sense' (lāzim bayyin bil-ma‘nā al-a‘amm), which is not counted in the technical definition of iltizām.
This shows the importance of distinguishing between different types of implication.
5
Appreciate Attar's practical focus
Attar adds that the relations between taḍammun and iltizām are uncertain; thus logicians focus on the primary relation (muṭābaqa) with the other two, since they are subsidiary.
This explains why the logical discussion emphasizes muṭābaqa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا الأَوَّلُ : فَلِجواز أن يكون من المعاني المركبة ما لا يكونُ لازم ذهني، فهناك تضمن بدونِ الالتزام.
وأمَّا الثّاني: فَلِجواز أن يكون للمعنى البسيط لازم ذهني، فهناك الالتزام بدونِ التَّضمن .
الدسوقي
مخلوق، وهو إمَّا عَرَضٌ ؛ ويلزمُهُ القيام بالغير، وإمَّا جوهرٌ ؛ ويلزمه التحيز، أو أمر اعتباري؛ ويلزمُهُ أَنَّهُ مُغاير لغيرِهِ مِنَ الأشياء، وهذا بناءً على أنَّ اللُّزوم الأعم معتبر، وإلا ؛ فقد لا يكون لشيء لازم أخص .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَلِجَوَازِ ... إلخ) عبر بالجواز إشارة إلى أنَّ هذا أمر ممكن عقلاً، وإن لم يوجد له مثال ؛ لأنَّ مجرَّدَ الإمكان لا يستلزم الوجود.
وقولُ الفخرِ: لا يتأتّى وجود المطابقيَّة بدون الالتزاميَّة؛ لأنَّ معنى كلِّ لفظ يلزمه المغايرة لغيره، فَفِيه : أنَّ المغايرة المذكورة لازمٌ بيّن بالمعنى الأعم، وهو
غيرُ مُعتبر في دلالة الالتزام ، نعم على القول باعتباره يتمُ مَا قَالَهُ.
العطار
والالتزامِ لِلآخرِ غيرُ مَعلوم وجوداً وعدماً، كَمَا أنَّ استلزام المطابقة للالتزام غيرُ معلوم ؛ فَالأَوْلى توجيه الاقتصار على بيانِ حال المطابقةِ مَعَ التَّضَمُّنِ والالتزامِ مِنَ اللزوم، وعدم الاستلزام وتركُ التَّعرُّض لحالِ أحدهما معَ الآخَرِ لعدم الاهتمامِ بشأنهما لفرعيَّتِهما ؛ بِخِلافِ المطابقة لأصالتها أو لكونهما مهجورين في الجملة، كما اشتهر فيما بينَهُم بِخِلافِها ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَلِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) هذا جواز عقلاً؛ بناءً على مَا فَرَّرْنَاهُ سابقاً، والَّذي بعدَهُ وُقوعيٌّ، فإنَّ النُّقطة معنّى بسيط، وعدم الانقسام خارج عن ماهيتها، وإلا ؛ كانت أمراً عدميًّا وَلَا لازم بيّن لها بالمعنى الأخصّ، وَلِذَا أَخذُوهُ في تعريفها وكَذَا كونها ذاتَ وضع، ويُقَالُ مثلُ ذلكَ في الوحدة وسائر البسائط، وَلِذلكَ قَالُوا : إِنَّ تعاريفها رُسومٌ لا حدود؛ لعدم التركيب فيها .
As for the first: because it is possible that among compound meanings there may be that which has no mental implication, so there is inclusion without implication. And as for the second: because it is possible that a simple meaning has a mental implication, so there is implication without inclusion. Dusuqi: Created, and it is either an accident, so it requires inherence in another, or a substance, so it requires occupying space, or a consideration, so it requires being other than others. And this is based on the view that the more general implication is considered; otherwise, some things may have no specific implication. (His statement: 'For the possibility... etc.') He used 'possibility' to indicate that this is rationally possible, even if no example exists, because mere possibility does not entail existence. And the statement of Fakhr: 'Conformance cannot exist without implication, because the meaning of every word entails its being other than others.' In it: the mentioned otherness is an implication clear in the more general sense, and it is not considered in the implication (iltizam). Yes, according to the view that it is considered, what he said is sound. Attar: And the implication for the other is not known in existence or non-existence, just as the entailment of conformance for implication is not known; so it is better to direct restriction to explaining the state of conformance with inclusion and implication from the perspective of implication and non-implication, and to leave discussing the state of one with the other due to lack of concern for them because they are subsidiary, unlike conformance for its originality or because they are forsaken in general, as is well-known among them, unlike it. (His statement: 'For the possibility that it be... etc.') This is possibility rationally, based on what we established previously; and what follows it is actual. For the point is a simple meaning, and lack of division is outside its essence; otherwise, it would be a non-being, and it has no clear implication in the more specific sense, and that is why they took it in its definition, as well as its having a position. And the like is said about unity and other simple things. For that reason they said: their definitions are drawings (rusum), not boundaries (hudud), because of the lack of composition in them.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage, from a hashiya by Dusuqi and Attar, discusses the logical relationship between the two types of signification: تضمن (inclusion) and التزام (implication). The opening statement presents two theses: first, that inclusion can occur without implication; second, that implication can occur without inclusion. The first is justified by the possibility of compound meanings that have no mental implication—their components are included but no necessary further meaning is implied. The second is justified by the possibility of simple meanings that have an implication; for example, the concept of 'point' is simple yet it implies indivisibility, which is not part of its essence. Dusuqi then provides an example of a created thing, detailing its possible natures (accident, substance, consideration) and the implications each entails, noting that this depends on whether the more general implication is considered. He emphasizes that 'possibility' here is rational, not necessarily actual. He then critiques Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's claim that conformance (مطابقة) cannot exist without implication because every word implies its otherness. Dusuqi responds that this otherness is a clear implication in the more general sense, which is not usually counted in the definition of التزام. Attar adds that the relation between تضمن and التزام is not evident; thus it is better to focus on the relation between مطابقة and the other two, since تضمن and التزام are subsidiary. He provides the example of the point, which is simple; its definition (as indivisible) includes something outside its essence, making it a رسم not a حد. This illustrates that simple meanings can have implications that are not part of their essential definition.

Key Terms
تضمن
التزام
مطابقة
لازم
رسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dusuqi, why does he say that inclusion without implication is possible?
A) Because every compound meaning has a mental implication.
B) Because some compound meanings may have no mental implication.
C) Because simple meanings always have a mental implication.
D) Because Fakhr al-Razi said so.

Key Takeaway
Inclusion and implication are distinct and can logically occur independently; this is a fundamental distinction in the analysis of signification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of a fiqh term where inclusion exists without implication, and another where implication exists without inclusion. Justify your choices using the criteria from the lesson.

Why Simple Meanings are Defined by Rusum
Attar explains why simple meanings are defined by rusum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why simple meanings like 'point' and 'unity' are defined by rusum (descriptions) rather than hudud (boundaries).

Core Idea
Simple meanings lack composition, so their definitions must draw on external concomitants.

In this lesson, we examine Attar's commentary on the definition of simple concepts. The point (nukta) is a simple meaning; its definition as 'indivisible' uses a property outside its essence. For that reason, logicians call such definitions rusum, not hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify what makes a meaning 'simple'
A simple meaning is one that has no parts or components. It is not composed of genus and differentia.
Examples: 'point', 'unity', 'existence'.
2
Understand why composition is necessary for a hadd
A true definition (hadd) requires a genus (jins) and a differentia (fasl), i.e., composition. If a meaning is simple, it cannot have a hadd.
3
Apply the example of the point
The text states: 'The point is a simple meaning, and lack of division is outside its essence.' Therefore, the definition 'a point is that which has no division' uses a concomitant (indivisibility), not an essential part. This is a rasm.
Similarly, 'unity' is simple; its definition 'lack of composition' is a rasm.
4
Generalize to other simple concepts in Islamic sciences
In kalam and logic, many foundational terms are simple. Their definitions are always rusum. Recognizing this prevents mistakes in defining terms.
Example: 'substance' (jawhar) is sometimes defined by its concomitant 'occupying space', making it a rasm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا الأَوَّلُ : فَلِجواز أن يكون من المعاني المركبة ما لا يكونُ لازم ذهني، فهناك تضمن بدونِ الالتزام.
وأمَّا الثّاني: فَلِجواز أن يكون للمعنى البسيط لازم ذهني، فهناك الالتزام بدونِ التَّضمن .
الدسوقي
مخلوق، وهو إمَّا عَرَضٌ ؛ ويلزمُهُ القيام بالغير، وإمَّا جوهرٌ ؛ ويلزمه التحيز، أو أمر اعتباري؛ ويلزمُهُ أَنَّهُ مُغاير لغيرِهِ مِنَ الأشياء، وهذا بناءً على أنَّ اللُّزوم الأعم معتبر، وإلا ؛ فقد لا يكون لشيء لازم أخص .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَلِجَوَازِ ... إلخ) عبر بالجواز إشارة إلى أنَّ هذا أمر ممكن عقلاً، وإن لم يوجد له مثال ؛ لأنَّ مجرَّدَ الإمكان لا يستلزم الوجود.
وقولُ الفخرِ: لا يتأتّى وجود المطابقيَّة بدون الالتزاميَّة؛ لأنَّ معنى كلِّ لفظ يلزمه المغايرة لغيره، فَفِيه : أنَّ المغايرة المذكورة لازمٌ بيّن بالمعنى الأعم، وهو
غيرُ مُعتبر في دلالة الالتزام ، نعم على القول باعتباره يتمُ مَا قَالَهُ.
العطار
والالتزامِ لِلآخرِ غيرُ مَعلوم وجوداً وعدماً، كَمَا أنَّ استلزام المطابقة للالتزام غيرُ معلوم ؛ فَالأَوْلى توجيه الاقتصار على بيانِ حال المطابقةِ مَعَ التَّضَمُّنِ والالتزامِ مِنَ اللزوم، وعدم الاستلزام وتركُ التَّعرُّض لحالِ أحدهما معَ الآخَرِ لعدم الاهتمامِ بشأنهما لفرعيَّتِهما ؛ بِخِلافِ المطابقة لأصالتها أو لكونهما مهجورين في الجملة، كما اشتهر فيما بينَهُم بِخِلافِها ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَلِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) هذا جواز عقلاً؛ بناءً على مَا فَرَّرْنَاهُ سابقاً، والَّذي بعدَهُ وُقوعيٌّ، فإنَّ النُّقطة معنّى بسيط، وعدم الانقسام خارج عن ماهيتها، وإلا ؛ كانت أمراً عدميًّا وَلَا لازم بيّن لها بالمعنى الأخصّ، وَلِذَا أَخذُوهُ في تعريفها وكَذَا كونها ذاتَ وضع، ويُقَالُ مثلُ ذلكَ في الوحدة وسائر البسائط، وَلِذلكَ قَالُوا : إِنَّ تعاريفها رُسومٌ لا حدود؛ لعدم التركيب فيها .
As for the first: because it is possible that among compound meanings there may be that which has no mental implication, so there is inclusion without implication. And as for the second: because it is possible that a simple meaning has a mental implication, so there is implication without inclusion. Dusuqi: Created, and it is either an accident, so it requires inherence in another, or a substance, so it requires occupying space, or a consideration, so it requires being other than others. And this is based on the view that the more general implication is considered; otherwise, some things may have no specific implication. (His statement: 'For the possibility... etc.') He used 'possibility' to indicate that this is rationally possible, even if no example exists, because mere possibility does not entail existence. And the statement of Fakhr: 'Conformance cannot exist without implication, because the meaning of every word entails its being other than others.' In it: the mentioned otherness is an implication clear in the more general sense, and it is not considered in the implication (iltizam). Yes, according to the view that it is considered, what he said is sound. Attar: And the implication for the other is not known in existence or non-existence, just as the entailment of conformance for implication is not known; so it is better to direct restriction to explaining the state of conformance with inclusion and implication from the perspective of implication and non-implication, and to leave discussing the state of one with the other due to lack of concern for them because they are subsidiary, unlike conformance for its originality or because they are forsaken in general, as is well-known among them, unlike it. (His statement: 'For the possibility that it be... etc.') This is possibility rationally, based on what we established previously; and what follows it is actual. For the point is a simple meaning, and lack of division is outside its essence; otherwise, it would be a non-being, and it has no clear implication in the more specific sense, and that is why they took it in its definition, as well as its having a position. And the like is said about unity and other simple things. For that reason they said: their definitions are drawings (rusum), not boundaries (hudud), because of the lack of composition in them.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage, from a hashiya by Dusuqi and Attar, discusses the logical relationship between the two types of signification: تضمن (inclusion) and التزام (implication). The opening statement presents two theses: first, that inclusion can occur without implication; second, that implication can occur without inclusion. The first is justified by the possibility of compound meanings that have no mental implication—their components are included but no necessary further meaning is implied. The second is justified by the possibility of simple meanings that have an implication; for example, the concept of 'point' is simple yet it implies indivisibility, which is not part of its essence. Dusuqi then provides an example of a created thing, detailing its possible natures (accident, substance, consideration) and the implications each entails, noting that this depends on whether the more general implication is considered. He emphasizes that 'possibility' here is rational, not necessarily actual. He then critiques Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's claim that conformance (مطابقة) cannot exist without implication because every word implies its otherness. Dusuqi responds that this otherness is a clear implication in the more general sense, which is not usually counted in the definition of التزام. Attar adds that the relation between تضمن and التزام is not evident; thus it is better to focus on the relation between مطابقة and the other two, since تضمن and التزام are subsidiary. He provides the example of the point, which is simple; its definition (as indivisible) includes something outside its essence, making it a رسم not a حد. This illustrates that simple meanings can have implications that are not part of their essential definition.

Key Terms
نقطة
بسيط
رسم
حد
لازم

D) Because the point is a compound meaning.

Key Takeaway
Simple concepts cannot have real definitions (hudud) because they lack composition; their definitions are always rusum (descriptions) using properties outside their essence.

Parsing 'Fa' and 'Hal' in Logical Definitions
Author analyzes the grammatical role of 'fa' and 'hal' in the definition's phrasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the Arabic phrasing of a logical definition by identifying the grammatical roles of 'fa' as jawab al-shart and 'ma‘a al-dalalah' as a circumstantial accusative (hal).

Core Idea
A 'fa' in a definition can signal a conditional response, and the next phrase may be a hal.

In Islamic logic, definitions are often phrased as conditional statements. Understanding the precise grammatical structure is crucial for correctly interpreting the definition's scope and conditions. This lesson breaks down a key parsing issue from the commentary on al-Tahdhib, showing how grammarians analyze the particle 'fa' and the circumstantial accusative 'ma‘a al-dalalah'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditional Structure
Notice that 'fa' often functions as the response to a conditional (jawab al-shart) in definitions. Locate the implied 'if' clause from the context.
The original conditional order is usually 'if ... then ...'.
2
Locate the Circumstantial Accusative (Hal)
Find the phrase that describes the state of the subject. Here, 'ma‘a al-dalalah' (with the indication) is a hal describing the state of the subject while it is independent.
The hal is governed by the same operator as its owner.
3
Recognize the Operator (Amil)
Determine what governs the hal. In this passage, the operator is 'istaqalla' (is independent). The hal 'ma‘a al-dalalah' is derived from the pronoun in 'istaqalla'.
4
Understand the 'Fa' Prevention Rule and Resolution
Normally, 'fa' prevents the preceding word from operating on what follows. Here, the objection is that 'istaqalla' cannot govern the hal because of 'fa'. The resolution: 'fa' is not in its proper position; the original order is 'if it is independent ... then it is a word', so the prevention does not apply.
Syntax is considered from wording, not meaning (as al-‘Attar adds).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : «فمع الدلالةِ» الفاء في جواب الشرط، و «مع الدلالة» حال من الضمير في «استقل». الدسوقي وكذا قال السيد : ولكنَّهُ يُخالف قولَ الشَّارح، وعند النحاة: فِعْل، وسُمِّي ذلكَ المفردُ المستقلُّ بالمفهوميَّةِ الدَّالُّ على أحد الأزمنة بهيئته كلمةٌ ؛ لأنَّ الكَلَّمَ هو الجرح، وهي لتأثيرها في الفؤادِ بسبب تغييرِ الزَّمانِ كأَنَّها جَرَحته، وإِنَّما قدَّمَ الفعل في التقسيم على الاسم، مع أنَّ الاسم أشرفُ؛ لأنَّ مَفهوم الفعل وجودي، والوجود مُقدَّم على العدم. (قَوْلُهُ : حَالٌ مِنَ الضَّمِيرِ ... إلخ) فيه : أنَّ العامل في الحال هو العامل في صاحبها، والعامل في صاحبها استقل، والفاء تمنعُ مِن عمل ما قبلها فيما بعدها ويُجابُ : بأنَّ محلَّ المنع فيما إذا كانَتِ الفاء واقعةً موقعها، وهذا ليس كذلِكَ؛ فهي مُقدَّمة من تأخير . والأصلُ : إنّ استقلَّ مع الدَّلالةِ بهيئتِهِ على أحدِ الأزمنة؛ فهو كلمة، وفيه: أنَّ الإعراب إنَّما ينظر له مِن جهةِ اللّفظِ لَا مِن جهة المعنى . العطار
And his statement: 'So with the indication' — the 'fa' is the response to the conditional, and 'with the indication' is a circumstantial accusative (hal) from the pronoun in 'istаqalla' (is independent). Al-Dasuqi: Likewise al-Sayyid said, but it contradicts the statement of the commentator, and according to grammarians: verb, and that independent singular [expression] that is independent in meaning, indicating one of the times by its form, is called 'kalima' because 'kalma' is a wound, and due to its effect on the heart because of the change of time, as if it wounded it. And he only gave precedence to the verb in division over the noun, even though the noun is nobler, because the concept of the verb is existential, and existence is given precedence over non-existence. (His statement: 'a circumstantial accusative from the pronoun...') — therein: that the operative (amil) in the circumstantial accusative is the same as the operative in its owner, and the operative in its owner is 'istаqalla', and the 'fa' prevents the operation of what is before it on what comes after it. And it is answered: that the place of prevention is when the 'fa' is in its proper position, and this is not so; it is postponed from its original place. And the original is: 'If it is independent with the indication by its form of one of the times, then it is a word.' And therein: that syntax is considered from the perspective of the wording, not from the perspective of meaning. Al-‘Attar.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical analysis of the definition's phrasing. The phrase 'fa-ma‘a al-dalalah' is parsed: 'fa' is the response particle (jawab al-shart) of an implied conditional, and 'ma‘a al-dalalah' (with the indication) is a circumstantial accusative (hal) derived from the pronoun embedded in the verb 'istaqalla' (is independent). Al-Dasuqi notes that al-Sayyid agreed but this contradicts the commentator's own statement. Then there is an etymological explanation: 'kalima' is called such because it derives from 'kalma' meaning wound, as it affects the heart due to changing time. The verb is given precedence over the noun in division because its concept is existential, and existence precedes non-existence. The commentator then raises a grammatical objection: normally, a 'fa' prevents the preceding operator from acting on what follows, so 'istаqalla' cannot govern 'ma‘a al-dalalah' as a circumstantial accusative. The answer is that this 'fa' is not in its proper position; it is postponed from its original place, so the original order is: 'If it is independent with the indication... then it is a word.' Thus the prevention does not apply. Al-‘Attar adds that syntax concerns only the wording, not the meaning.

فَاء

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' in logical definitions often signals a conditional response, and the phrase following can be a circumstantial accusative, parseable by understanding the original order of the conditional.

Grammatical Precision in Defining Kalima: The Displaced Fa and the Hal
Commentators clarify the grammatical function of 'fa' and 'hal' in the definition of kalima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the displacement of the 'fa' in a conditional construction affects grammatical operation, and apply this to the logical definition of kalima.

Core Idea
A displaced 'fa' does not block the 'amil—showing grammar serves logic in definition.

This passage from the Tadhhib commentary unpacks the precise grammatical structure of the definition of kalima (word). The commentators debate whether the 'fa' in 'fa-ma'a al-dalalah' prevents the preceding verb 'istaqalla' from operating on the following phrase. Their resolution—that the 'fa' is displaced from its normal position—safeguards the logical independence of the definition. Understanding this grammatical nuance prevents misinterpretation of a foundational logical term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of kalima
Extract the full definition from the Arabic: 'ذلك المفرد المستقل بالمفهومية الدال على أحد الأزمنة بهيئته كلمة'. Note the key components: independent, indivisible, concept, indicates time by its form.
This definition is the basis for the grammatical analysis.
2
Locate the grammatical structure
Recognize that 'فمع الدلالة' consists of 'fa' as the answer particle of the conditional, and 'مع الدلالة' as a hal (state) from the pronoun in 'استقل'.
The hal describes the condition of the subject of 'استقل'.
3
Evaluate the objection
Understand the problem: the 'fa' normally prevents the preceding 'amil (istaqalla) from operating on what follows. Why is this a challenge for the definition’s parsing?
If the 'fa' blocks the 'amil, the hal would be ungrammatical.
4
Apply the solution
See how the commentators argue that the 'fa' is moved from its original position (تقديم من تأخير) and therefore does not block the 'amil. Analogize to the Quranic example: 'وأما السائل فلا تنهر'.
The displacement removes the blocking effect.
5
Reflect on the outcome
Recognize that the displaced 'fa' allows the hal to be governed by 'istaqalla', preserving the logical flow of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : «فمع الدَّلالةِ» الفاء في جواب الشرط، و «مع الدَّلالة» حال من الضَّميرِ في «استقل». الدسوقي وَكَذا قالَ السَّيِّدُ : ولكنَّهُ يُخالف قولَ الشَّارح، وعند النحاة: فِعْل، وسُمِّي ذلكَ المفردُ المستقلُّ بالمفهوميَّةِ الدَّالُّ على أحد الأزمنة بهيئته كلمةٌ ؛ لأنَّ الكَلَّمَ هو الجرح، وهي لتأثيرها في الفؤادِ بسبب تغييرِ الزَّمانِ كأَنَّها جَرَحته، وإِنَّما قدَّمَ الفعل في التقسيم على الاسم، مع أنَّ الاسم أشرفُ؛ لأنَّ مَفهوم الفعل وجودي، والوجود مُقدَّم على العدم. (قَوْلُهُ : حَالٌ مِنَ الضَّمِيرِ ... إلخ) فيه : أنَّ العامل في الحال هو العامل في صاحبها، والعامل في صاحبها استقل، والفاء تمنعُ مِن عمل ما قبلها فيما بعدها ويُجابُ : بأنَّ محلَّ المنع فيما إذا كانَتِ الفاء واقعةً موقعها، وهذا ليس كذلِكَ؛ فهي مُقدَّمة من تأخير . والأصلُ : إنّ استقلَّ مع الدَّلالةِ بهيئتِهِ على أحدِ الأزمنة؛ فهو كلمة، وفيه: أنَّ الإعراب إنَّما ينظر له مِن جهةِ اللّفظِ لَا مِن جهة المعنى . العطار بذلكَ التَّعريف لا تتناولُ اسم الفعل، فَالفعلُ المرادفُ له لا يتناوله أيضاً، والسَّيِّدُ ما جعل اسم الفعلِ دَاخِلاً في الكلمة إلا على تعريفها بما يصلح للإخبارِ بِهِ وحدَهُ لا عنه أيضاً، ومَنَ قَالَ : فِعْلُ ؛ أي: أو اسمُ فِعْل ، واستدلَّ بِكلام السَّيِّدِ؛ فَمَا أجادَ ؛ لأنَّه مع عدم مُناسبتِهِ لهُ، فيهِ حملَ الدَّلالة في كلام المصنفِ على ما يشمل أن يكون الهيئةَ لِلمدلول، وربَّما يلزمه أن يكونَ لفظُ الفعل كلمةٌ لذلك، ولمْ يَقُلْ بِهِ أحدٌ ، تَأَمَّلْ، قالَهُ بعضُ الحواشي وَهُوَ مُتَّجه . (قَوْلُهُ : حَالٌ مِنَ الضَّمِيرِ فِي اسْتَقَلَّ لا مِنَ المبتدأ المقدَّرِ قبل كلمة، ومَا قِيلَ لا يصح حاليتُهُ مِن فاعل استقل؛ لأنَّ ما قبل فاء الجزاء لا يعمل فيما بَعدَهُ مُندفع ؛ لأنَّ الفاءَ إِذا نُحْلِقَتْ عَن مَحلَّها ؛ لا تمنعُ ، ومحلُّهَا هُنَا كلمةٌ على حَدٌ مَا قِيلَ في : وَأَمَّا السَّائِلَ فَلَا تَنْهَرْ [الضحى : ١٠].
He said: 'So with the indication' – the fa is the answer of the conditional, and 'with the indication' is a hal (state) from the pronoun in 'istaqalla' (is independent). Al-Dasuqi: And likewise said al-Sayyid, but it contradicts the words of the commentator. According to grammarians, it is a verb. And that independent indivisible [word] which is a concept indicating one of the times by its form is called kalima; because 'kalima' means 'wound', and it is so called because of its effect on the heart due to the change of time, as if it had wounded it. And he only gave precedence to the verb in the division over the noun, even though the noun is nobler, because the concept of the verb is existential, and existence takes precedence over non-existence. (His words: 'a hal from the pronoun...') There is that the operator in the hal is the same as the operator in its owner, and the operator in its owner is istaqalla, and the fa prevents the operation of what came before it on what comes after it. And it is answered: that the place of prevention is when the fa occupies its proper position, and this is not so; for it is advanced from a delayed position. The original is: 'If it is independent with the indication by its form of one of the times, then it is a kalima.' And there is that parsing only considers it from the aspect of the wording, not from the aspect of meaning. Al-'Attar: With this definition, it does not include ism al-fi'l (noun of verb), and the verb synonymous with it does not include it either. Al-Sayyid only made ism al-fi'l enter into kalima according to its definition as 'what is fit to be predicated by itself and not about something else'. And whoever said 'fi'l' meaning 'or ism fi'l' and used the words of al-Sayyid as evidence, he did not do well, because besides being unsuitable for him, it involves making the indication in the words of the author include that the form be for the indicated, and perhaps it would necessitate the word 'fi'l' itself being a kalima for that, and no one has said that. Contemplate. Some of the margins said that, and it is sound. (His words: 'a hal from the pronoun in istaqalla, not from the supposed subject before kalima. And what was said that it is not valid for it to be a hal from the subject of istaqalla because what comes before the fa of the answer does not operate on what comes after it, is repelled because the fa, when it is removed from its proper place, does not prevent, and its place here is 'kalima', according to what was said in: 'وَلَا تَنْهَرْ' [Ad-Duha: 10].'
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the super-commentators (al-Dasuqi, al-'Attar, and others) of the grammatical analysis of a line from the matn defining 'kalima'. The focus is on the phrase 'فمع الدلالة' (so with the indication). The author first explains that the 'fa' is the answer of the conditional, and 'مع الدلالة' is a hal (state) from the pronoun in 'استقل' (is independent). Al-Dasuqi notes that al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani agreed with this but it contradicts the plain commentator (perhaps al-Akhdari). Then there is a digression on why the verb is called 'kalima': because 'kalima' originally means 'wound', and the verb affects the heart due to change of time. The verb is given precedence over the noun in division because its concept is existential. Next, the discussion turns to the operator of the hal. The objection is that the 'fa' prevents the preceding operator from operating on what follows. The answer is that the 'fa' is not in its normal position here, so it does not prevent. Then al-'Attar raises the issue of 'ism al-fi'l' (noun of action) not being included in the definition, and criticizes those who try to include it by misinterpreting al-Sayyid. Finally, a new comment clarifies that the hal is from the pronoun of 'استقل' not from a supposed subject, using the Quranic verse 'وَأَمَّا السَّائِلَ فَلَا تَنْهَرْ' as an analogy for the 'fa' not preventing when it is removed from its proper place. The passage guards against misunderstanding the grammatical structure of the definition.

فاء الجزاء
حال
استقلال
كلمة

D) Because the definition is about independence

Key Takeaway
The displaced 'fa' in the definition of kalima does not block the grammatical operator, preserving the logical independence (istiqlal) of the term.
