قال المصنف في شرح الرسالة : وَهَهُنَا نظر، وهو أن جعل وحدةَ الشَّرط والجزء والكُلِّ راجعة إلى وحدة الموضوع، والبواقي إلى وحدة المحمول ؛ مِمَّا لا يصح على إطلاقِهِ؛ لأنَّهُ إذا عكشتَ القضايا المذكورة انعكس الأمرُ، وصارت وحدةُ الشَّرط والجزء والكُلِّ راجعة إلى المحمول، والبواقي إلى الموضوع، فالأولى القولُ برجوع جميع الوحدات إلى وحدة الموضوع والمحمول من غير تخصيص ا.هـ. وأجاب السيد : بأنَّ المخصص كأنَّهُ راعَى مَا هو الظَّاهِرُ مِن أنَّ رجوع وحدةِ الشَّرطِ وَوحدةِ الكُلِّ والجزء إلى وحدة الموضوع، ورجوع البواقي إلى وحدة المحمول أظهرُ ؛ لأنَّ اعتبار الشَّرطِ والكُلِّ والجزء في الموضوع، واعتبار الزَّمانِ والمكان والإضافةِ والقوَّةِ والفعل في المحمول أنسبُ .ا.هـ. وفي شرح المطالع : لا يُقالُ : الزَّمانُ خارج عن طَرفَي القضيَّة؛ لأنَّ نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لا بُدَّ لها مِن زمانٍ، فَلَو كَانَ الزَّمانُ داخلاً في المحمول ؛ لَكَانَ نسبةُ ذلك المحمول إلى الموضوع واقعةً في زمان، فيكونُ للزَّمانِ زمانُ آخَر ، ولأنَّ تعلَّقَ الزَّمانِ بالقضيَّةِ بحسب ظرفيَّةِ النِّسبةِ والشَّيء لا يصيرُ ظرفاً لآخر إلا بعدَ تَحقُّقِهِ، فيكونُ تعلُّق الزَّمانِ مُتأخّراً عن النِّسبةِ المتأخّرةِ عَن طَرفَي القضيَّة، فلو كان داخلاً فى أحدهما ؛ لَكانَ مُتأخّراً عن نفسه بمراتب، وإِنَّهُ محال لأَنَّا نقولُ : تعلَّق المكان أيضاً بحسب الظرفية، إذ لا بُدَّ لِلنِّسبة مِن مكانٍ، كَمَا لا بُدَّ لها من زمان، فلا وجه لاندراج وحدة المكان تحتَ وحدة الموضوع وإخراج وحدة الزَّمانِ عنها ا.هـ. ومُحصِّلُ الجوابِ معارضةٌ لِلسؤالِ السَّائِلِ أَنَّهُ على تقديرِ لزومِ الزَّمانِ لِلزَّمانِ يلزمُ أيضاً أن يكون للمكان مكان آخَرُ بالدَّليل المذكور، ثمَّ هذا مبني على القول بأنَّ الزَّمانَ مَوجودٌ ، وأنَّهُ مِقدارُ الحركةِ كَما هو رأي الحكماء، أما على
The author said in Sharh al-Risala: And here there is a consideration: that making the unity of condition, part, and whole revert to unity of subject, and the rest to unity of predicate, is not valid absolutely, because if you invert the mentioned propositions, the matter reverses, and the unity of condition, part, and whole reverts to the predicate, and the rest to the subject. So the better view is to say that all unities revert to both unity of subject and predicate without specification. End. And al-Sayyid answered: that the specifier seems to have considered what is apparent: that the reversion of unity of condition, part, and whole to unity of subject, and the reversion of the rest to unity of predicate is more apparent, because considering condition, part, and whole in the subject is more appropriate, and considering time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality in the predicate is more appropriate. End. And in Sharh al-Matali': It cannot be said that time is outside the two terms of the proposition, because the attribution of the predicate to the subject necessarily requires a time. So if time were included in the predicate, then that predicate's attribution to the subject would occur in a time, so time would have another time. And because the attachment of time to the proposition is according to the circumstance of the relation, and a thing does not become a circumstance for another except after its existence, so the attachment of time would be posterior to the relation which is posterior to the two terms of the proposition. So if time were included in one of them, it would be posterior to itself by degrees, which is impossible. For we say: the attachment of place is also according to circumstance, since a relation necessarily requires a place just as it requires a time. So there is no justification for including unity of place under unity of subject and excluding unity of time from it. End. And the gist of the answer is to counter the objector's question: that on the assumption of the necessity of time for time, there would also necessarily be another place for place by the mentioned proof. Then this is based on the view that time exists and that it is the measure of motion, as is the view of the philosophers. As for...
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the earlier classification of unities. The author of Sharh al-Risala (a commentary on another logic text) objects that the assignment of condition, part, whole to subject and the others to predicate is not absolute. He argues that if you take the same propositions but convert them (i.e., switch subject and predicate? or invert the quantifier?), the assignment flips. Therefore, it is better to say all unities revert to both subject and predicate without specification. Al-Sayyid responds that the original assignment is more apparent and appropriate: condition, part, whole naturally belong to the subject, while time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality belong to the predicate. Then a further discussion from Sharh al-Matali' addresses an objection about time: if time is external to the two terms of the proposition, it cannot be part of the predicate, else an infinite regress occurs. The reply is that the same objection applies to place, so if place can be included in the subject, time should be treated similarly. The passage ends mid-sentence, mentioning that this depends on the view that time exists and is the measure of motion (the philosophers' view). The incomplete text suggests a further condition.

Key Takeaway
The classification of unities is not fixed; logical operations like conversion reveal its relativity, and the debate shows the need for precise specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition: 'If the sale is valid, then ownership transfers.' Identify which unity (condition) applies to the subject (sale) and which to the predicate (ownership transfer). Then convert the proposition to 'Ownership transfers only if the sale is valid.' Does the assignment of condition switch? Discuss whether the classification is absolute.
?
In usul al-fiqh, scholars debated whether the unity of time is part of the predicate or external. Use the conversion test from this lesson to see if the assignment is consistent, and compare with the case of place.

Analyzing a Critical Refinement on Modal Proposition Conversion
Super-commentator critiques the answer distinguishing quantity and modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a critical refinement in a hashiya and evaluate the grounds on which a commentator rejects an earlier answer.

Core Idea
The super-commentator dissects the answer to find where it fails—quantity vs. modality is insufficient for specific conditionals.

This lesson takes you inside a hashiya (super-commentary) where a later logician refines an earlier answer about the conversion of particular negative modal propositions. The passage shows how the super-commentator, along with al-'Attar and al-Sharnubi, challenges the distinction between quantity and modality, and offers al-Harawi's alternative. Understanding this refinement helps you see how Islamic logicians built cumulative knowledge through precise critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original answer being refined
Find the claim that the conversion of particular negative modal propositions is valid by distinguishing quantity and modality.
This is the target of the critique.
2
Locate the super-commentator's objection
Note the argument that this fails for the specific conditional ('urfiyya khāṣṣa) because the modality is the same in both original and converted proposition.
3
Find the alternative answer (al-Harawi's)
Identify al-Harawi's answer: the conversion is by considering the positive part (al-juz' al-ījābī) understood from the condition of non-permanence (al-lādawām).
This shifts the basis from modality to the positive part of the condition.
4
Analyze the dismissal of the commentator's analogies
Examine the super-commentator's rejection of the first analogy (first part of compound proposition) as 'nonsense', and the acceptance of the second analogy (essential entailment) as correct but weak.
The super-commentator clarifies that restrictions have an effect on rulings.
5
Summarize the final resolution (al-Sharnubi's footnote)
Review al-Sharnubi's endorsement of al-Harawi's answer and his warning that rejecting it leads to inconsistency.
The truth is that the conversion concerns the tail part, indicating a particular affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : وَنَحْنُ نُثْبِتُ انْعِكَاسَهَا بحسب الجهةِ فيه : أنَّ هذا لا يظهر بالنسبةِ لعكس العرفية الخاصَّةِ إلى عرفيَّة خاصَّةٍ، فإنَّ الجهة فيهما واحدةٌ، وحينئذٍ ; فَلَا يظهر هذا الجواب. العطار قَوْلُهُ: فَإِنْ قُلْتَ منشأ هذا السُّؤالِ; انتفاء انعكاس المشروطة والعرفية الخاصَّتَيْنِ عرفيةً خاصةً. قَوْلُهُ: قُلْتُ: أَرَادَ الْمُصَنِّفُ وأجابَ الهروي بجواب آخَرَ; وهوَ أَنَّ ذلكَ الانعكاس باعتبار الجزء الإيجابي المفهومِ مِنْ قَيْدِ اللادوامِ ا.هـ. وأمَّا تَنظيرُ المحشّي في جوابِهِ بأنَّ العبرة في القضيَّةِ المركَّبةِ إنَّما هو بالجزء الأَوَّلِ; فَمِنَ اللَّغْوِ; لأنَّه ليس في الجوابِ تعرُّضُ لتعليل كونها موجبةٌ أو سالبةٌ، وإِنَّما بَيَّنَ أَنَّ ذلك الانعكاس باعتبار الجزء الإيجابي، فَهذا بيان لمحل الانعكاس; لا تعليل للإيجاب أو السَّلْبِ حتّى يرد عليه ما ذكر، وكذلك تنظيرُهُ في جوابِ الشَّارحِ بِقَولِهِ بأنَّ الجهة بيان للقضيَّة، وبيان لِحالِ نِسبتها في الواقع، وإذا كان أصل القضيَّة لا ينعكس; فالقيد لا ينفعُ في الانعكاس ا.هـ; لأنَّ مَعنى كلامِ الشَّارِحِ أَنَّ القضيَّةَ نفسَها انعكسَتْ باعتبارِ مُلاحظةِ الجهةِ إلى ما ذكر، كما أنَّهُ بِاعتبارِ مُلاحظةِ الكَمّ لم تنعكش، فليست الجهة بعينها منعكسةً ، وقوله : وإذا كان أصل القضيَّة . .. إلخ، كلامٌ ليسَ لهُ معنّى مُحصَّلٌ ; لأنَّ لِلقيودِ تأثيراً في اختلافِ الأحكام، فالقضيَّةُ مُجرَّدةٌ عن ملاحظة هذا القيد واعتباره لا عكس لها وباعتباره تنعكس، وأمَّا تَنظيرُهُ في جوابِ الشَّارحِ الثَّاني وهو قَولُهُ: ويمكنُ أنْ يُقال ... إلخ، من أنَّ العكس إذا تخلَّفَ في مادَّةٍ ; دلَّ على أنَّ القضيَّةَ لم تستلزِمْ لِذَاتِهَا العكس، والعبرة بالاستلزام الذاتي; لَا مَا يكونُ بخصوص المادَّةِ ا.هـ. فَفِي محلّهِ، وَلِذلكَ أَخَّرَهُ الشَّارِحُ وعَنْوَنَهُ بقولِهِ : ويمكنُ أنْ يُقالَ لِلإشارة إلى ضعفِهِ بورود ما ذكر، وقولُ المحشّي: المقام محل إشكالٍ ; فهوَ مُجرَّدُ استهوال. (قَوْلُهُ : كاتب أي بالفعل، وقوله : ( وهو الجزء الثاني من العكس) وهو المشار إليه بلا دائماً . (قَوْلُهُ : لا يظهر ... إلخ) أي : وإن ظهر بالنسبة إلى المشروطة الخاصة السالبة الجزئية فلم هذا الجواب، وكذا الثَّاني لما أن العكس لازم لا يتخلف، وقد تختلف في الجزئية يتم : السالبة عامة الموضوع وصحة العكس هنا وفي نحو بعض الإنسان ليس بأبيض فلخصوص المادة، فالحق في الجواب ما قاله الهروي من أن صحة العكس هنا بالنسبة للعجز وهو اللادوام لأنه إشارة إلى موجبة جزئية، فإن تم اعتراض العلامة يس عليه بأن العبرة في المركبة بإيجاب الصدر أو سلبه دون العجز ; وجب عدم صحة عكس السالبة الجزئية مطلقاً حتى الخاصتين وإلا لزم التنافي في كلامهم وعدم الاطراد في قواعدهم وبما ذكرنا تعلم ما في العطار من الطعن والإكثار بدون اقتصار . ا.هـ. الشَّرْنوبي.
His statement: 'And we affirm its conversion with respect to modality' — there is in it that this does not appear regarding the conversion of the specific conditional [proposition] to a specific conditional, for the modality in both is one; so this answer does not appear. al-'Attar: His statement: 'If you say' — the origin of this question is the negation of the conversion of the conditional and the two specific propositions to a specific conditional. His statement: 'I say: The author intended...' And al-Harawi answered with another answer: that that conversion is by considering the positive part understood from the condition of non-permanence. As for the commentator's analogy in his answer that the consideration in a compound proposition is only the first part, it is nonsense, because in his answer there is no attempt to explain why it is affirmative or negative; rather he clarified that the conversion is by considering the positive part, so this is an explanation of the locus of conversion, not a cause for affirmation or negation. Similarly his analogy in the commentator's answer by his saying that the modality is an explanation of the proposition and an explanation of the state of its relation in reality; and if the original proposition does not convert, the restriction does not benefit in conversion. So the meaning of the commentator's speech is that the proposition itself converted by considering the modality to what was mentioned, just as by considering quantity it did not convert, so the modality itself is not converted. And his saying 'if the original proposition does not convert' etc. is speech with no sound meaning, because restrictions have effect in differing rulings; the proposition stripped of considering this restriction has no conversion, but by considering it, it converts. As for his analogy in the second answer of the commentator, which is his saying 'and it is possible to say' etc., that if conversion fails in a particular matter, it indicates that the proposition does not entail conversion essentially, and the consideration is of essential entailment, not what occurs by particular matter — that is correct. That is why the commentator delayed it and headed it with 'and it is possible to say' to indicate its weakness due to what was mentioned. And the saying of the commentator: 'the place is problematic' is just exaggeration. His statement: 'writer' meaning actually; and his statement: 'and it is the second part of the conversion' which is indicated by 'without always'. His statement: 'it does not appear' etc. means: even if it appears regarding the specific conditional particular negative, why this answer? Similarly the second because conversion is necessary and does not fail; and it may differ in the particular [case]. 'The negative is general in subject' and the validity of conversion here and in the case of 'some human is not white' is due to the particular matter. So the truth in the answer is what al-Harawi said: that the validity of conversion here is regarding the tail [part of the proposition], which is the non-permanence, because it indicates a particular affirmative. If the objection of the scholar 'Isa is accepted that the consideration in a compound proposition is the affirmation or negation of the first part, not the tail, then the conversion of the particular negative must be invalid absolutely, even for the two specific propositions; otherwise there would be contradiction in their statements and lack of consistency in their rules. By what we mentioned, you know what is in al-'Attar's criticism and excess without concision. End of al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) where the super-commentator (likely al-Dasuqi) and al-'Attar challenge the answer given in the previous lesson. The critique begins by noting that the answer distinguishing quantity and modality fails when applied to the specific conditional propositions ('urfiyya khāṣṣa), because both the original and converted propositions have the same modality. al-'Attar then explains that the original objection arose from the negation of conversion for conditional propositions. He cites al-Harawi's alternative answer: the conversion is valid because it considers the positive part (al-juz' al-ījābī) implied by the condition of non-permanence (al-lādawām). The super-commentator dismisses the analogy made by the commentator (muḥashshī) as 'nonsense' because it misinterprets the role of restrictions. He further clarifies that the commentator's second answer, which appeals to essential entailment (al-istilzām al-dhātī), is correct but was delayed by the sharh to hint at its weakness. The footnote by al-Sharnubi summarizes that the true answer is al-Harawi's: the conversion is valid because it concerns the tail part (al-'ajz) of the proposition, which indicates a particular affirmative. He warns that rejecting this leads to inconsistency in logical rules. The overall tone is one of meticulous refinement, typical of hashiya literature.

اللادوام
الجزء الإيجابي
الاستلزام الذاتي

Al-Matar's Critique: Real vs. Epistemic Necessity in Qiyas
Al-Matar critiques the implication of the definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real necessity (luzum fi nafs al-amr) and epistemic necessity (luzum 'ilmi) as applied to the definition of qiyas.

Core Idea
The uttered qiyas does not entail its conclusion in reality, only epistemically.

Al-Matar's critique of the qiyas definition raises a profound point: does the uttered statement of a syllogism itself necessitate the conclusion, or merely indicate it? He argues that the necessity is only epistemic, not real, and that the definition should be for mental qiyas. This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of logical formalism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of necessity
Find in al-Matar's text where he contrasts 'luzum bihasab nafs al-amr' (necessity in itself) and 'luzum 'ilmi' (epistemic necessity). Understand that the first is about objective consequence, the second about subjective cognition.
2
Understand al-Matar's argument
The uttered statement (qawl malfuz) does not entail anything in itself; it only indicates the mental meaning. The mental meaning then entails the conclusion. Hence, the uttered qiyas only has epistemic necessity via indication.
3
Recognize the implication for the definition
If the definition requires real necessity, the uttered qiyas fails. Therefore, al-Matar suggests the definition should apply to mental qiyas only, though other scholars allow alternative interpretations.
4
Note the alternative view
Some commentators (like the author and al-Qutb al-Razi) consider the definition applicable to either mental or uttered qiyas interchangeably. Al-Matar notes this but prefers mental qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطار
كِلَا التَّقديريْنِ يُراد بالقولِ الأَوَّلِ : الآخَرُ المعقولُ ؛ لعدم لزوم التلفظ بالقول الملفوظ لشيءٍ؛ لَا مِنَ القولِ الملفوظ وَلَا مِنَ القولِ المعقول، بل إنَّما يلزمُ القول المعقول من القولِ المعقول بلا واسطةٍ وَمِنَ القولِ الملفوظ بواسطةٍ ؛ دلالته على المعقول بالنَّظر إلى العالم بالوضع، لأنَّ التَّلفظَ يَستلزم تعقُل المعنى بالنسبة للعالم بالوضع، وتَعقُلُ المعنى على تقدير تسليمه يستلزم النتيجةَ ، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّ المرادَ بِاللُّزوم هَهُنا هو اللُّزوم بحسب نفس الأمر لا اللُّزوم العلمي، والقولُ الملفوظ المؤلَّفَ مِنَ القضايا الملفوظة؛ لَا يَستلزم مدلولَهُ بحسب نفس الأمرِ حتّى يستلزم القولَ الآخَرَ بحسب نفس الأمرِ بواسطة، بل إنَّما يَستلزمه بحسب العلم لدلالته عليه، وأيضاً القولُ الملفوظ يستلزم مدلوله بحسب العلم التّصوُّري، ومَدلُولُهُ إِنَّما يستلزم القول الآخَرَ باعتبار العلم التّصديقي، وأيضاً قد يؤدى المعقولُ بألفاظ مجازيَّةٍ، والمجاز ليس موضوعاً، إلا أنْ يُرادَ بِالوضع : مَا يشملُ النَّوعيَّ والتَّخصُّصي، والأَوْلَى أن يجعل التعريف للقياس العقليّ ؛ وإنْ كانَ المتبادرُ مِن عبارةِ الشَّارحِ كَالمصنفِ في شرح الرسالة والقطبِ الرَّازي أنَّ المراد العقلي أو اللفظي على البدل في المعرَّفِ والمعرفِ وفي بعض الحواشي، وأمَّا احتمالُ المركَبِ مِنَ الملفوظ والمعقول؛ فَلَمْ يقع في كلامِهِم التّصريح باعتبارِهِ ، فإن صح اعتبارُهُ فنقولُ : إِنَّ عبارةَ المصنِّف تتنزَّلُ عليه ا . هـ. وهذا كلام ليسَ لهُ اعتبار عندَ ذَوي الأنظار .

العطّار
(قَوْلُهُ : مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا يُخْرِجُ ) أي : بمجموع مؤلَّفَ مِن قضايا ؛ لِكَونِهِ بمنزلة الفصل، وفي شرح المطالع : أنَّ لفظ (مؤلف) مُستدرَكٌ ؛ لأنَّ القول هو المركَّب، وأجابَ السَّيِّدُ في شرح المواقفِ : بأنَّ ذِكْرَ المؤلَّفِ لِئَلَّا يُتوهم أَنَّ المراد قولٌ مِن جملة القضايا بجعل قولِهِ : مِنَ القضايا بمنزلة فردٍ مِنَ الأفراد، فتكونُ (مِنْ)
Al-Matar:
In both estimations, by the first statement is meant the other [conclusion] as mental; because the uttering of the uttered statement does not necessitate anything, neither from the uttered statement nor from the mental statement. Rather, the mental statement entails the mental statement without mediation, and from the uttered statement it entails with mediation; because it indicates the mental with reference to one who knows the conventional language, because uttering necessitates understanding the meaning for one who knows the conventional language, and understanding the meaning, assuming it is valid, entails the result. But there is consideration: because the intended necessity here is necessity in itself, not epistemic necessity. And the uttered statement composed of uttered propositions does not entail its meaning in itself such that it entails the other statement in itself by mediation; rather, it entails it epistemically because it indicates it. Also, the uttered statement entails its meaning in terms of conceptual knowledge, and its meaning entails the other statement in terms of assertoric knowledge. Also, the mental may be expressed using metaphorical expressions, and metaphor is not conventional, unless by 'conventional' is meant what includes generic and specific. And it is better to make the definition for mental qiyas, although the apparent meaning from the statement of the commentator, like the author in his commentary on al-Risala and al-Qutb al-Razi, is that the intended is mental or uttered alternatively in the defined and the definition, and in some glosses. As for the possibility of a composite of uttered and mental, its consideration has not occurred explicitly in their words. If its consideration is valid, we say: the author's wording accommodates it. End. And this speech is not considered by those with insight.

Al-Attar:
(His saying: 'composed of premises excludes') i.e., by a totality composed of premises; because it is like a differentia. And in Sharh al-Matali': that the word (mu'allaf) is redundant because 'qawl' is already compound. And al-Sayyid in Sharh al-Mawaqif answered: that mentioning 'mu'allaf' is to prevent imagining that what is meant is a statement among the premises, making his saying 'from premises' like an individual among individuals, so that 'min' becomes [partitive].
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage contains two critical glosses on the definition of qiyas. The first is from al-Matar, who analyzes the implication (luzum) in the definition. He argues that in both the mental and uttered estimations, the conclusion is mental because the uttered statement itself does not necessitate anything; it only indicates the mental meaning, which then entails the result. He distinguishes between necessity in itself (which the uttered statement does not have) and epistemic necessity (which it does via indication). He also notes that the mental meaning may be expressed metaphorically, which complicates the conventional relation. He prefers that the definition be for mental qiyas only, though the apparent meaning of the commentators allows mental or uttered alternatively. The second gloss is from al-Attar, who discusses the word 'mu'allaf' (composed) in the definition. He points out that some scholars consider it redundant because 'qawl' already implies composition. However, al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani argued that it is necessary to prevent misunderstanding: without it, one might think that 'qawl min qadaya' means a single proposition among the premises. Thus 'mu'allaf' ensures that it means a composition of premises. This critical refinement clarifies the precision of the definition.

Key Terms
لزوم
قول ملفوظ
لزوم في نفس الأمر
لزوم علمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Matar, why does the uttered statement composed of uttered propositions not entail the conclusion in itself?
A) Because the uttered statement is not logically sound
B) Because it only indicates the mental meaning epistemically, not necessarily in reality
C) Because the conclusion must be mental
D) Because the premises are not true

Key Takeaway
Al-Matar's critique shows that the definition of qiyas must be carefully understood: the uttered qiyas only provides epistemic indication, not real entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the uttered syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram, wine is an intoxicant, therefore wine is haram.' According to al-Matar, does the utterance of these three statements entail the conclusion in reality? Explain using the concepts of real vs. epistemic necessity.
?
Find a theological or legal argument that you have encountered (e.g., the proof of God's existence from causality). Describe how the distinction between real and epistemic necessity applies to evaluating that argument's force.

6 min
Al-Attar's Refinement: The Necessity of 'Mu'allaf' in Qiyas Definition
Al-Attar refines the wording 'mu'allaf'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Sayyid al-Jurjani argued that the word 'mu'allaf' is necessary in the definition of qiyas and how it prevents a misunderstanding.

Core Idea
'Mu'allaf' prevents mistaking the premises as a single proposition rather than a composition.

The definition of qiyas includes the phrase 'qaul mu'allaf min qadaya'. Some scholars thought 'mu'allaf' was redundant because 'qawl' already implies composition. But al-Sayyid al-Jurjani showed that without it, one might misunderstand the structure—taking 'min qadaya' as a partitive, meaning a single proposition among the premises, rather than a composition of premises. This lesson examines that critical refinement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition without 'mu'allaf'
Consider the phrase 'qawl min qadaya'. Without 'mu'allaf', this could be read as 'a statement from among premises', i.e., a single premise. This is not the intended meaning.
2
Identify the concern raised by al-Sayyid
Al-Sayyid argued that without 'mu'allaf', the word 'min' might be interpreted as partitive (tab'idiyya), making the definition refer to one premise, not a composition. Adding 'mu'allaf' clarifies that it is a composite whole.
3
Appreciate the solution
By adding 'mu'allaf', the definition becomes 'a composed statement from premises', where 'mu'allaf' acts like a differentia, distinguishing qiyas from a single proposition. The totality is emphasized.
4
Reflect on the nature of such refinements
This example shows how critical refinements in definitions serve to eliminate ambiguity and ensure precision in logical terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطار
كِلَا التَّقديريْنِ يُراد بالقولِ الأَوَّلِ : الآخَرُ المعقولُ ؛ لعدم لزوم التلفظ بالقول الملفوظ لشيءٍ؛ لَا مِنَ القولِ الملفوظ وَلَا مِنَ القولِ المعقول، بل إنَّما يلزمُ القول المعقول من القولِ المعقول بلا واسطةٍ وَمِنَ القولِ الملفوظ بواسطةٍ ؛ دلالته على المعقول بالنَّظر إلى العالم بالوضع، لأنَّ التَّلفظَ يَستلزم تعقُل المعنى بالنسبة للعالم بالوضع، وتَعقُلُ المعنى على تقدير تسليمه يستلزم النتيجةَ ، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّ المرادَ بِاللُّزوم هَهُنا هو اللُّزوم بحسب نفس الأمر لا اللُّزوم العلمي، والقولُ الملفوظ المؤلَّفَ مِنَ القضايا الملفوظة؛ لَا يَستلزم مدلولَهُ بحسب نفس الأمرِ حتّى يستلزم القولَ الآخَرَ بحسب نفس الأمرِ بواسطة، بل إنَّما يَستلزمه بحسب العلم لدلالته عليه، وأيضاً القولُ الملفوظ يستلزم مدلوله بحسب العلم التّصوُّري، ومَدلُولُهُ إِنَّما يستلزم القول الآخَرَ باعتبار العلم التّصديقي، وأيضاً قد يؤدى المعقولُ بألفاظ مجازيَّةٍ، والمجاز ليس موضوعاً، إلا أنْ يُرادَ بِالوضع : مَا يشملُ النَّوعيَّ والتَّخصُّصي، والأَوْلَى أن يجعل التعريف للقياس العقليّ ؛ وإنْ كانَ المتبادرُ مِن عبارةِ الشَّارحِ كَالمصنفِ في شرح الرسالة والقطبِ الرَّازي أنَّ المراد العقلي أو اللفظي على البدل في المعرَّفِ والمعرفِ وفي بعض الحواشي، وأمَّا احتمالُ المركَبِ مِنَ الملفوظ والمعقول؛ فَلَمْ يقع في كلامِهِم التّصريح باعتبارِهِ ، فإن صح اعتبارُهُ فنقولُ : إِنَّ عبارةَ المصنِّف تتنزَّلُ عليه ا . هـ. وهذا كلام ليسَ لهُ اعتبار عندَ ذَوي الأنظار .

العطّار
(قَوْلُهُ : مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا يُخْرِجُ ) أي : بمجموع مؤلَّفَ مِن قضايا ؛ لِكَونِهِ بمنزلة الفصل، وفي شرح المطالع : أنَّ لفظ (مؤلف) مُستدرَكٌ ؛ لأنَّ القول هو المركَّب، وأجابَ السَّيِّدُ في شرح المواقفِ : بأنَّ ذِكْرَ المؤلَّفِ لِئَلَّا يُتوهم أَنَّ المراد قولٌ مِن جملة القضايا بجعل قولِهِ : مِنَ القضايا بمنزلة فردٍ مِنَ الأفراد، فتكونُ (مِنْ)

Critique of the Phrase 'كلتاهما' in the Fourth Figure
Al-Dusuqi identifies an error in the matn regarding the productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid moods in the fourth figure by verifying the conditions of universality and affirmation.

Core Idea
A single phrase can introduce an invalid syllogistic mood, showing the precision required in logical analysis.

In this passage, al-Dusuqi corrects a mistake in the matn regarding the fourth figure's productive moods. The phrase 'كلتاهما' (both of them) suggests an invalid combination of premises. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate syllogistic validity, a skill essential in kalam and fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the criticized phrase
Find 'كلتاهما' in the matn context. It refers to two particular premises: a particular negative minor and a particular affirmative major.
The matn mistakenly considered this combination productive.
2
State the conditions of the fourth figure
The fourth figure requires (1) at least one universal premise and (2) both premises affirmative. These conditions are derived from the figure's structure.
Violating either condition invalidates the mood.
3
Test the combination against conditions
The phrase implies a particular negative minor (O) and a particular affirmative major (I). This lacks a universal premise and has a negative premise, violating both conditions.
Hence, it is a glaring error (غلط فاحش).
4
Analyze al-Dusuqi's reasoning
He argues that the combination is not considered valid because it fails the conditions, and if accepted, it would increase productive moods to nine instead of eight.
This demonstrates the importance of precise enumeration.
5
Review al-'Attar's illustrative examples
Al-'Attar provides examples of non-productive combinations: both premises negative, both affirmative with particular minor, or differing in quality with both particular.
These clarify which combinations are truly invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قوله: «كلتاهما غلط فاحش ; لأنَّ الصُّغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية غيرُ مُعتَبَرٍ ; لاختلافِ مُقدِّمتيه مع عدم كلِّيَّةِ إحداهما ، فَلَا يوجدُ فيهِ ما اشتُرِطَ في هذا الشكل من إيجاب المقدمتين . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَر) أي : معتبر ،الإنتاج، وأيضاً يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ تكونَ الضُّروبُ المنتجة تسعة مع أنَّها ثمانية . العطار شيءٌ مِنَ الإنسان بفرس، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحمار أو الصَّاهل بإنسان، وأمَّا الثَّاني فَكَقَولِنَا : بعضُ الحيوان إنسان، وكُلُّ ناطق أو كُلُّ فرسٍ حيوان، وأَمَّا الثَّالث فَكَقَولِنَا في إيجاب الصُّغرى : بعضُ النَّاطِقِ إنسان وبعضُ الحيوان أو بعضُ الفرس ليس بناطق، وفي إيجابِ الكُبرى : بعضُ الإنسان ليس بفرس وبعضُ الحيوان أو بعضُ النَّاطِقِ إنسان، وإنّما لم يذكر شرط الشَّكلِ الرَّابع بحسب الجهة، وهو أمورٌ خمسةٌ؛ لِخَفائها وطول الكلام عليها .
And in his saying: 'both of them' is a glaring error. Because the particular negative minor with the particular affirmative major is not considered valid, due to the difference of their premises without universality of either, so it does not have what is required in this figure of the two premises being affirmative... al-Dusuqi: His saying: 'not considered' means not considered for production, and also it would necessitate that the productive moods be nine while they are eight. al-'Attar: Something like 'No man is a horse, and no donkey or neighing thing is a man'; as for the second, like our saying: 'Some animal is a man, and every speaker or every horse is an animal'; and as for the third, like our saying in the affirmative minor: 'Some speaker is a man, and some animal or some horse is not a speaker'; and in the affirmative major: 'Some man is not a horse, and some animal or some speaker is a man'. And he only did not mention the condition of the fourth figure regarding modality, which is five things, due to their subtlety and length of discussion.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This is a critical refinement. Al-Dusuqi identifies that the matn's inclusion of the two negative minors with a particular affirmative major as productive is a clear mistake because it violates the condition that at least one premise must be universal. He also notes that if it were considered valid, it would add an extra mood, making nine instead of eight. Al-'Attar then explains with examples to illustrate the non-productive combinations (both premises negative, both affirmative with particular minor, differing in quality but both particular). He also remarks that the fourth figure's conditions regarding modality are not mentioned because they are hidden and lengthy.

Key Terms
غلط فاحش (ghalat fahish): A glaring error, indicating a clear mistake in the logical analysis.
غير معتبر (ghayr mu'tabar): Not considered valid for producing a conclusion in the syllogism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Dusuqi consider the phrase 'كلتاهما' a glaring error?
A) It is grammatically incorrect
B) It leads to an invalid mood because no premise is universal
C) It contradicts the first condition
D) It makes nine moods

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure requires at least one universal premise and both premises affirmative; the phrase 'كلتاهما' violates this, making it invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid fourth figure syllogism using an Islamic legal example (e.g., from fiqh analogical reasoning) and verify its mood against the conditions of universality and affirmation.
?
Using the invalid combination 'some A is B, some B is not C' (particular minor O, particular major I) as in the passage, explain why it fails to produce a valid conclusion in the fourth figure, and then contrast it with a valid mood like EIO (Fresison) from kalam.

Types of Coextension and the Critical Refinement by al-Sharnubi
Al-Sharnubi corrects the misinterpretation of dawaran types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of coextension (dawaran) and identify which type is valid for causal inference in Islamic legal theory.

Core Idea
Only full coextension in existence and non-existence establishes a causal link.

Al-Sharnubi's hashiah corrects a critical misunderstanding about the types of coextension. He clarifies that the causal method requires a specific type of dawaran where the ruling's existence and non-existence both depend on the attribute, not just one direction. This distinction is essential for correctly applying the method of coextension in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of coextension
From al-Sharnubi's text, list the three types: full (existence and non-existence), partial (non-existence only), and the third (clear from example, perhaps existence only or other). Understand their definitions.
The first type is the one that necessitates both presence and absence of the ruling. The second only absence. The third is straightforward.
2
Recognize the general vs. specific meaning
Al-Sharnubi notes that a previous commentator (al-Muhashshi) used a general definition of dawaran from Adab al-Bahth. This general sense includes cases where coextension is not strictly causal. You must see why that is inappropriate here.
3
Locate the author's restriction
The original author (likely Yus al-Mawardi?) restricted the meaning to the specific causal coextension. Al-Sharnubi endorses this. Find the phrase 'الخاص وهو الذي يلزم من وجوده، ومن عدمها عدمه' and confirm it matches the first type.
This is the type that is valid for establishing 'illah via dawaran.
4
Apply the correct type to a fiqhi example
Use the example of ritual purity for prayer from the second type. Why does it not satisfy the full coextension? Because purity exists but prayer may not (other conditions missing). So it is only coextensive in non-existence.
This helps you differentiate between full and partial coextension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوده ولا يلزم من عدمها عدمه لحصوله بغيرها كالبيع، وقوله : والثاني وهو الذي يدور مع علته عدماً فقط كالطهارة للصلاة فإنه يلزم من عدمها عدم الصلاة ولا يلزم من وجودها وجود الصلاة لفقد شرط آخر كستر العورة، وقوله : والثَّالث ظاهر كمثال الشارح. واعلم أن ما نقله المحشّي عن يس مأخوذ من رسالة آداب البحث على أنَّه بالمعنى الأعم ولا يصح إرادته هنا ولذا قيده المصنف في شرح الرسالة في هذا المقام بالخاص وهو الذي يلزم من وجوده، ومن عدمها عدمه ويرشد لذلك مثال الشارح فتعين أن تكون الواو على وجود بابها كما يعلم بمراجعة ابن سعيد. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
...its existence and its non-existence does not necessitate its non-existence because it can occur through something else, like sale; and his statement: 'the second type is that which coextends with its cause only in non-existence, like ritual purity for prayer, for its absence necessitates the absence of prayer, but its presence does not necessitate the presence of prayer due to another condition being lost, such as covering the private parts'; and his statement: 'the third type is clear, like the author's example.' Know that what the commentator (al-Muhashshi) transmitted from Yas is taken from the treatise Adab al-Bahth, and it is in the general sense; it is not correct to intend it here. Therefore the author in his commentary on the treatise in this context restricted it to the specific meaning, which is that from whose existence and non-existence the ruling's existence and non-existence are necessitated. The author's example indicates this, so the waw must be in its proper place as can be known by consulting Ibn Sa'id. End of al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 866

Commentary
This hashiah by al-Sharnubi corrects a misunderstanding about the types of coextension (dawaran). He distinguishes three types: the first where the cause and effect are coextensive in existence and non-existence (the one used here); the second where coextension is only in non-existence (e.g., ritual purity and prayer: without purity there is no prayer, but purity alone does not guarantee prayer because other conditions like covering the private parts are needed); the third is obvious as per the author's example. Al-Sharnubi criticizes a previous commentator (al-Muhashshi) for citing a definition from Risalah Adab al-Bahth that uses coextension in a general sense. The author (of the main text) had restricted it to the specific sense (causal coextension in both directions). This clarification ensures the correct application of the method.

Key Terms
دوران
الخاص
العام
علة
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sharnubi, which type of coextension is correctly used to establish a causal link ('illah) in the context of the method of dawaran?
A) The type that coextends only in non-existence
B) The type that coextends only in existence
C) The type that coextends in both existence and non-existence
D) The general type as defined in Adab al-Bahth

Key Takeaway
Coextension (dawaran) is a valid method for discovering 'illah only when it is full, i.e., the attribute's presence and absence coincide with the ruling's presence and absence respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Using the second type of coextension (ritual purity for prayer), construct a similar example from another area of fiqh where the attribute coextends only in non-existence. Explain why it fails to prove causation.
?
Task: Critique the following reasoning: 'The presence of economic activity (tijara) coextends with the obligation of Zakat; therefore, economic activity is the cause of Zakat.' Determine which type of coextension is being used and whether it is sufficient for causal proof.

Al-Dasuqi's Analysis of the Conditional Syllogism
Al-Dasuqi analyzes a conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism in al-Dasuqi's hashia and verify its validity using modus tollens.

Core Idea
Denying the consequent disproves the antecedent.

Al-Dasuqi explicates the logical structure behind the commentator's claim that predicates of problems are not parts of subjects. He shows it is a conditional syllogism where the falsehood of the consequent forces the rejection of the antecedent, thereby proving the desired conclusion. Understanding this structure sharpens your ability to analyze arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall claim being proven
The claim is that predicates of problems are not parts of their subjects but external matters. This is the desired conclusion of the argument.
2
Locate the conditional statement
The conditional is: 'If the predicates were parts of their subjects, they would not be proven in the art.' This is the conjunctive implication (ملازمة).
3
Find the exception denying the consequent
The exception is: 'But the consequent is false because problems are proven in the art.' This is denying the consequent (استثناء نقيض التالي).
4
Determine the conclusion
By modus tollens, the antecedent is false, so the predicates are not parts. This establishes the claim.
5
Assess why 'otherwise...' is superfluous
Al-Dasuqi notes that the conclusion is already complete without the additional phrase 'otherwise...' (وإلا...), making it redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح: إذ لو كانت ... إلخ) قياس استثنائي مركب من ملازمة ومن استثناء نقيض التالي فأنتج نقيض المقدم هكذا : لو كانت محمولات المسائل أجزاء لموضوعاتها لم يبرهن عليها في الفنّ لكن التالي باطل فبطل المقدم فثبت نقيضه وهو أن محمولات المسائل لیست أجزاء موضوعاتها بل أمور خارجة عنها وهو المطلوب ودليل الملازمة أن جزء الشيء لا يحتاج في إثبات له إلى برهان ودليل بطلان التالي أن مسائل الفنّ مطالب خبرية يبرهن عليها في الفنّ هذا إيضاح كلامه وقوله وإلا ... إلخ مستدرك ؛ لأن النتيجة تمت. ا.هـ.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْعَوَارِضُ الذَّاتِيَّةُ الحاصلُ : أنَّ العارض إمَّا ذاتي، وإمَّا غريب، فالذَّاتي : ما يكون لحوقُهُ لِلمعروض لذاتِهِ بأن كانَ لحوقُهُ بِهِ بِلا واسطةٍ أصلاً كَلحوقِ التَّعجُبِ لِلإنسان، أو بواسطة جزئِهِ كَالحركة الإراديَّةِ اللاحقة للإنسانِ بواسطة أنَّهُ حيوان، أو بواسطة أمرٍ خارج عَنِ المعروض، لكن تلك الواسطة مساويةٌ لِلمعروضِ كَلحوقِ الضَّحكِ لِلإنسان بواسطةِ التَّعجُبِ، والتَّعجبُ مُساوي الضّحكِ للإنسان . والغريب : ما يكون لحوقه للمعروض بواسطةٍ أخص منه كَلحوقِ الضّحكِ للحيوان بسبب كونه إنساناً، وهو أخصُّ أو أعمُ كَلحوقِ التَّحرُّكِ لِلإنسان بواسطة كونِهِ حيواناً ، أو مُباينه كَلحوقِ الحرارةِ لِلماءِ بواسطةِ النَّارِ، وبينها وبينَ الماءِ تباين؛ كذا قالُوا فيما تقدَّمَ.
(قَوْلُهُ : مَا لَا يَكُوْنُ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ الْمَعْرُوْضَاتِ وَاسِطَةٌ) أي : لأَنَّها مَا تلحقُ الشَّيءَ لذاتِهِ حقيقةً أو حكماً كَاللاحق له بواسطةٍ مُساوية له.
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح : فربَّما ... إلخ) ذكر ربما هنا ينافي ما سبق له من أن مسائل الفنّ لا تكون إلا نظرية فالصواب حذفها على أن المسألة خلافية كما يعلم بمراجعة العطارا. هـ.
(The commentator's statement: 'for if they were ... etc.') is a compound conditional syllogism consisting of a conjunctive implication and an exception of the contradictory of the consequent, producing the contradictory of the antecedent as follows: If the predicates of the problems were parts of their subjects, they would not be proven in the art; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false, thus its contradictory is established: that the predicates of the problems are not parts of their subjects but rather external matters, which is the desired conclusion. The evidence for the implication is that a part of a thing neither needs proof for its establishment. The evidence for the falsity of the consequent is that the problems of the art are declarative demands that are proven in the art. This is a clarification of his statement. And his statement 'otherwise ... etc.' is superfluous because the conclusion is already complete. End.
(His statement: 'The essential accidents...') The summary: The accident is either essential or non-essential. The essential is that whose attachment to the object is by its essence, either without any intermediary, like the attachment of wonder to man, or through its part, like voluntary movement attaching to man through being animal, or through a matter external to the object but equal to it, like laughter attaching to man through wonder, and wonder is equal to man. The non-essential is that whose attachment to the object is through something more specific (like laughter attaching to animal through being human, which is more specific) or more general (like movement attaching to man through being animal) or something different (like heat attaching to water through fire, with mutual difference). This is as they said previously.
(His statement: 'There is no intermediary between them and the objects') means that they attach to the thing by its essence in reality or by judgment, like something that attaches through an equal intermediary.
(The commentator's statement: 'but perhaps...') The mention of 'perhaps' here contradicts what he previously stated that the problems of the art are only theoretical; so it is correct to delete it, though the matter is disputed as can be known by reviewing al-‘Attar. End.
Source pages p. 905

Commentary
This lesson presents four glosses (hashiya) from commentators on the main text. The first, by al-Dasuqi, analyzes the logical structure of the initial argument: it is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) that proves the predicates are not parts of subjects by modus tollens. He points out that the commentator's phrase 'otherwise...' is superfluous. The second hashia, also by al-Dasuqi, summarizes the types of accidents: essential (dhati) and non-essential (gharib). Essential accidents have three subtypes: by itself without medium, by a part, or by an external equal. Non-essential accidents attach through something more specific, more general, or different. The third hashia, by al-'Attar, clarifies the phrase 'no intermediary' by distinguishing between actual attachment (by essence in reality) and judgmental attachment (through an equal). The fourth, by al-Sharnubi, critiques the use of 'perhaps' (rubba) in the answer, arguing it contradicts the earlier claim that problems are theoretical and thus should be omitted. These glosses refine the logical precision of the original commentary.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
عارض غريب
ربما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical form of the argument analyzed by al-Dasuqi?
A) A categorical syllogism with Barbara mood
B) A conditional syllogism with modus tollens
C) A disjunctive syllogism
D) An immediate inference by conversion

Key Takeaway
Al-Dasuqi shows that denying the consequent in a conditional argument proves the denial of the antecedent, a key logical tool in Islamic scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a modus tollens argument on the issue of whether a particular action is wajib or not, using a conditional statement and the denial of its consequent.

Classification of Essential and Non-essential Accidents
Classification of essential and non-essential accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given accident as essential (dhati) or non-essential (gharib) by identifying the type of intermediary.

Core Idea
Essential accidents attach by essence; non-essential accidents attach via an intermediary that is more specific, more general, or different.

Al-Dasuqi summarizes the categories of accidents: essential (dhati) which attach without medium, via a part, or via an external equal; and non-essential (gharib) which attach via a more specific, more general, or different medium. This classification is crucial for understanding logical definitions in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential from non-essential
Essential accidents (dhati) attach by the essence of the subject; non-essential (gharib) attach through an intermediary. The key is whether the accident is intrinsic or comes from an external cause.
2
Identify the three subtypes of essential accidents
Essential: (1) without any intermediary (e.g., wonder attaching to man), (2) through a part of the essence (e.g., voluntary movement through being animal), (3) through an external but equal intermediary (e.g., laughter through wonder, which is equal to man).
3
Identify the three subtypes of non-essential accidents
Non-essential: (1) through something more specific (e.g., laughter attaching to animal through being human), (2) through something more general (e.g., movement attaching to man through being animal), (3) through something different (e.g., heat attaching to water through fire).
4
Apply classification to new examples
Given an example, determine the category by checking the relation between the accident and the subject, and the nature of the intermediary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح: إذ لو كانت ... إلخ) قياس استثنائي مركب من ملازمة ومن استثناء نقيض التالي فأنتج نقيض المقدم هكذا : لو كانت محمولات المسائل أجزاء لموضوعاتها لم يبرهن عليها في الفنّ لكن التالي باطل فبطل المقدم فثبت نقيضه وهو أن محمولات المسائل لیست أجزاء موضوعاتها بل أمور خارجة عنها وهو المطلوب ودليل الملازمة أن جزء الشيء لا يحتاج في إثبات له إلى برهان ودليل بطلان التالي أن مسائل الفنّ مطالب خبرية يبرهن عليها في الفنّ هذا إيضاح كلامه وقوله وإلا ... إلخ مستدرك ؛ لأن النتيجة تمت. ا.هـ.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْعَوَارِضُ الذَّاتِيَّةُ الحاصلُ : أنَّ العارض إمَّا ذاتي، وإمَّا غريب، فالذَّاتي : ما يكون لحوقُهُ لِلمعروض لذاتِهِ بأن كانَ لحوقُهُ بِهِ بِلا واسطةٍ أصلاً كَلحوقِ التَّعجُبِ لِلإنسان، أو بواسطة جزئِهِ كَالحركة الإراديَّةِ اللاحقة للإنسانِ بواسطة أنَّهُ حيوان، أو بواسطة أمرٍ خارج عَنِ المعروض، لكن تلك الواسطة مساويةٌ لِلمعروضِ كَلحوقِ الضَّحكِ لِلإنسان بواسطةِ التَّعجُبِ، والتَّعجبُ مُساوي الضّحكِ للإنسان . والغريب : ما يكون لحوقه للمعروض بواسطةٍ أخص منه كَلحوقِ الضّحكِ للحيوان بسبب كونه إنساناً، وهو أخصُّ أو أعمُ كَلحوقِ التَّحرُّكِ لِلإنسان بواسطة كونِهِ حيواناً ، أو مُباينه كَلحوقِ الحرارةِ لِلماءِ بواسطةِ النَّارِ، وبينها وبينَ الماءِ تباين؛ كذا قالُوا فيما تقدَّمَ.
(قَوْلُهُ : مَا لَا يَكُوْنُ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ الْمَعْرُوْضَاتِ وَاسِطَةٌ) أي : لأَنَّها مَا تلحقُ الشَّيءَ لذاتِهِ حقيقةً أو حكماً كَاللاحق له بواسطةٍ مُساوية له.
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح : فربَّما ... إلخ) ذكر ربما هنا ينافي ما سبق له من أن مسائل الفنّ لا تكون إلا نظرية فالصواب حذفها على أن المسألة خلافية كما يعلم بمراجعة العطارا. هـ.
(The commentator's statement: 'for if they were ... etc.') is a compound conditional syllogism consisting of a conjunctive implication and an exception of the contradictory of the consequent, producing the contradictory of the antecedent as follows: If the predicates of the problems were parts of their subjects, they would not be proven in the art; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false, thus its contradictory is established: that the predicates of the problems are not parts of their subjects but rather external matters, which is the desired conclusion. Al-Sharnubi's Critique of 'Rubba' (Perhaps)
Al-Sharnubi critiques the use of 'rubba'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate al-Sharnubi's objection to the use of 'rubba' and determine whether his recommendation to delete it is justified.

Core Idea
Using 'perhaps' contradicts the assertion that problems are theoretical; consistency demands deletion.

Al-Sharnubi notes that the commentator earlier stated that the problems of the art are only theoretical (nazariyya), so using 'rubba' (perhaps) introduces doubt and should be removed. However, al-'Attar notes that the matter is disputed. This hashia teaches the importance of logical consistency in scholarly writing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory statements
The commentator earlier said problems are only theoretical (nazariyya); later he uses 'rubba' (perhaps) in the answer, implying uncertainty.
2
Understand al-Sharnubi's reasoning
Al-Sharnubi argues that 'rubba' contradicts the earlier definitive statement, so it should be deleted to maintain consistency.
3
Consider the disputed nature
Al-'Attar points out that the matter is disputed; not all scholars agree that problems are exclusively theoretical, so 'rubba' may be acceptable.
4
Evaluate whether deletion is justified
If the earlier claim is taken as definitive, deletion is justified. If the issue is debated, 'rubba' may be a prudent qualification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح: إذ لو كانت ... إلخ) قياس استثنائي مركب من ملازمة ومن استثناء نقيض التالي فأنتج نقيض المقدم هكذا : لو كانت محمولات المسائل أجزاء لموضوعاتها لم يبرهن عليها في الفنّ لكن التالي باطل فبطل المقدم فثبت نقيضه وهو أن محمولات المسائل لیست أجزاء موضوعاتها بل أمور خارجة عنها وهو المطلوب ودليل الملازمة أن جزء الشيء لا يحتاج في إثبات له إلى برهان ودليل بطلان التالي أن مسائل الفنّ مطالب خبرية يبرهن عليها في الفنّ هذا إيضاح كلامه وقوله وإلا ... إلخ مستدرك ؛ لأن النتيجة تمت. ا.هـ.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْعَوَارِضُ الذَّاتِيَّةُ الحاصلُ : أنَّ العارض إمَّا ذاتي، وإمَّا غريب، فالذَّاتي : ما يكون لحوقُهُ لِلمعروض لذاتِهِ بأن كانَ لحوقُهُ بِهِ بِلا واسطةٍ أصلاً كَلحوقِ التَّعجُبِ لِلإنسان، أو بواسطة جزئِهِ كَالحركة الإراديَّةِ اللاحقة للإنسانِ بواسطة أنَّهُ حيوان، أو بواسطة أمرٍ خارج عَنِ المعروض، لكن تلك الواسطة مساويةٌ لِلمعروضِ كَلحوقِ الضَّحكِ لِلإنسان بواسطةِ التَّعجُبِ، والتَّعجبُ مُساوي الضّحكِ للإنسان . والغريب : ما يكون لحوقه للمعروض بواسطةٍ أخص منه كَلحوقِ الضّحكِ للحيوان بسبب كونه إنساناً، وهو أخصُّ أو أعمُ كَلحوقِ التَّحرُّكِ لِلإنسان بواسطة كونِهِ حيواناً ، أو مُباينه كَلحوقِ الحرارةِ لِلماءِ بواسطةِ النَّارِ، وبينها وبينَ الماءِ تباين؛ كذا قالُوا فيما تقدَّمَ.
(قَوْلُهُ : مَا لَا يَكُوْنُ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ الْمَعْرُوْضَاتِ وَاسِطَةٌ) أي : لأَنَّها مَا تلحقُ الشَّيءَ لذاتِهِ حقيقةً أو حكماً كَاللاحق له بواسطةٍ مُساوية له.
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح : فربَّما ... إلخ) ذكر ربما هنا ينافي ما سبق له من أن مسائل الفنّ لا تكون إلا نظرية فالصواب حذفها على أن المسألة خلافية كما يعلم بمراجعة العطارا. هـ.
(The commentator's statement: 'for if they were ... etc.') is a compound conditional syllogism consisting of a conjunctive implication and an exception of the contradictory of the consequent, producing the contradictory of the antecedent as follows: If the predicates of the problems were parts of their subjects, they would not be proven in the art; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false, thus its contradictory is established: that the predicates of the problems are not parts of their subjects but rather external matters, which is the desired conclusion. The evidence for the implication is that a part of a thing neither needs proof for its establishment. The evidence for the falsity of the consequent is that the problems of the art are declarative demands that are proven in the art. This is a clarification of his statement. And his statement 'otherwise ... etc.' is superfluous because the conclusion is already complete. End.
(His statement: 'The essential accidents...') The summary: The accident is either essential or non-essential. The essential is that whose attachment to the object is by its essence, either without any intermediary, like the attachment of wonder to man, or through its part, like voluntary movement attaching to man through being animal, or through a matter external to the object but equal to it, like laughter attaching to man through wonder, and wonder is equal to man. The non-essential is that whose attachment to the object is through something more specific (like laughter attaching to animal through being human, which is more specific) or more general (like movement attaching to man through being animal) or something different (like heat attaching to water through fire, with mutual difference). This is as they said previously.
(His statement: 'There is no intermediary between them and the objects') means that they attach to the thing by its essence in reality or by judgment, like something that attaches through an equal intermediary.
(The commentator's statement: 'but perhaps...') The mention of 'perhaps' here contradicts what he previously stated that the problems of the art are only theoretical; so it is correct to delete it, though the matter is disputed as can be known by reviewing al-‘Attar. End.
Source pages p. 905

Commentary
This lesson presents four glosses (hashiya) from commentators on the main text. The first, by al-Dasuqi, analyzes the logical structure of the initial argument: it is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) that proves the predicates are not parts of subjects by modus tollens. He points out that the commentator's phrase 'otherwise...' is superfluous. The second hashia, also by al-Dasuqi, summarizes the types of accidents: essential (dhati) and non-essential (gharib). Essential accidents have three subtypes: by itself without medium, by a part, or by an external equal. Non-essential accidents attach through something more specific, more general, or different. The third hashia, by al-'Attar, clarifies the phrase 'no intermediary' by distinguishing between actual attachment (by essence in reality) and judgmental attachment (through an equal). The fourth, by al-Sharnubi, critiques the use of 'perhaps' (rubba) in the answer, arguing it contradicts the earlier claim that problems are theoretical and thus should be omitted. These glosses refine the logical precision of the original commentary.

Key Terms
عارض غريب
ربما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-Sharnubi's critique underscores the need for consistency in scholarly discourse; even a single word can undermine an argument's coherence.

Distinguishing Mabadi and Muqaddimat in Islamic Sciences
Author distinguishes two senses of 'mabadi' and defines 'muqaddimat' with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mabadi (principles) and muqaddimat (preliminaries) as they are used in the organization of Islamic sciences, and explain why stating them at the outset prevents error.

Core Idea
Mabadi are what you start with; muqaddimat are what you need for expertise.

This lesson clarifies two key terms that scholars use when they introduce a science: mabadi (principles) and muqaddimat (preliminaries). Understanding their precise meanings will help you navigate any Islamic science text and appreciate why proper organization is critical for avoiding error, as demonstrated by mathematicians versus philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general sense of mabadi
The author states that 'mabadi' can be used for anything one begins with before reaching the intended object. This is a broad sense, including both internal and external matters.
Do not confuse with the technical 'principles of a science' which is a subset.
2
Define muqaddimat specifically
The passage specifies 'muqaddimat' as that upon which the commencement of a science depends 'in the manner of expertise' (بوجه الخبرة). This includes the science's definition, need/benefit, and subject.
Muqaddimat are a type of mabadi that provide insight and desire for learning.
3
List the three components of muqaddimat
From the text: 1) definition of the science, 2) explanation of the need for it (benefit and purpose), 3) its subject matter. These are examples, not necessarily exhaustive.
This pattern appears in many Islamic science texts.
4
Analyze the commentator's argument about principles
The commentator of Sullam al-Ulum emphasizes that stating both tasawwuri and tasdiqi principles at the start of a science (or chapter) prevents error. He contrasts mathematicians who do this (and avoid error) with physicists and philosophers who neglect it (and thus fall into confusion).
This underscores the practical benefit of clear organization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في أجزاء العلوم. (وَقَدْ تُقَالُ ؛ أي: كما تقالُ المبادي على ما ذَكَرَ، كذلك تُقال (الْمَبَادِي : لِمَا يُبْدَأُ بِهِ قَبْلَ الْمَقْصُودِ). (ق) تُقالُ (المُقَدِّمَاتُ أَيْضَاً : لِمَا يُتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ الشُّرُوعُ بِوَجْهِ الخِبْرَةِ)؛ أي : البصيرة وفَرْطِ الرَّغبة، كتعريف العِلم، وبيان الحاجة إليه؛ أي: بيان منفعته وغرضه، وموضوعه. و(قَدْ تُقَالُ الْمَبَادِي) قال شارح سلم العلوم: الأحسن والأليق بكل علم أن تذكر مباديه التصورية والتصديقية صدر العلم أو صدر كل باب ما يليق به؛ ليأمن المتعلم عن الغلط، وقد حافظ عليه أهل الهندسة والحساب وسائر الرياضيين، ولذلك لا يقع لهم غلط، ولم يحافظ عليه أهل الطبيعي والفلسفة، ولذلك يقع فيها خلط وخبط ا.هـ. بمعناه.
Chapter: On the parts of sciences. (And it is also said, i.e., just as 'al-mabadi' is used in the sense mentioned, similarly 'al-mabadi' is used for that with which one begins before the intended object. (Q) 'al-muqaddimat' is also used for that upon which the commencement [of the science] depends in the manner of expertise, i.e., insight and excess of desire, such as the definition of the science, and explaining the need for it, i.e., explaining its benefit and its purpose, and its subject. And (it is also said 'al-mabadi') the commentator of Sullam al-Ulum said: The best and most fitting for every science is that its tasawwuri and tasdiqi principles be mentioned at the beginning of the science or at the beginning of each chapter what is fitting for it, so that the learner is safe from error. The people of geometry, arithmetic, and the rest of the mathematicians have preserved this, and therefore no error occurs to them. The people of physics and philosophy did not preserve it, and therefore confusion and disorder occur in them. End quote, paraphrased.
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory commentary on the terms 'mabadi' (principles) and 'muqaddimat' (premises) as they relate to the parts of a science. The author begins by clarifying that the term 'mabadi' can be used generically for anything one starts with before the intended object, not only the technical 'principles' of a science. Then he specifies a second usage: 'al-muqaddimat' also refers to what is required to commence a science with true insight (بوجه الخبرة), meaning deep understanding and keen desire. Examples include defining the science, explaining the need for it (which includes its benefit and purpose), and stating its subject matter. The author then quotes the commentator of Sullam al-Ulum, who emphasizes the importance of stating the conceptual (tasawwuri) and assertoric (tasdiqi) principles at the outset of a science or each chapter, as done by mathematicians to avoid error, contrasting with the negligence of natural philosophers and metaphysicians which leads to confusion. This reinforces the methodical approach to presenting a science.

Key Takeaway
Muqaddimat are foundational preliminaries that equip a learner with insight, and clearly stating them prevents confusion, as shown by the success of mathematicians.

Interpreting the Commentator's Source Clarification
Attar clarifies the author's source reference and shares his own writing progress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret a commentator's clarification about source references in a logical text and recognize the implications for scholarly practice.

Core Idea
A commentator's clarification on sources reveals the broader textual web behind a single reference.

In this passage from his gloss on the author's statement, Attar addresses a potential misunderstanding. The author had said he worked only on the commentary of al-Risala. Attar clarifies that this refers to the act of explaining its content, not a limitation on sources. He also shares a personal note about his own progress in writing, mentioning Burhan al-Khalf and his travel to Alexandria.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author's Original Statement
Find the phrase 'على شرح الرسالة' (on the commentary of al-Risala). This is the statement Attar is clarifying.
The author likely meant that his main focus was on explaining that specific text, not that he ignored other works.
2
Understand Attar's Interpretation
Attar says 'المراد : ما يتعلق بشرح كلامها' – the intended meaning is 'that which pertains to explaining its words'. He narrows the scope to the act of commentary.
This shows that Attar reads the statement as a methodological focus, not a bibliographical exclusion.
3
Recognize the Implication for Other Sources
Attar explicitly says this does not conflict with the author citing al-Sayyid's Hashiya elsewhere. He points to his own prior notes ('كما نبهنا على ذلك هناك').
Commentators often cross‑reference to show consistency across their works.
4
Note the Personal Narrative
Attar adds that he had reached 'برهان الخلف' (proof by contradiction) in his writing, then traveled to Alexandria where he completed the work, and did not bring certain books.
This gives historical context – the commentary was finished under travel conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار (قوله : إلا على شرح الرسالة) المرادُ : ما يتعلَّق بشرح كلامِهَا، فَلَا يُنافي أَنَّهُ ذكر في بعض المباحث شيئاً مِنَ حاشيةِ السَّيِّدِ كَما نبهنا على ذلك هناك، وإلى هنا انتهى بِنَا الكلام، والحمدُ للهِ في المبدأ والختام، وقد كنتُ وصلَتْ بي الكتابة إلى برهان الخلف، ثمَّ توجَّهْتُ إلى الإسكندرية، وفيها وقع الإتمام، ولم أستصحب
Al-Attar: (His statement: 'except on the commentary of al-Risala') The intended meaning is that which pertains to explaining its words, so it does not conflict with the fact that he mentioned in some investigations something from the Hashiya of al-Sayyid, as we pointed out there. And here our speech ends, and praise be to Allah in the beginning and the conclusion. And I had reached in writing to the proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf), then I headed to Alexandria, and there the completion occurred, and I did not bring with me
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is Attar's super-commentary on the author's statement that he had access only to the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. Attar clarifies that the author's phrase 'على شرح الرسالة' (on the commentary of the epistle) refers to explaining its content, not to the physical book alone. Thus, it does not contradict the fact that the author in some places cites from the Hashiya of al-Sayyid (al-Sharif al-Jurjani). Attar then digresses to note that he himself had reached the section on Burhan al-Khalf (proof by contradiction) in his writing, traveled to Alexandria where he completed the work, and he did not bring with him certain books (the sentence is incomplete).

Ellipsis in Scholarly Apologies: The Case of Forgiveness for Forgetfulness
Dasuqi resolves an objection about seeking forgiveness for forgetfulness by positing an ellipsis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify elliptical constructions (hadhf) in scholarly Arabic and explain how they resolve apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
An author's apology often contains a hidden cause; ellipsis reveals the true object of forgiveness.

In this passage, Dasuqi tackles a subtle objection to the author's closing prayer: if forgetfulness is not a sin, why seek forgiveness? His answer hinges on an ellipsis—the missing 'cause of forgetfulness' (taqsir). This lesson trains you to spot such elliptical expressions, a critical skill when reading usul al-fiqh and tafsir, where implied words often carry the legal weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Read the author's phrase: 'And I seek forgiveness from Allah from my forgetfulness.' Note the objection: forgetfulness (sahw) is not a sin, so why ask forgiveness? This creates a logical problem.
2
Posit an ellipsis
Dasuqi suggests that the phrase is elliptical: 'from my forgetfulness' really means 'from the cause of my forgetfulness.' The missing word is sabab or mujib (cause).
In Arabic grammar, this is called hadhf al-mudaf (ellipsis of the genitive noun).
3
Verify with the commentator's statement
Dasuqi states: 'his statement: from my forgetfulness involves ellipsis of a genitive; i.e., from the cause of my forgetfulness, which is shortcoming.' This confirms the resolution.
4
Apply the same reasoning to other examples
Now consider a similar case: in the Quran, 'wa ashhidu dhaway adlin minkum' (bring two witnesses of justice from among you). Some scholars say 'adl' is a condition, implying an ellipsis of 'ma'a al-adl' (with justice). Could you spot such an ellipsis?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وأستغفر الله) جملةٌ ماضويّةٌ عطفٌ على ما قَبْلَها ؛ أي: واطلبُ مِنَ اللهِ أن يغفر لي من أجل سهوي. وفيه : أَنَّ السَّهو ليس ذنباً حتَّى يُطلب مغفرته، وأُجِيبُ : بأنَّ قولَه : مِن سَهوي على حذف مضاف ؛ أي: مِن مُسبّب سهوي، وهو التقصيرُ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . (قوله : ورهلي) أي : مَشْيِي؛ عطفٌ على البضاعة، ويحتمل أَنَّه معطوفٌ على قلة؛ أي: وَبِأَنِّي ماشي في ذلك الميدان، وَلَسْتُ بِفَارس في مضمار . (قوله : في مضمار) المضمارُ : الموضعُ تضمرُ فيهِ الخيل؛ أي: تركب ويتسابق فيه الفرسان . (قوله : ودهائي) الدَّهاءُ : - بفتحِ الدَّالِ المهملة ـ جودة الرأي، كما في القاموس، والمراد هنا : الرأي. قبله . (قوله : وذكائي) الذكاء : سرعة الفهم والمراد به هنا : الفهم، فهو مُرادِفٌ لِمَا قبله . (قوله : والله المستعان) أي: المستعان به على تحصيل كلِّ أمرٍ . (قوله : التكلان) أي : التَّوكُلُ والاعتمادُ في كلِّ شيء .
His statement: 'And I seek forgiveness from Allah' is a past-tense sentence, coordinated with what precedes it; i.e., I ask from Allah that He forgive me because of my forgetfulness. And in it: that forgetfulness is not a sin such that forgiveness is sought for it. And it is answered: that his statement: 'from my forgetfulness' involves ellipsis of a genitive; i.e., from the cause of my forgetfulness, which is shortcoming, so ponder. (His statement: 'and my foot') i.e., my walking; coordinated with 'paucity of stock', and it is possible that it is coordinated with 'paucity'; i.e., and that I am walking in that field and I am not a horseman in the racecourse. (His statement: 'in the racecourse') The racecourse is the place where horses are trained; i.e., they ride and compete in it. (His statement: 'and my intelligence') Intelligence is soundness of opinion, as in al-Qamus, and the intended meaning here is opinion. Before it. (His statement: 'and my acumen') Acumen is quickness of understanding, and the intended meaning here is understanding, so it is synonymous with what came before. (His statement: 'And Allah is the One sought for help') i.e., He is sought for help to accomplish every matter. (His statement: 'reliance') i.e., trust and dependence in everything.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is Dasuqi's super-commentary on the author's closing prayer. He addresses each phrase sequentially. First, on 'وأستغفر الله من سهوي', he notes that forgetfulness is not inherently a sin, yet the author asks forgiveness. He resolves this by suggesting that 'من سهوي' is elliptic: the real cause is the shortcoming (taqsir) that leads to forgetfulness. Next, he explains 'ورهلي' (my foot) as a metaphor for walking (mashyi), coordinated with 'paucity of stock'—the author is a pedestrian, not a horseman in logic. He also considers it possibly coordinated with 'paucity', emphasizing the author's lowly status in the field. He defines 'مضمار' as the training ground for horses. Then on 'دهائي', he gives its lexical meaning as soundness of opinion, equating it here with mere opinion (ra'y). Similarly, 'ذكائي' is quickness of understanding, synonymous with understanding (fahm). Finally, on the concluding formulas, he glosses 'المستعان' as the One sought for help in all affairs, and 'التكلان' as trust and dependence.

Key Terms
سهو
تقصير
حذف مضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because forgetfulness is a major sin
B) Because forgetfulness leads to error in logical proofs

D) Because the author is being overly humble

Key Takeaway
Dasuqi teaches that elliptic constructions are often the key to resolving apparent contradictions in scholarly texts.

Metaphorical Humility: Understanding the Scholar's Self-Deprecation
Dasuqi explains the metaphors of foot and racecourse in the author's closing apology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and interpret common metaphors for humility in Islamic scholarly texts (e.g., walking vs. riding, racecourse).

Core Idea
Scholars use metaphors of travel to express epistemic modesty; misreading them distorts the author's self-assessment.

After explaining the ellipsis, Dasuqi unpacks the author's metaphorical language: 'and my foot' and 'in the racecourse.' These are not literal but convey the author's humble status as a pedestrian in the field of logic, not a champion horseman. This lesson will help you decode such metaphors when you encounter them in scholarly introductions and conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphor
Look at the author's phrase 'and my foot' (wa-rijli). This is not a literal body part but a metaphor for 'my walking' (mashyi). Similarly, 'in the racecourse' (midmar) is a metaphor for the field of logic.
2
Determine the literal meaning
Rijli means 'my foot' and midmar means 'the place where horses are trained and race.' A horseman (faris) is a skilled rider.
3
Interpret the metaphor in context
Dasuqi says the author means: 'I am walking in that field, and I am not a horseman in the racecourse.' This indicates the author's low level of competence and humility.
The author is not claiming great skill; rather, he is apologizing for his modest contribution.
4
Recognize the rhetorical purpose
Such metaphors serve to show humility (tawadu') and ward off accusations of pride. In Islamic scholarly tradition, it is common to begin or end works with self-deprecation.
5
Apply to other texts
When you read the introduction of a classical commentary, look for similar metaphors: 'rajil', 'midmar', 'sabiq', etc. What do they tell you about the author's attitude?

قوله : وأستغفر الله) جملةٌ ماضويّةٌ عطفٌ على ما قَبْلَها ؛ أي: واطلبُ مِنَ اللهِ أن يغفر لي من أجل سهوي. وفيه : أَنَّ السَّهو ليس ذنباً حتَّى يُطلب مغفرته، وأُجِيبُ : بأنَّ قولَه : مِن سَهوي على حذف مضاف ؛ أي: مِن مُسبّب سهوي، وهو التقصيرُ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . (قوله : ورهلي) أي : مَشْيِي؛ عطفٌ على البضاعة، ويحتمل أَنَّه معطوفٌ على قلة؛ أي: وَبِأَنِّي ماشي في ذلك الميدان، وَلَسْتُ بِفَارس في مضمار . (قوله : في مضمار) المضمارُ : الموضعُ تضمرُ فيهِ الخيل؛ أي: تركب ويتسابق فيه الفرسان . (قوله : ودهائي) الدَّهاءُ : - بفتحِ الدَّالِ المهملة ـ جودة الرأي، كما في القاموس، والمراد هنا : الرأي. قبله . (قوله : وذكائي) الذكاء : سرعة الفهم والمراد به هنا : الفهم، فهو مُرادِفٌ لِمَا قبله . (قوله : والله المستعان) أي: المستعان به على تحصيل كلِّ أمرٍ . (قوله : التكلان) أي : التَّوكُلُ والاعتمادُ في كلِّ شيء .
His statement: 'And I seek forgiveness from Allah' is a past-tense sentence, coordinated with what precedes it; i.e., I ask from Allah that He forgive me because of my forgetfulness. And in it: that forgetfulness is not a sin such that forgiveness is sought for it. And it is answered: that his statement: 'from my forgetfulness' involves ellipsis of a genitive; i.e., from the cause of my forgetfulness, which is shortcoming, so ponder. (His statement: 'and my foot') i.e., my walking; coordinated with 'paucity of stock', and it is possible that it is coordinated with 'paucity'; i.e., and that I am walking in that field and I am not a horseman in the racecourse. (His statement: 'in the racecourse') The racecourse is the place where horses are trained; i.e., they ride and compete in it. (His statement: 'and my intelligence') Intelligence is soundness of opinion, as in al-Qamus, and the intended meaning here is opinion. Before it. (His statement: 'and my acumen') Acumen is quickness of understanding, and the intended meaning here is understanding, so it is synonymous with what came before. (His statement: 'And Allah is the One sought for help') i.e., He is sought for help to accomplish every matter. (His statement: 'reliance') i.e., trust and dependence in everything.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is Dasuqi's super-commentary on the author's closing prayer. He addresses each phrase sequentially. First, on 'وأستغفر الله من سهوي', he notes that forgetfulness is not inherently a sin, yet the author asks forgiveness. He resolves this by suggesting that 'من سهوي' is elliptic: the real cause is the shortcoming (taqsir) that leads to forgetfulness. Next, he explains 'ورهلي' (my foot) as a metaphor for walking (mashyi), coordinated with 'paucity of stock'—the author is a pedestrian, not a horseman in logic. He also considers it possibly coordinated with 'paucity', emphasizing the author's lowly status in the field. He defines 'مضمار' as the training ground for horses. Then on 'دهائي', he gives its lexical meaning as soundness of opinion, equating it here with mere opinion (ra'y). Similarly, 'ذكائي' is quickness of understanding, synonymous with understanding (fahm). Finally, on the concluding formulas, he glosses 'المستعان' as the One sought for help in all affairs, and 'التكلان' as trust and dependence.

Key Terms
رهلي
مضمار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'rajli' (my foot) metaphorically represent in the author's closing?
A) The author's physical foot
B) The author's humble walking and pedestrian status
C) The author's horse
D) The author's pen

Key Takeaway
Metaphors of travel, like 'foot' and 'racecourse', are standard scholarly tropes for humility; interpreting them correctly is essential for understanding the author's stance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the opening of Fatiha or another short surah and see if there are any metaphors of travel (e.g., 'sirat al-mustaqim' as a path). But better: 'In the introduction of Ibn Hajar's Fath al-Bari, he uses phrases like 'ana al-rajil' (I am the pedestrian). Read a snippet and explain how the humility trope functions.'
?
Write a short paragraph in the style of a classical scholar, using a metaphor of foot or racecourse to humbly introduce your own work on a topic from fiqh or usul.
